Divine Attributes
advanced
8 min
God's Knowledge is Identical to His Essence
Razi argues against additional attribute via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's reductio argument proving that God's knowledge cannot be an attribute additional to His essence, and why this preserves divine simplicity.

Core Idea
If God's knowledge were additional, His simple essence would become composite and contingent.

The Ash'ari position holds that God's attributes are not additional to His essence. In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a powerful logical argument against the view that knowledge is a separate entity. Using a reductio ad absurdum, he shows that if knowledge were additional, the essence would have to be both the subject and the cause of the attribute, contradicting divine simplicity and leading to composition and contingency. Follow the steps carefully to see how Razi drives the argument to its necessary conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the supposition
Razi begins with the supposition that knowledge is additional to the essence. Note how he defines 'additional' as an attribute subsisting in the essence.
This is the starting point of the reductio.
2
Trace the implications
Follow the logical chain: an attribute needs its subject, so it is contingent and needs a cause. The cause is the essence itself, making the essence both the described (passive) and the effector (active) of the attribute.
The key contradiction: the simple essence cannot be both recipient and agent.
3
See the regress and conclusion
The concepts of being recipient and agent are distinct. If external to the essence, they lead to multiplicity in the essence, hence composition and contingency. This contradicts God's necessary existence, so the supposition is false.
The argument ends with a contradiction, proving the original claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون علمه زائدا على ذاته، فلأنه لو كان زائدا على أنه صفة قائمة بتلك الذات وجب أن يكون ذلك العلم مفتقرا في تحققه إلى تلك الذات؛ لأن الصفة مفتقرة إلى الموصوف، والمفتقر إلى الغير ممكن لذاته، مفتقر إلى المؤثر، والمؤثر فيه ليس إلا تلك الذات فتكون تلك الذات موصوفة به، ومؤثرة فيه، مع أن تلك الذات بسيطة منزهة عن جميع التركيبات فيكون البسيط قابلا وفاعلا معا، وذلك محال لأن المفهوم من كونه قابلا غير المفهوم من كونه فاعلاً، وهذان المفهومان إن كانا خارجين عن الذات كان المفهوم من استلزام الذات لأحدهما غير المفهوم من استلزامها للآخر، فيعود التقسيم الأول فيه ولا يتسلسل، بل ينتهي إلى كثرة تقع في الذات، فتكون ذاته مركبة من الأجزاء، وكل ما كان كذلك كان ممكناً لذاته، فيكون الواجب لذاته ممكنا لذاته، وهذا خلف محال.
As for its being impermissible that His knowledge be additional to His essence, it is because if it were additional as an attribute subsisting in that essence, then that knowledge would need that essence for its realization; because the attribute needs the described, and what needs something else is possible in itself and needs an effecter, and the effecter is none but that essence. So that essence would be described by it and effecting it, while that essence is simple, exempt from all compositions. Then the simple would be both recipient and agent simultaneously, and that is impossible because the concept of being recipient is different from the concept of being agent. And if these two concepts are external to the essence, then the concept of the essence's entailment of one is different from the concept of its entailment of the other. Then the first division returns in it and does not regress infinitely, but ends in multiplicity occurring in the essence, so that its essence would be composed of parts, and whatever is like that is possible in itself. Then the Necessary of Existence in Himself would be possible in Himself, and that is an impossible contradiction.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument against the view that God's knowledge is an attribute additional to His essence. The argument proceeds: If knowledge were an additional attribute, it would need the essence for its existence (since attributes subsist in a subject). That which needs another is contingent (ممكن) and requires a cause. The cause of this attribute would be the essence itself. Thus the essence would be both the subject (موصوف) of the attribute and its agent (مؤثر). But God's essence is absolutely simple (بسيط) and free from composition. It cannot be both passive (قابل) and active (فاعل) simultaneously, because the two concepts are distinct. If these concepts are external to the essence, then the essence's relation to each would be different, leading to a multiplicity of relations and eventually to the essence being composed of parts. But a composite is contingent, which would make the Necessary Being contingent—a contradiction. Hence, knowledge cannot be additional; it must be identical with the essence.

Key Terms
زائد على الذات
صفة قائمة
مفتقر
مؤثر
بسيط
قابل
فاعل
ترکیب
واجب الوجود
ممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God has knowledge at all.
Correction: The argument only denies that knowledge is an additional attribute; it affirms that God's knowledge is identical to His essence, so He knows by His essence.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what would happen if God's knowledge were additional to His essence?
A) God would not know anything
B) The essence would be both subject and agent of the attribute, leading to composition and contingency
C) Knowledge would be eternal
D) The essence would be simple

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that God's knowledge cannot be additional to His essence because it would force divine simplicity into composition and contingency, which is impossible for the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi’s argument shows that if we think of God’s knowledge as a separate ‘thing’ added to His essence, we end up making Him composite and contingent. Do you ever unconsciously imagine God as having ‘parts’ (like a mind, will, power) that are somehow separate from who He is? How does this realization challenge you to purify your concept of tawḥīd in your daily worship?
?
The argument forces you to affirm that God’s knowledge is exactly His essence—nothing added, nothing subtracted. When you pray, do you truly believe that the One you are addressing is absolutely simple, with no divisions or parts? Or does your heart still imagine a God who is like a composite being? What will you do to train your mind to think of God as the simple Necessary Being?
?
If God’s knowledge were additional, He would be dependent upon it—and thus not self-sufficient. Do you live as though God is independent and self-sufficient, or do you sometimes act as if He needs something from you? How can you align your inner state with the fact that He is al-Ṣamad, the Self-Sufficient, whose attributes are not separate from His essence?

Second Objection Against the Addition of Knowledge and Its Response
Objection that if essence is perfect without knowledge, knowledge is redundant; if imperfect, essence needs another. Razi responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that God's essence is either perfect without knowledge (making it redundant) or imperfect without it (making God dependent), by arguing that perfection necessitates attributes.

Core Idea
Perfection does not exclude attributes; it necessitates them.

This lesson presents a classic dilemma about divine attributes: if God is perfect without knowledge, is knowledge superfluous? If He is not perfect without it, does that imply deficiency? Razi's response reframes the relationship between essence and attributes, showing that perfection itself entails the possession of perfect attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the objection carefully. The objector poses two alternatives: either God's essence is absolutely perfect without knowledge (making knowledge redundant) or it is not perfect without knowledge (making God deficient and dependent on another).
Notice the logical structure: it's an 'either-or' argument aiming to force a contradiction.
2
Identify the flaw in the second horn
Razi rejects the implication that perfection cannot coexist with attributes. He argues that the second alternative (essence deficient without knowledge) is falsely framed.
The objector assumes that if something is perfect, adding anything cannot increase perfection. Razi challenges this assumption.
3
Grasp Razi's positive formulation
Razi states that the perfection of the essence necessitates (يقتضي) that it entails knowledge. This means knowledge is not an external addition but a necessary implication of being perfect.
This avoids both horns: knowledge is not redundant (it flows from perfection) and the essence is not deficient (perfection requires it).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : أنهم قالوا : ذاته سبحانه وتعالى بدون هذا العلم إما أن تكون كاملة على الإطلاق، وحينئذ لا يكون في حصول العلم كمال وجلال، ويجب نفيه، وإما أن لا تكون الذات بدون العلم كاملة وحينئذ تكون الذات الواجبة الوجود ناقصة بذاتها وكاملة بغيرها، وهو محال. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن كون تلك الذات كاملة يقتضي كونها مستلزمة لحصول هذا العلم ولا نقول : الذات ناقصة بذاتها، مستكملة بغيرها بل نقول كونها كاملة يستلزم حصول صفات الكمال.
The second doubt: They said: His essence, glorified and exalted, without this knowledge is either absolutely perfect, and then the acquisition of knowledge brings no perfection and majesty, and thus it must be negated; or the essence without knowledge is not perfect, and then the essence necessary of existence would be deficient in itself and perfect through another, which is impossible. The answer to the second doubt: Why may it not be said that the perfection of that essence necessitates that it entails the acquisition of this knowledge? We do not say that the essence is deficient in itself and made perfect by another; rather, we say that its being perfect necessitates the acquisition of attributes of perfection.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents a specific objection (شبهة) against the view that God possesses knowledge as an additional attribute, followed by Razi's response. The objection poses a dilemma: either God's essence is absolutely perfect without knowledge, in which case adding knowledge would add no perfection and should be denied; or His essence is not perfect without knowledge, which would make the Necessary Being deficient by itself and perfected by something else—an impossibility. Razi's answer rejects the second horn of the dilemma. He asserts that the perfection of the essence does not mean it is deficient on its own and then perfected by knowledge. Rather, perfection itself implies (يقتضي) that the essence entails knowledge. In other words, a perfect being necessarily has perfect attributes; knowledge is an attribute of perfection that flows from the very fact that the essence is perfect. Thus, there is no deficiency, because the essence's perfection is the reason for the attribute, not something added from outside.

Key Terms
شبهة
كاملة على الإطلاق
ناقصة بذاتها
مستكملة بغيرها
يستلزم
صفات الكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Razi accepts the dilemma and chooses one of its two horns.
Correction: Razi does not accept the dilemma as presented. He rejects the second premise by arguing that perfection entails the attribute rather than being deficient without it.

Checkpoint
In the answer to the second doubt, how does Razi explain the relationship between God's essence and His knowledge?
A) The essence is deficient without knowledge
B) The essence is perfect and knowledge is an accident
C) The perfection of the essence necessitates that it entails knowledge
D) Knowledge is not a perfection

Key Takeaway
The perfection of God's essence necessitates, not contradicts, the possession of perfect attributes like knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat God's attributes—like His knowledge—as 'extras' you only think about in theology, or as the very fabric of His perfection that should shape how you live every moment? How does your daily life reflect the belief that His knowledge is not separate from His essence but flows from it?
?
When you struggle with doubt about God's attributes, do you realize that your confusion often stems from measuring God by human standards of perfection? Will you, after this lesson, actively repent of thinking that God's essence could be 'lacking' without certain attributes?
?
If God's perfection necessitates His knowledge, then your sin is committed in the presence of a Perfect Knower. How does this truth change the way you will seek forgiveness tonight—not as a mere ritual, but as an encounter with the One whose knowledge is not accidental to His being?

The Third Objection: Divine Attributes and Composition
Objection that if God's being Lord depends on the world, then the world's cause would be composite; Razi responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the third objection against the addition of knowledge to God's essence, and Razi's response that the essence necessarily entails its attributes, avoiding composition.

Core Idea
Attributes entailed by the essence do not make God composite or contingent.

Imam Razi continues to address objections against the addition of divine attributes. The third objection tries to argue that if God's being Lord depends on the world, then the cause would be composite and thus contingent. Razi's response clarifies that the essence is necessary and entails its attributes, so no composition arises.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objection is presented in a conditional form, and how Razi's response refutes it.
The objection uses the term 'مبدأ العالم' (principle of the world) to refer to God as cause.
2
Analyze the objection's logic
Identify the two horns of the dilemma: either God's lordship depends on the world or not. Understand why each leads to an undesirable conclusion according to the objector.
The objector claims that if it depends, the cause becomes composite (essence + knowledge) and thus contingent.
3
Understand Razi's response
Focus on Razi's key move: the essence is necessary in itself and entails its attributes. Ask why this refutes the claim of composition and contingency.
Razi uses the term 'مستلزمة' (entails) to show attributes are not separate additions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : كونه إلها للعالم إن لم يتوقف على إثبات هذا العالم لم يجز إثباته، وإن توقف عليه كان مبدأ العالم مركبًا من الذات والعلم، وكل مركب ممكن، فكان مبدأ كل الممكنات ممكنا وذلك محال. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : مبدأ العالم هو الذات الواجبة الوجود، الموصوفة بالعلم والقدرة، والذات هي الواجبة لذاتها، وبذاتها وهي مستلزمة لهذه الصفات، فلم قلتم إن ذلك محال ؟
The third doubt: His being God of the world—if it does not depend on the existence of this world, then its proof is not possible; and if it depends on it, then the principle of the world would be composed of essence and knowledge, and every composite is possible, so the principle of all possibles would be possible, and that is impossible. The answer to the third doubt: The principle of the world is the essence necessary of existence, described by knowledge and power, and the essence is necessary in itself and by itself, and it entails these attributes. So why do you say that that is impossible?
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection (شبهة) concerning the relationship between God's being the Lord (إله) of the world and the world's existence, followed by Razi's response. The objection argues that if God's being Lord does not depend on the world's existence, then we cannot prove it (because there would be no connection). If it does depend, then the cause of the world (مبدأ العالم) would be composed of essence and knowledge, and whatever is composite is contingent (ممكن), making the First Cause contingent—impossible. Razi's answer is straightforward: The cause of the world is the divine essence, which is necessary in itself (واجب لذاتها) and is described with attributes like knowledge and power. The essence necessarily entails (مستلزمة) these attributes; they are not external additions that would create composition. Therefore, the objection's assumption that composition implies contingency does not apply, because the attributes are not separate components but necessary concomitants of the simple essence. So the First Cause remains necessarily existent and simple.

Key Terms
إله للعالم
مبدأ العالم
مركب
ممكن
بذاتها
مستلزمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Razi concedes that the cause is composite, making it contingent.
Correction: Razi denies that attributes constitute composition because they are entailed by the essence, not added as separate components.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the third doubt, why is it not impossible for the First Cause to have attributes?
A) Because the attributes are separate substances
B) Because the essence is necessary and entails its attributes
C) Because the world does not depend on God
D) Because composition is not a problem

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that divine attributes are not additive components but necessary entailments of the simple, necessary essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi insists that God's existence is necessary in itself and by itself, and that His attributes are entailed, not added. When you speak of God's power or knowledge, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as separate 'parts' or 'qualities' added to His essence? How does this lesson call you to purify your concept of God's oneness in worship?
?
The objector assumes that dependency implies composition. Do you ever treat your own relationship to God as if your faith depends on external circumstances, making God's lordship 'composite' in your mind? Reflect on one area where you can affirm God's necessary, simple lordship without condition.
?
If God's attributes are necessary entailments of His essence, then He does not need the world to be described as 'Lord'. How does this truth challenge your tendency to define God's attributes by how they relate to you? What would it look like to praise Him for His attributes without reference to His creation?

Proof That God Knows All Things
Razi proves that God knows all things because He is living and His essence relates equally to all knowables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's rational proof that God's knowledge encompasses all knowable things, based on His life and the uniform causality of His essence.

Core Idea
God's essence relates equally to all knowables, so His knowledge must be universal.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a concise, self-contained rational argument for God's omniscience. He builds on the established attribute of life (al-hayah) and the nature of efficient causality to show that if God knows anything at all—which has already been proven—then He must know everything. The key move is that the divine essence's relationship to all objects of knowledge is identical, leaving no room for partiality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the compact, logical style of the proof.
The proof has five clear steps.
2
Identify the first premise: God is living
Razi begins by stating that God is living (حي). This is an established attribute from previous proofs. Understand that life is a necessary condition for knowledge.
3
Identify the second premise: the cause is the essence
The cause (موجب) of God's being knower is His own essence, not an external factor. This ensures that the explanation for knowledge is internal and uniform.
4
Recognize the uniform relation argument
The essence relates equally (على السوية) to all knowable things. Therefore there is no basis for it to cause knowledge of some things but not others.
This is the crucial step that eliminates partiality.
5
Follow the conclusion
Since God knows some things (as established elsewhere) and the essence is the cause, the same cause must produce knowledge of all things. Thus God knows all.
The argument is deductive, not empirical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في بيان أنه سبحانه وتعالى عالم بكل المعلومات برهانه أنه سبحانه وتعالى حي، وكل من كان حيا فإنه يصح منه أن يعلم كل واحد من المعلومات والموجب أيضًا لهذه العالمية هو ذاته، ونسبة الذات إلى الكل على السوية، فلم يكن بأن توجب ذاته كونه عالمًا بالبعض أولى من أن توجب كونه عالما بالباقي، فلما أوجب كونه عالما بالبعض وجب كونه عالما بالباقي، فثبت كونه تعالى عالما بكل المعلومات.
The second chapter on explaining that He, glorified and exalted, is knower of all known things. Its proof: He, glorified and exalted, is living, and everything that is living, it is valid for it to know every one of the known things. Also, the giver of this ‘being knower’ is His essence, and the relation of the essence to all is equal. So it is not that His essence makes Him knower of some rather than that it makes Him knower of the rest. So since He is knower of some, He must be knower of the rest. Thus it is established that He, exalted, is knower of all known things.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a self-contained proof that God knows all things. Razi presents a concise argument: (1) God is living (حي). (2) Anything that is living is capable of knowing every knowable thing (i.e., the living subject is not restricted by a particular capacity). (3) The cause (موجب) of God's knowledge is His own essence, not something external. (4) The essence's relation to all knowable objects is uniform (على السوية). (5) Therefore, if the essence makes Him knower of some things (which is established), it must make Him knower of all things, because there is no basis for partiality. So God knows everything. This argument relies on the equality of the essence's relation to all knowables and the fact that knowledge is a necessary concomitant of the living essence.

Key Terms
حي
عالم بكل المعلومات
موجب
نسبة الذات
على السوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the proof assumes God knows some things without establishing that He knows any.
Correction: The proof relies on prior established knowledge that God knows some things (e.g., Himself). The argument is not circular; it builds on previously proven attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Razi's conclusion that God must know all things?
A) Revelation
B) The uniform relation of the essence to all knowables
C) Consensus of scholars
D) The fact that God is all-powerful

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that God's knowledge is all-encompassing because His living essence causes knowledge uniformly with respect to every knowable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God's essence relates equally to every secret thought and action, do you live as if your inner life is fully exposed? What would change if you truly believed that nothing is hidden from Him, not even your most private doubts?
?
If God's knowledge is caused by His essence and not by anything you do, then your good deeds do not increase His knowledge and your sins do not diminish it. Does this make you more grateful or more careless? How will you respond to the fact that His knowledge is complete regardless of your efforts?
?
The proof shows that God must know all because His essence is the source and it relates equally to all. Are there areas of your life where you act as if God's knowledge is partial—as if He doesn't see certain habits or thoughts? What would it mean to surrender those areas to His all-encompassing awareness starting today?

The Opponent's Argument Against God's Self-Knowledge
Opponents' argument that God cannot know Himself because knowledge requires two distinct things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponent's argument that knowledge requires two distinct things, and why human self-knowledge is considered possible despite that.

Core Idea
Knowledge is a relation; a single thing cannot relate to itself.

The first group of opponents deny that God can know His own essence. They argue that knowledge is a relation between two distinct entities. In this lesson, we examine their reasoning and see how they distinguish between divine and human self-knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how they define knowledge as a specific relation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إضافة مخصوصة'.
2
Identify the conclusion
What conclusion do they draw about God knowing Himself? Why is it impossible according to them?
3
Note the exception for humans
They acknowledge that humans can know themselves. Why is this possible? Connect it to the concept of composition.
This distinction is crucial for the later refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول من المخالفين الذين يقولون : إنه يمتنع كونه تعالى عالما بذاته : احتجوا عليه بأن كون الشيء عالما بالشيء إضافة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم، وهذا لا يحصل إلا بين الشيئين، فالشيء الواحد من جميع الوجوه يمتنع كونه عالما بنفسه وهذا بخلاف علم الواحد منا بنفسه، فإن نفس الواحد منا ليست منزهة من جميع جهات التركب فلا جرم صح في الواحد منا أن يعلم نفسه. لا يقال : كونه تعالى عالمًا مغاير لكونه معلومًا، فلم لا يكفي هذا القدر من التغاير في حصول علمه بذاته؟ لأنا نقول : كونه عالمًا ومعلومًا فرع عن قيام العلم به، وقيام العلم به فرع عن هذا التغاير، فيلزم وقوع الدور. والجواب : قد دللنا على أنه تعالى عالم بشيء ما، وكل مَنْ علم الشيء أمكنه أن يعلم كونه عالما بذلك الشيء، ومن علم ذلك فقد علم نفسه، فثبت أنه تعالى عالم بنفسه قوله : " إن العلم إضافة مخصوصة، وإضافة الشيء إلى نفسه محال" قلنا : لا نسلم بدليل أنه يصح أن يقال : علم ذاته حقيقته.
The first type of opponents, those who say that it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be knower of His essence, argued: That a thing's being knower of a thing is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and this occurs only between two things. So a single thing from all aspects is impossible to be knower of itself. This is unlike the knowledge of one of us of himself, because the soul of one of us is not free from all aspects of composition, so it is valid for one of us to know himself. It cannot be said: His being knower is different from His being known, so why does this amount of difference not suffice for His knowledge of Himself? Because we say: His being knower and known are branches of the establishment of knowledge in Him, and the establishment of knowledge in Him is a branch of this difference, so circularity would occur. The answer: We have proven that He, exalted, is knower of something, and whoever knows a thing can know that he knows that thing, and whoever knows that has known himself. So it is established that He, exalted, is knower of Himself. Regarding their statement: 'Knowledge is a specific relation, and a thing's relation to itself is impossible,' we say: We do not accept it, based on the evidence that it is valid to say: 'He knows His essence is His reality.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage addresses the first group of opponents who deny that God can know His own essence. Their argument: Knowledge is a relation between two distinct things. A single thing cannot be related to itself; thus God cannot know Himself. They contrast this with human self-knowledge, which is possible because the human self has composition (body and soul) allowing a distinction. If we object that the difference between being knower and being known might suffice, they reply that this would lead to circularity: the two roles depend on knowledge, which depends on the difference. Razi's response: It has already been established that God knows something (some object). And anyone who knows something can know that he knows it (second-order knowledge). Therefore, God knows that He knows, and that constitutes knowledge of His own essence. As for the claim that a relation to self is impossible, Razi rejects it by noting that the phrase 'He knows His essence is His reality' is meaningful and valid, implying that self-knowledge is not incoherent.

Key Terms
إضافة مخصوصة
علم نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human self-knowledge is also impossible because it involves a relation to self.
Correction: The opponents argue it is possible because the human self is composite, providing the needed distinction between knower and known.

Checkpoint
According to the opponents, why is human self-knowledge possible?
A) Because humans are not real
B) Because the human self is composite
C) Because God made it possible
D) Because knowledge is not a relation

Key Takeaway
The opponents argue that knowledge requires two distinct things, so God, being absolutely simple, cannot know Himself, but humans can due to their composite nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does your own experience of self-awareness—knowing that you are the same person who thinks, feels, and acts—challenge or confirm the opponent's claim that a single thing cannot know itself? If you are composite, are you ever truly unified with yourself? What does that tell you about your need for a Creator who is perfectly simple and self-knowing?
?
The opponent allows humans to know themselves because we are composite. But your composite nature also means you are constantly changing and fragmented. In light of this lesson, do you trust your own self-knowledge to be reliable? Or does this remind you that only God's knowledge of Himself is perfect and unchanging? How should that affect your humility before Him?
?
If God cannot know Himself according to this argument, then He would be less than you in self-awareness. Does that possibility disturb you? Why or why not? What does your immediate reaction reveal about what you truly believe about God's perfection?

The Circularity Objection Against Self-Knowledge
Opponents refute a possible objection that the difference between being knower and known suffices for self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent's assumption that the difference between being knower and known suffices leads to circularity.

Core Idea
If difference of roles requires knowledge, knowledge requires the roles: a vicious circle.

After presenting the main objection, the opponents anticipate a possible reply: perhaps the mere difference between being knower and known is enough to establish self-knowledge. They show that this leads to circularity. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify what the potential objection is and how it is countered.
The objection is phrased as 'لا يقال' meaning 'it cannot be said.'
2
Understand the circularity
Why does the argument lead to circularity? Trace the dependency: being knower/known depends on knowledge, and knowledge depends on the difference.
This is a classic 'دور' (circularity) argument.
3
Reflect on the strength of the objection
Consider why this circularity is problematic for the opponent's own position.
It shows that the opponent cannot simply appeal to the difference of attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الأول من المخالفين الذين يقولون : إنه يمتنع كونه تعالى عالما بذاته : احتجوا عليه بأن كون الشيء عالما بالشيء إضافة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم، وهذا لا يحصل إلا بين الشيئين، فالشيء الواحد من جميع الوجوه يمتنع كونه عالما بنفسه وهذا بخلاف علم الواحد منا بنفسه، فإن نفس الواحد منا ليست منزهة من جميع جهات التركب فلا جرم صح في الواحد منا أن يعلم نفسه. لا يقال : كونه تعالى عالمًا مغاير لكونه معلومًا، فلم لا يكفي هذا القدر من التغاير في حصول علمه بذاته؟ لأنا نقول : كونه عالمًا ومعلومًا فرع عن قيام العلم به، وقيام العلم به فرع عن هذا التغاير، فيلزم وقوع الدور. والجواب : قد دللنا على أنه تعالى عالم بشيء ما، وكل مَنْ علم الشيء أمكنه أن يعلم كونه عالما بذلك الشيء، ومن علم ذلك فقد علم نفسه، فثبت أنه تعالى عالم بنفسه قوله : " إن العلم إضافة مخصوصة، وإضافة الشيء إلى نفسه محال" قلنا : لا نسلم بدليل أنه يصح أن يقال : علم ذاته حقيقته.
The first type of opponents, those who say that it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be knower of His essence, argued: That a thing's being knower of a thing is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and this occurs only between two things. So a single thing from all aspects is impossible to be knower of itself. This is unlike the knowledge of one of us of himself, because the soul of one of us is not free from all aspects of composition, so it is valid for one of us to know himself. It cannot be said: His being knower is different from His being known, so why does this amount of difference not suffice for His knowledge of Himself? Because we say: His being knower and known are branches of the establishment of knowledge in Him, and the establishment of knowledge in Him is a branch of this difference, so circularity would occur. The answer: We have proven that He, exalted, is knower of something, and whoever knows a thing can know that he knows that thing, and whoever knows that has known himself. So it is established that He, exalted, is knower of Himself. Regarding their statement: 'Knowledge is a specific relation, and a thing's relation to itself is impossible,' we say: We do not accept it, based on the evidence that it is valid to say: 'He knows His essence is His reality.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage addresses the first group of opponents who deny that God can know His own essence. Their argument: Knowledge is a relation between two distinct things. A single thing cannot be related to itself; thus God cannot know Himself. They contrast this with human self-knowledge, which is possible because the human self has composition (body and soul) allowing a distinction. If we object that the difference between being knower and being known might suffice, they reply that this would lead to circularity: the two roles depend on knowledge, which depends on the difference. Razi's response: It has already been established that God knows something (some object). And anyone who knows something can know that he knows it (second-order knowledge). Therefore, God knows that He knows, and that constitutes knowledge of His own essence. As for the claim that a relation to self is impossible, Razi rejects it by noting that the phrase 'He knows His essence is His reality' is meaningful and valid, implying that self-knowledge is not incoherent.

Key Terms
تغاير
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference between being knower and known is sufficient to establish self-knowledge without circularity.
Correction: The opponents show that this leads to circularity because the two roles depend on knowledge, which in turn depends on the difference.

Checkpoint
What problem does the opponents identify if we say the difference between being knower and known suffices?
A) It leads to infinite regress
B) It leads to circularity
C) It contradicts the unity of God
D) It implies God is composed

Key Takeaway
The opponents argue that appealing to the difference between knower and known to explain self-knowledge results in a vicious circle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's self-knowledge, do you assume that your own mental categories (like 'knower' and 'known') map onto God without distortion? How might this lesson expose an unexamined arrogance in your theological reasoning?
?
Have you ever caught yourself relying on a distinction that itself depends on what you are trying to explain? How does the warning against circularity here challenge you to inspect the foundations of your beliefs about God?
?
What would it cost you to admit that God's self-knowledge may be beyond logical models like 'knower' and 'known'? Are you willing to let go of neat explanations that might be circular, even if it leaves you with a humbler, more mysterious understanding of Him?

Razi's Refutation: From Knowledge of Something to Self-Knowledge
Razi provides a proof that God knows Himself and responds to the relation objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that God knows His own essence because He knows something and from that can know that He knows.

Core Idea
Knowing anything implies knowing that you know, which is self-knowledge.

In this final part, Razi presents his positive argument. He reminds us that it has been established that God knows something. From that, God can know that He knows, and that constitutes knowledge of Himself. He also rebuts the claim that self-relation is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the two parts of his response.
The answer begins with 'والجواب'.
2
Trace the argument chain
Follow the logic: God knows something → can know that He knows → that is self-knowledge.
This avoids circularity by starting with knowledge of an external object.
3
Examine the rebuttal of the relation objection
Razi rejects the claim that a relation to self is impossible by pointing to the meaningful statement 'He knows His essence is His reality.'
This shows that self-knowledge is coherent.

Response to the Second Objection: Essence Perfect in Itself
Razi rejects the idea that God's essence is perfected by attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that a perfect essence does not require external attributes for completion; rather, perfection itself necessitates the possession of attributes of perfection.

Core Idea
Perfection implies attributes – the essence is not deficient.

Razi now addresses the second objection which likely claimed that God's essence needs attributes to become perfect. He firmly rejects this, arguing that a perfect essence inherently necessitates attributes of perfection, not that the essence is deficient and completed by them. This lesson unpacks his concise rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Razi begins by asking 'Why is it not permissible to say...' – he is rejecting a possible claim.
The objection itself is not quoted, but the answer reveals its content.
2
Identify the two positions
Razi contrasts two views: (1) that the essence is deficient and completed by attributes (which he rejects), and (2) that the essence being perfect necessitates attributes of perfection (his view).
He explicitly says 'we do not say the essence is deficient... rather we say...'
3
Understand the core claim
Focus on the phrase 'كونها كاملة يستلزم حصول صفات الكمال' – its being perfect necessitates the occurrence of attributes of perfection. This is the key argument.
Perfection is not a state of lack but of fullness, so attributes flow from it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية عشرة
والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن كون تلك الذات كاملة يقتضي كونها مستلزمة لحصول هذا العلم ولا نقول: الذات ناقصة بذاتها، مستكملة بغيرها بل نقول: كونها كاملة يستلزم حصول صفات الكمال.
والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة: مبدأ العالم هو الذات الواجبة الوجود، الموصوفة بالعلم والقدرة، والذات هي الواجبة لذاتها وبذاتها وهي مستلزمة لهذه الصفات، فلم قلتم إن ذلك محال؟
The twelfth issue. And the answer to the second doubt: Why is it not permissible to say: That this essence being perfect necessitates it being imperative for the occurrence of this knowledge, and we do not say: the essence is deficient in itself, perfected by another, rather we say: its being perfect necessitates the occurrence of attributes of perfection. And the answer to the third doubt: The origin of the world is the essence necessary of existence, described with knowledge and power, and the essence is necessary in itself and by itself, and it necessitates these attributes. So why have you said that is impossible?
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's concise responses to two objections (shubah) about divine knowledge. In the first answer, he rejects the idea that God's essence is deficient and needs attributes to become perfect; instead, a perfect essence necessarily possesses attributes of perfection. In the second, he asserts that the necessary essence (al-dhat al-wajibat al-wujud) is inherently described with knowledge and power, and these follow necessarily from its nature. The objections themselves are not quoted, but Razi's answers show he is defending the intrinsic connection between God's essence and His attributes.

Key Terms
ذات كاملة
صفات الكمال
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's essence is deficient and requires attributes to become perfect.
Correction: Razi explicitly denies that the essence is deficient; rather, its perfection necessarily includes attributes of perfection.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does the perfection of God's essence entail?
A) It is deficient and needs attributes to be completed.
B) It necessitates the occurrence of attributes of perfection.
C) It is separate from all attributes.
D) It is perfected by another being.

Key Takeaway
A perfect essence does not lack attributes; rather, its perfection logically requires them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's perfection, do you ever subconsciously imagine that He 'lacks' something that His attributes supply? How does Razi's insistence that the essence itself is perfect challenge that mental habit?
?

?
If you truly believed that God's essence requires nothing outside itself, how would that reshape the way you ask from Him? Would it make your supplication more reverent or more desperate?

Response to the Third Objection: The Necessary Essence Possesses Attributes Inherently
Razi defends that the necessary essence inherently possesses attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends the view that the necessary essence (God) inherently possesses attributes like knowledge and power, and that these follow necessarily from its nature.

Core Idea
The necessary essence is inherently qualified with attributes.

The third objection likely argued that it is impossible for the necessary essence to be described with attributes, perhaps on grounds that attributes would be contingent. Razi counters by asserting that the origin of the world is the necessary essence, which by its very nature entails attributes like knowledge and power. This lesson examines his answer.

Guided Path

Read the answer

Razi states the essence is necessary in itself and by itself (واجبة لذاتها وبذاتها).
2
Identify the logical structure
Razi builds a chain: the origin of the world is the necessary essence, which is described with knowledge and power, and the essence is necessary in itself and necessitates these attributes. The question 'why have you said that is impossible?' challenges the objector.
The argument is: if the essence is necessary, and it entails attributes, then the attributes are not impossible.
3
Understand the term 'مستلزمة'
The phrase 'وهي مستلزمة لهذه الصفات' means the essence necessitates these attributes. This is key: attributes are not separate from the essence but necessarily follow from it.
This directly addresses the objection that attributes would make God composite or dependent.

Razi's Refutation of the Denial of God's Self-Knowledge
Razi refutes the first group who argue God cannot know His own essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the argument that God cannot know His own essence because knowledge requires a relation between two distinct things.

Core Idea
Knowing that God knows something implies God knows Himself.

Razi addresses the first group who deny that God can know His own essence, based on the claim that knowledge is a relation between two distinct things. He first presents a hypothetical objection that leads to circularity, then gives his own proof and rejects the opponent's definition of knowledge. This lesson traces the logical sequence of Razi's refutation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's premise
Read the opponent's argument that knowledge requires two distinct things. Notice how they distinguish human self-knowledge from God's.
The opponent says human self-knowledge is possible due to composition, but God is simple.
2
Examine the circularity objection
Follow Razi's hypothetical objection: if we say 'knower' and 'known' are distinct in God, that leads to circularity because knowledge depends on the relation and the relation depends on knowledge.
3
Grasp Razi's final answer
See how Razi proves God knows Himself by arguing that God knows something, and knowing that one knows that thing is self-knowledge. Also note he rejects the definition of knowledge as a relation.
Razi's final move is to reject the opponent's definition of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المخالفين في هذه المسألة طوائف ونحن نشير إلى شبهة كل واحد منهم إشارة خفية:
النوع الأول من المخالفين الذين يقولون: إنه يمتنع كونه تعالى عالما بذاته: احتجوا عليه بأن كون الشيء عالما بالشيء إضافة مخصوصة بين العالم وبين المعلوم، وهذا لا يحصل إلا بين الشيئين، فالشيء الواحد من جميع الوجوه يمتنع كونه عالما بنفسه وهذا بخلاف علم الواحد منا بنفسه، فإن نفس الواحد منا ليست منزهة من جميع جهات التركب فلا جرم صح في الواحد منا أن يعلم نفسه.
لا يقال: كونه تعالى عالمًا مغاير لكونه معلومًا، فلم لا يكفي هذا القدر من التغاير في حصول علمه بذاته؟ لأنا نقول: كونه عالمًا ومعلومًا فرع عن قيام العلم به، وقيام العلم به فرع عن هذا التغاير، فيلزم وقوع الدور.
والجواب: قد دللنا على أنه تعالى عالم بشيء ما، وكل من علم الشيء أمكنه أن يعلم كونه عالما بذلك الشيء، ومن علم ذلك فقد علم نفسه، فثبت أنه تعالى عالم بنفسه. قوله: "إن العلم إضافة مخصوصة، وإضافة الشيء إلى نفسه محال" قلنا: لا نسلم بدليل أنه يصح أن يقال: علم ذاته حقيقته.
And know that the opponents in this issue are groups, and we will allude to the doubt of each of them with a subtle reference.
The first type of opponents are those who say: It is impossible for Him, exalted, to be knower of His essence. They argued by that a thing's being knower of a thing is a specific relation between the knower and the known, and this occurs only between two things. So a single thing in all respects is impossible to be knower of itself. This is unlike the knowledge of any one of us of himself, for the self of any one of us is not free from all aspects of composition, so indeed it is valid for any one of us to know himself.
It may not be said: His being knower is different from His being known, so why is this degree of difference not sufficient for the occurrence of His knowledge of His essence? For we say: His being knower and known is derivative of the subsistence of knowledge in Him, and the subsistence of knowledge in Him is derivative of this difference, so circularity occurs.
And the answer: We have proven that He, exalted, is knower of something, and everyone who knows a thing it is possible for him to know that he knows that thing, and whoever knows that has known himself. So it is established that He, exalted, is knower of Himself. His statement: 'Knowledge is a specific relation, and the relation of a thing to itself is impossible.' We say: We do not accept that, by evidence that it is valid to say: He knows His essence as His reality.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage addresses the first group of opponents who deny that God can know His own essence. Their argument is that knowledge requires a relation between two distinct things, so a single entity cannot know itself. Razi counters first with a hypothetical objection: if we say 'knowing' and 'being known' are distinct, that might suffice, but that leads to circularity because knowledge and the relation are interdependent. His own answer: since we have already established that God knows something (from previous arguments), knowing that one knows that thing constitutes knowledge of oneself. He then rejects the opponent's definition of knowledge as a relation, asserting that it is valid to say God knows His essence as it truly is.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi agrees that knowledge is a relation.
Correction: Razi explicitly rejects the definition of knowledge as a relation in his final response.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the first group against God knowing His essence?
A) Knowledge requires a relation between two distinct entities.
B) God's essence is unknowable in principle.
C) Knowledge is a created accident.
D) God only knows universals, not individuals.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the denial of God’s self-knowledge by arguing that knowledge of one’s own knowledge constitutes self-knowledge and by rejecting the relational definition of knowledge.

?

?
The opponents argued that knowledge requires two distinct things, so God cannot know Himself. In your daily life, do you treat knowledge as a mere relation between you and facts, or do you recognize that some truths are known by direct presence? How would your spiritual life change if you pursued knowledge of God not as a concept but as a witness of His reality?

Refuting Multiplicity through Relations
Razi refutes the objection and redefines knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that multiple objects of knowledge would necessitate multiplicity in God's essence by redefining knowledge as relations.

Core Idea
Knowledge is relations, not forms — unity is preserved.

This passage presents a key objection to God's knowledge of multiple things: that it would lead to infinite multiplicity in His essence. Imam Razi responds with a profound redefinition of the nature of knowledge, showing that it consists of relations that do not compromise divine unity. The number one analogy illustrates this clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the overall argument before diving into details.
Pay attention to the contrast between the objection and the answer.
2
Identify the objection and its evidence
The opponents argue that knowledge of different things are distinct, proven by the possibility of knowing one while doubting another. Understand why they think this leads to infinite multiplicity in God.
The key term is مغايرة (difference) between knowledges.
3
Understand Razi's redefinition of knowledge
Razi denies that knowledge is an impressed form (صورة منطبعة) in the knower. Instead, it is a relation (نسبة) and addition (إضافة) between the knower and the known.
This shift from forms to relations is critical.
4
Examine the number one analogy
Razi uses the analogy of the number one having infinite relations (half, third, etc.) without compromising its oneness. Apply this to God's knowledge.
The analogy shows that many relations do not imply many essences.
5
Connect to divine simplicity
Reflect on how this answer preserves tawhid and God's transcendence. Confirm that the objection is refuted without denying God's omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من المخالفين الذين يسلمون كونه تعالى عالما بذاته المخصوصة لكنهم ينكرون كونه عالما بغيره واحتجوا عليه بأن العلم بأحد المعلومين مغاير للعلم بالمعلوم الآخر، بدليل أنه يصح أن يعلم كون زيد عالمًا بأحد المعلومين مع صحة الشك في كونه عالما بالمعلوم الآخر، والمعلوم غير المشكوك فكونه عالمًا بأحد المعلومين يوجب أن يكون مغايرا لكونه عالماً بالمعلوم الآخر.
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: لو كان الباري عالما بالمعلومات الكثيرة لوجب أن يحصل في ذاته بحسب كل معلوم علم على حدة، وعلى هذا التقدير يحصل في ذاته كثرة لا نهاية لها، وذلك محال.
الجواب: ليس العلم عبارة عن الصور المساوية للماهيات المعلومات المنطبعة في ذات العالم بل العلم عبارة عن نسب مخصوصة وإضافات مخصوصة بين ذات العالم وذات المعلوم، وإذا كان كذلك فكونه عالما بالمعلومات الكثيرة يقتضي أن يحصل لذاته نسب كثيرة وإضافات كثيرة، وهذا لا يقدح في وحدة الذات، بدليل أن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وثلث الثلاثة وربع الأربعة وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له من النسب ولم يقدح في كون الوحدة وحدة فكذلك القول في هذه المسألة.
The second type of opponents are those who affirm that He, exalted, is knower of His specific essence but deny that He is knower of other than Him. They argued that knowledge of one known thing is different from knowledge of another known thing, evidenced by that it is valid that Zayd is knower of one known thing while it is possible to doubt that he is knower of the other known thing, and the known is not the doubted. So his being knower of one known thing necessitates that it is different from his being knower of the other known thing. If this is established, we say: If the Maker were knower of many known things, then there would have to occur in His essence, corresponding to each known thing, a separate knowledge. On this assumption, there would occur in His essence an infinite multiplicity, and that is impossible. The answer: Knowledge is not the forms equal to the essences of the known things impressed in the essence of the knower. Rather knowledge is specific relations and specific additions between the essence of the knower and the essence of the known. If that is so, then His being knower of many known things requires that there occur for His essence many relations and many additions, and this does not impair the oneness of the essence, evidenced by that one is half of two, and a third of three, and a fourth of four, and so on to infinite relations, and it does not impair the oneness of oneness. So likewise the statement in this issue.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage deals with the second group who accept that God knows Himself but deny He knows anything else. Their argument: knowledge of different objects are distinct, so multiple objects would require multiple knowledges in God, leading to infinite multiplicity in His essence. Razi's answer redefines knowledge: it is not a set of impressed forms (which would be multiple) but rather relations (nisab) and additions (idafat) between knower and known. Many relations can exist without compromising the unity of the subject, just as the number one can have infinite relations (half, third, etc.) while remaining one. Thus God can know many things without multiplicity in His essence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that multiple objects of knowledge require multiple real entities (forms) in God, thus multiplying His essence.
Correction: Razi clarifies that knowledge is not an entity but a relation. Relations do not add to the substance; they are external to the essence. Hence many relations do not make the essence multiple.

Razi defends God's knowledge of all things without compromising unity by redefining knowledge as relations, not impressed forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi says that just as one number can have infinite relations without ceasing to be one, so God can have infinite knowledge-relations. Your life involves many relationships—with family, friends, colleagues. Do you ever worry that God is 'too busy' with others to attend to you? What would it take for you to rest in the truth that His unity allows Him to relate to each of you fully and simultaneously?
?
If knowledge is relational, then your ignorance of God is not a lack of information but a lack of relation. Does your heart feel the weight of that? Are you content with a 'form' of God in your mind, or are you seeking a real relation with the One who already relates to you? What is one step you can take today to turn your 'knowledge about' into 'knowledge of'?

God's Knowledge of Changing Particulars: Objection and Response
Razi presents the objection against God's knowledge of changing particulars and the first faction's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's knowledge of changing particulars would lead to ignorance or change, and the first faction's response that foreknowledge is identical to present knowledge.

Core Idea
What God knows about the future is exactly what He knows when it happens.

Theologians debate whether God knows changing particulars as they change. The third group of objectors argues that such knowledge would require change in God. In response, the majority of Sunni and Mu'tazili scholars claim that God's foreknowledge of an event is the same as His knowledge of it when it occurs. This lesson examines that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Focus on the third group's argument: if God knows Zayd is sitting, then after Zayd stands, either knowledge remains (ignorance) or changes (change in God).
This objection is based on the assumption that knowledge must conform to reality.
2
Understand the first faction's response
Notice that the first faction (majority of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) argue that foreknowledge and present knowledge are identical. They use the human example of knowing someone will enter the city tomorrow and still knowing it when they arrive.
The key is that for humans, forgetfulness interrupts the knowledge, but God has no forgetfulness.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If God's knowledge of future events is the same as His knowledge when they occur, does that solve the objection? The passage ends with introduction of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's rejection, which we will explore later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث من المخالفين الذين سلموا كونه تعالى عالما بالماهيات الكلية: لكنهم منعوا من كونه تعالى عالمًا بالمتغيرات من حيث هي متغيرة، واحتجوا عليه بأنه تعالى لو علم أن زيدا جالس الآن في هذا المكان فإذا قام زيد من ذلك المكان فإن بقي ذلك العلم كان جهلاً؛ لأن اعتقاده أنه جالس ههنا مع أنه غير جالس ههنا جهل، وإن لم يبق ذلك العلم كان تغيرًا والتغير على الله تعالى محال.
واعلم أن المتكلمين صاروا فريقين بسبب هذه الشبهة:
الفريق الأول. وهم جمهور المشايخ من أهل السنة ومن المعتزلة - قالوا: إن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد واحتجوا على قولهم بأنا إذا علمنا أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا فإذا استمر هذا العلم إلى الغد وإلى أن دخل زيد البلد فإنا بهذا العلم نعلم أن زيدًا دخل الآن البلد، فعلمنا بأن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد، وإنما يحتاج الواحد منا إلى علم آخر لأجل طريان الغفلة على العلم الأول، والباري تعالى لما امتنع طريان الغفلة عليه لا يكون علمه بأن الشيء الفلاني سيوجد هو نفس علمه بوجود ذلك الشيء حال ما يوجد.
جرم وأما أبو الحسين البصري فقال: هذا المذهب باطل، ويمتنع أن يقال: بأن
The third type of opponents are those who affirmed that He, exalted, is knower of universal essences but prevented that He is knower of changing things inasmuch as they are changing. They argued that if He knew that Zayd is sitting now in this place, then when Zayd stands from that place, if that knowledge remains, it would be ignorance, because His belief that he is sitting here while he is not sitting here is ignorance. If that knowledge does not remain, that would be change, and change is impossible for God. And know that the mutakallimun became two factions due to this doubt: The first faction – and they are the majority of the shaykhs from Ahl al-Sunna and from the Mu'tazila – said: That knowledge that a thing will exist is the very knowledge of its existence when it exists. They argued by that we, if we know that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, then if this knowledge continues until tomorrow and until Zayd enters the city, then by this knowledge we know that Zayd entered the city now. Thus we know that the knowledge that a thing will exist is the very knowledge of its existence when it exists. The one of us only needs another knowledge because of the occurrence of forgetfulness regarding the first knowledge. As for the Maker, exalted, since forgetfulness cannot occur to Him, His knowledge that a certain thing will exist is identical to His knowledge of the existence of that thing at the time it exists. Indeed, and as for Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, he said: This doctrine is false, and it is impossible to say that...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: God's knowledge of a changing particular would either remain after the change (becoming ignorance) or change itself (implying change in God). Razi then notes that theologians split into two factions. The first faction (majority of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) argue that foreknowledge and present knowledge are identical; as evidence, they point to human knowledge: if we know someone will come tomorrow and retain that knowledge, when he arrives we already know he has arrived. The only difference for humans is forgetfulness, which does not apply to God. Therefore, God's knowledge of a future event is the same as His knowledge when it occurs. The passage ends mid-sentence introducing Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's objection to this view, which is incomplete in the window.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the first faction denies any change in God's knowledge; rather they affirm that the same knowledge applies to both future and present, so no change occurs.
Correction: The first faction does not deny the possibility of change; they argue that the knowledge itself is unchanged—it is the same knowledge from before the event to after it occurs, avoiding ignorance and change.

Key Takeaway
The first faction's argument defends God's knowledge of changing particulars by identifying foreknowledge with present knowledge, thus avoiding ignorance or change in God.

?
The passage says humans need separate knowledge because of forgetfulness. Where in your life do you 'forget' that God already knows everything about you—your secret thoughts, your hidden sins? How does that forgetfulness affect your relationship with Him?
?

Defending Divine Knowledge Against Multiplicity
Razi presents an objection against divine knowledge of many things and refutes it with a relational definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends God's knowledge of multiple things without compromising divine unity by defining knowledge as a relation.

Core Idea
Knowledge is relation, not imprint—multiplicity of knowns does not fragment the One.

Imagine an objection: if God knows many things, His essence becomes filled with many distinct 'knowledges,' making Him composite. Razi answers by redefining what knowledge is. Let's trace this argument and see how a simple analogy resolves the tension.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the translation. Pay attention to how the opponent builds their argument: knowledge of different knowns are distinct, leading to infinite multiplicity in God if He knows many things.
The opponent uses a logical lemma: if two items are distinct in doubt, they are distinct in reality.
2
Identify the core of the refutation
Razi's answer redefines what 'knowledge' is. It is not an imprint or form in the knower's essence, but a relation between the knower and the known. Note that he uses the terms 'nisaab' (relations) and 'idaafaat' (additions).
3
Understand the analogy of the number one
Razi gives an analogy: the number one can have infinite relations (half of two, third of three, etc.) without losing its unity. Similarly, God can have many relations to many known things without His essence becoming multiple.
The analogy is powerful because it shows that relational multiplicity does not imply essential multiplicity.
4
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: why is this distinction important for Islamic theology? How does it protect tawheed? Razi shows that divine attributes like knowledge do not add separate entities to God's essence.
This connects to broader discussions about the nature of divine attributes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنهم ينكرون كونه عالما بغيره واحتجوا عليه بأن العلم بأحد المعلومين مغاير للمعلم بالمعلوم الآخر، بدليل أنه أن يعلم كون زيد عالمًا بأحد المعلومين، مع صحة الشك في كونه عالما بالمعلوم الآخر، والمعلوم غير المشكوك فكونه عالمًا بأحد المعلومين يوجب أن يكون مغايرا لكونه عالماً بالمعلوم الآخر. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كان الباري عالما بالمعلومات الكثيرة لوجب أن يحصل في ذاته بحسب كل معلوم علم على حدة، وعلى هذا التقدير يحصل في ذاته كثرة لا نهاية لها، وذلك محال. الجواب : ليس العلم عبارة عن الصور المساوية للماهيات المعلومات المنطبعة في ذات العالم بل العلم عبارة عن نسب مخصوصة وإضافات مخصوصة بين ذات العالم وذات المعلوم، وإذا كان كذلك فكونه عالما بالمعلومات الكثيرة يقتضي أن يحصل لذاته نسب كثيرة وإضافات كثيرة، وهذا لا يقدح في وحدة الذات، بدليل أن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وثلث الثلاثة وربع الأربعة وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له من النسب ولم يقدح في كون الوحدة وحدة فكذلك القول في هذه المسألة.
But they deny that He is knowing by something else and argued that knowledge of one known thing is different from knowledge of another known thing, evidence that if one knows that Zayd is knowledgeable of one known thing, while it is valid to doubt that he is knowledgeable of the other known thing, and the known is not the doubted, so his being knowledgeable of one known thing necessitates that it be different from his being knowledgeable of the other known thing. If this is established, we say: if the Creator were knowing many known things, then necessarily in His essence according to each known thing there would be a separate knowledge, and on this supposition there would occur in His essence an infinite multiplicity, and that is impossible. The answer: knowledge is not expressions of forms equal to the essences of known things imprinted in the essence of the knower; rather knowledge is expressions of specific relations and specific additions between the essence of the knower and the essence of the known. If that is so, then His being knowing of many known things necessitates that His essence have many relations and many additions, and this does not impair the unity of the essence. Evidence: the one is half of two, third of three, quarter of four, and so on to infinite relations, and it does not impair that its unity is unity. So is the statement in this question.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The opponent argues that if God knows multiple distinct things, His essence would contain a multiplicity of sciences, leading to an infinite plurality in the divine essence, which is impossible. Razi refutes by redefining the nature of knowledge: it is not a set of imprinted forms corresponding to each known object, but rather a set of relations between the knower and the known. Multiple relations do not fragment the essence, just as the number 'one' can sustain an infinite number of relations (e.g., being half of two, third of three) without losing its unity. Thus, multiplicity in known objects does not entail multiplicity in the essence of the knower.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God knows many things, His essence must contain multiple distinct knowledges, making Him composite.
Correction: Knowledge is a relation, not a separate entity in the essence. Many relations do not divide the essence, just as a single number can have many relations without losing unity.

Objection on Changing Particulars and the First Response
Razi presents the objection and the first faction's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma against God's knowledge of changing particulars and the first theological response that identifies future knowledge with present knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of future and present is one, immune to change or forgetfulness.

This passage confronts a serious objection to divine omniscience: if God knows a changing event in its present state, what happens when it changes? Theologians split over the answer, and the majority proposed a unified view of God's timeless knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma
Read the objection carefully. The opponents accept God's knowledge of universals but deny knowledge of changing particulars. Their proof is a dilemma: if God knows Zayd is sitting now, then when Zayd stands, either the knowledge remains (making God ignorant) or it changes (impossible for God).
The dilemma hinges on the assumption that the object's change forces a change in knowledge.
2
See the split among theologians
Notice that Razi states that theologians split into two factions due to this difficulty. The first faction, the majority of Sunnis and Mu'tazila, will be explained here; the second faction is not covered in this passage.
This split shows the significance of the objection.
3
Grasp the first group's argument
The first group argues that knowledge that something will exist is identical to knowledge of its existence when it occurs. They support this with the example of Zayd entering the city tomorrow. Humans need additional knowledge only because of forgetfulness; God has no forgetfulness, so His knowledge of the future is the same as His knowledge of the present.
The key is God's immunity from heedlessness (ghaflah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث من المخالفين الذين سلموا كونه تعالى عالما بالماهيات الكلية : لكنهم منعوا من كونه تعالى عالمًا بالمتغيرات من حيث هي متغيرة، واحتجوا عليه بأنه تعالى لو علم أن زيدا جالس الآن في هذا المكان فإذا قام زيد من ذلك المكان فإن بقي ذلك العلم كان جهلاً ؛ لأن اعتقاده أنه جالس ههنا مع أنه غير جالس ههنا جهل، وإن لم يبق ذلك العلم كان تغيرًا والتغير على الله تعالى محال. واعلم أن المتكلمين صاروا فريقين بسبب هذه الشبهة : الفريق الأول . وهم جمهور المشايخ من أهل السنة ومن المعتزلة - قالوا : إن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد واحتجوا على قولهم بأنا إذا علمنا أن زيدا سيدخل البلد غدًا فإذا استمر هذا العلم إلى الغد وإلى أن دخل زيد البلد فإنا بهذا العلم نعلم أن زيدًا دخل الآن البلد، فعلمنا بأن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد، وإنما يحتاج الواحد منا إلى علم آخر لأجل طريان الغفلة على العلم الأول، والباري تعالى لما امتنع طريان الغفلة عليه لا يكون علمه بأن الشيء الفلاني سيوجد هو نفس علمه بوجود ذلك الشيء حال ما يوجد.
The third type of opponents are those who affirmed that He, exalted, is knowing of universal essences, but they denied that He, exalted, is knowing of changing things insofar as they are changing. They argued that if He, exalted, knew that Zayd is now sitting in this place, then if Zayd stands from that place, if that knowledge remains, it is ignorance, because believing that he is here when he is not here is ignorance; and if that knowledge does not remain, then there is change, and change upon God, exalted, is impossible. Know that the theologians became two factions due to this difficulty: the first faction, and they are the majority of the masters from the People of the Sunna and from the Mu'tazila, said: knowledge that something will exist is the same knowledge as its existence when it exists. They argued for their statement: if we know that Zayd will enter the city tomorrow, and this knowledge continues until tomorrow and until Zayd enters the city, then by this knowledge we know that Zayd has now entered the city. We know that knowledge that something will exist is the same knowledge as its existence when it exists. One of us only needs another knowledge because of the occurrence of heedlessness on the first knowledge, and the Creator, exalted, because heedlessness upon Him is impossible, His knowledge that a certain thing will exist is the same as His knowledge of the existence of that thing at the time it exists.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage introduces a new objection and the resulting division among theologians. The opponents accept that God knows universal essences but deny that He knows changing particulars in their state of change. Their proof is a dilemma: if God knows Zayd is sitting now, when Zayd stands, either the knowledge remains (making God ignorant) or it changes (which is impossible for God). Then Razi notes that theologians split into two groups. The first group, the majority of Sunnis and Mu'tazila, argue that knowledge of a future event is identical to knowledge of that event when it occurs. They support this by an example: if we know Zayd will enter tomorrow, and that knowledge persists until he enters, we know by that same knowledge that he is entering. Humans need additional knowledge only due to being prone to forgetfulness; God has no such forgetfulness, so His knowledge of the future is exactly His knowledge of the present when the event occurs.

الغفلة
الجمهور
المشايخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that change in the known object forces change in God's knowledge.
Correction: The first group shows that God's knowledge of the future is identical to His knowledge of the present, so no change occurs; only human forgetfulness creates an illusion of change.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma raised by those who deny God's knowledge of changing particulars as changing?
A) If God knows a future event, His knowledge may be wrong.
B) If God knows Zayd is sitting, and then Zayd stands, either knowledge remains (ignorance) or changes (impossible for God).
C) God cannot know universals and particulars at the same time.
D) God's knowledge is eternal, so change is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The first group resolves the dilemma by affirming that God's knowledge of a future event is identical to His knowledge of its present occurrence, due to His freedom from forgetfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that God 'learns' new information as events unfold, as if His knowledge were like your own? How does this passage challenge that subtle anthropomorphism, and what would it mean for your trust in God if you fully believed His knowledge never changes?
?
The opponents' objection implies that change in known objects forces a change in God. In what area of your life do you subconsciously expect God to 'adjust' His will based on your actions? How can you repent from this and embrace the doctrine of one timeless knowledge?
?
The first group's response highlights that forgetfulness is the only reason humans need 'new' knowledge. What fears or anxieties in your life stem from a lack of trust that God's knowledge of your past, present, and future is complete and unchanging? Commit today to recalling this truth whenever you worry about the future.
