Objection from Relational Attributes (Sifat Idafiyya)
The second group argues that change is possible in relational attributes of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real and relational attributes and how an objection to divine immutability arises from the possibility of change in relational attributes.

Core Idea
Change in God's relational attributes does not imply change in His essence.

This passage presents a key objection to the claim that God is absolutely immutable. The opponents argue that while real attributes (like knowledge) may be unchanging, relational attributes (like being 'with' creation) necessarily change as creation changes. Understanding this distinction is crucial for defending divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the objection structure. Notice how the opponents separate real from relational attributes.
The objection uses a concrete example: God's 'withness' to created things.
2
Understand the distinction
Identify the two categories: real attributes (sifat haqiqiyya) which are unchanging, and relational attributes (sifat idafiyya) which can change. The opponents concede that the proof against change applies only to the first category.
Relational attributes depend on created entities and thus change with them.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the example of God being 'with' a created thing. When the thing comes into existence, the relation of 'withness' is established; when it perishes, that relation ends. This illustrates a change in a relational attribute.
The example shows that change in relational attributes does not affect God's essence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The opponents conclude that changes in relations and attachments (ta'allaqat) are not impossible. Think about how this objection challenges the claim of absolute immutability.
This sets the stage for a theological response distinguishing essential from relational change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللفريق الثاني أن يقولوا : ما ذكرتم من الدليل المانع من التغير إنما يجري في الصفات الحقيقية، أما الصفات الإضافية فلا يمكن منع التغير فيها، وكيف لا نقول هذا، وإذا وجد حادث فإن الله تعالى يكون معه فإذا فني ذلك الحادث بطلت تلك المعية ؟
وهذا يقتضي وقوع التغير في الإضافات، وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: هذه التعلقات
من باب النسب والإضافات، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يمتنع وقوع التغيرات فيها.
And to the second group they may say: What you mentioned from the proof preventing change applies only to real attributes (al-sifat al-haqiqiyya). As for the relational attributes (al-sifat al-idafiyya), it is not possible to prevent change in them. And how can we not say this? For when an accident (hadith) occurs, God (ta'ala) is with it, and when that accident perishes, that 'withness' is nullified. This entails the occurrence of change in the relations. And if this is established, then we say: These attachments (al-ta'allaqat) are of the category of relations (nisab) and additions (idafat). And if such is the case, then the occurrence of changes in them is not impossible.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (i'tirad) from a group that distinguishes between real attributes (al-sifat al-haqiqiyya) and relational attributes (al-sifat al-idafiyya). They concede that the previous proof that change is impossible applies only to the former, not the latter. They illustrate with an example: when a created thing comes into existence, God is described as being "with" it (ma'iyya), and when that thing perishes, the relation of "withness" ceases. This is a change in a relational attribute. They conclude that such changes in relations and attachments are permissible.

Key Terms
صفات حقيقية (real attributes)
صفات إضافية (relational attributes)
تعلقات (attachments)
نسب (relations)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any change in God's attributes implies imperfection in His essence.
Correction: The objection clarifies that relational attributes change without affecting God's essence; they depend on created things, not on God Himself.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what type of attributes can undergo change?
A) Real attributes
B) Relational attributes
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The objection forces theologians to clarify that only real attributes are immutable, while relational attributes can change without affecting God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often stress about whether your changing circumstances affect your relationship with God. But if God's essence never changes, and only the relation adapts to your state, could it be that your anxiety stems from forgetting that He is always constant? Today, will you meditate on one relational attribute of God—like His being 'with you' even in your lowest moments—and let it transform how you face a specific trial you are avoiding?
?
The objector mentions that when a created thing perishes, God's 'withness' to it ends. Consider a past sin you remember vividly: in that moment, God was 'with you' in His knowledge and justice. Now that you have repented, that relation has shifted to mercy. How will this truth change the way you speak about your past to yourself or others? Will you stop using your past as an excuse and instead live today in the light of His relational mercy?

Hisham's First Argument: Foreknowledge Necessitates Determinism
Hisham's first doubt and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Hisham's first proof that God's eternal knowledge of particulars leads to the negation of power and the refutation of this argument.

Core Idea
Foreknowledge does not cause the action; power remains intact.

Hisham ibn al-Hakam argued that if God knew all future particulars, they would be necessitated, eliminating all power. In this lesson, we examine his first proof and the response that shows foreknowledge is compatible with power and does not negate it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand Hisham's first doubt and the response.
2
Identify the logical chain
Notice how Hisham argues from God's foreknowledge to the necessity of actions and then to the denial of power.
3
Understand the refutation
The response points out that our own knowledge of our power to act contradicts Hisham's conclusion. Reflect on how the response refutes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع من المخالفين الذين قالوا: إنه تعالى في الأزل كان عالما بحقائق الأشياء وماهياتها وأما العلم بالأشخاص والأحوال فذلك إنما يحصل عند حدوث تلك الأشخاص، وهذا مذهب هشام بن الحكم ومذهب أبي الحسين البصري، كأنه لا يتمشى إلا بالتزام هذا المذهب.
واحتج هشام بن الحكم على هذا المذهب بوجوه :
الأول : لو كان تعالى عالمًا في الأزل بجميع الجزئيات التي توجد في لا يزال لكان عالما بكل ما يصدر من الناس من أفعالهم وعالما بما لا يصدر عنهم، وكل ما علم الله تعالى وقوعه كان واجب الوقوع، وكل ما علم الله تعالى عدم وقوعه كان ممتنع الوقوع فيلزم أن يقال: جميع أفعال الخلق إما واجبة الوقوع، أو ممتنعة الوقوع، ولو كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن لشيء من الحيوانات قدرة على الفعل.
،
لأن الذي كان معلوم الله تعالى أنه يوجد يكون واجب الوقوع، والذي علم أنه لا يصدر منه يكون ممتنع الوقوع ، ولا قدرة البتة لا على ما يكون واجب الوقوع، ولا على ما يكون ممتنع الوقوع، وهذا يقتضي أن لا يكون لله تعالى قدرة البتة، وأن لا يكون لشيء من المخلوقات قدرة ،البتة وأن تكون التكاليف وبعثة الرسل كلها عبئًا ضائعا، وأن يكون الوعد والوعيد والثواب والعقاب كلها عبثًا وجوراً، وهذا يبطل
القول بالربوبية.
،
لأن نفي القدرة عن الله تعالى يبطل القول بالربوبية، ويبطل القول أيضًا بالعبودية ؛ لأن العبد إذا لم تكن له قدرة على العبودية، كان الأمر والنهي عبثاً، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك

وجب أن يقال: إنه تعالى كان عالما في الأزل بذاته، وبصفاته وبماهيات الأشياء وحقائقها وصفاتها، وأما العلم بالأشخاص وأحوالها المعتبرة فذلك لا يحصل إلا عند دخولها في الوجود حتى تندفع الإشكالات.
الشبهة الثانية : كل ما كان معلوما فهو متميز عن غيره وكل ما له تميز وتخصص وتعين فهو ثابت متحقق وما لا يكون ثابتا ولا متعينا ولا متحققا وجب أن لا يكون معلوماً، وهذه الأشخاص وصفاتها وأحوالها كانت نفيًا محضا وعدمًا صرفاً قبل دخولها في الوجود
فوجب أن لا تكون معلومة.
،
لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المعدوم شيء وذات فلا جرم لم يمتنع كونها
معلومة ؟
لأنا نقول : القول بأن المعدوم شيء هو باطل، وبتقدير تسليمه فالثابت في العدم إنما هي الذوات والحقائق والماهيات أما الذوات ينعت كونها مركبة مؤلفة موصوفة بالأعراض، فغير ثابتة في العدم بالاتفاق، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن لا تكون هذه الأشخاص والأحوال معلومة قبل تحققها.
،
الشبهة الثالثة : لو كان عالما بكل ما سيدخل في الوجود، لكان عالما بعدد ما يدخل في الوجود من حركات أهل الجنة وأهل النار وكل ما كان عدده معلومًا كان متناهيا فيلزم إثبات النهاية لثواب أهل الجنة ولعقاب أهل النار، وذلك محال، فعلمنا أنه تعالى لا يعلم هذه المتغيرات إلا عند وقوعها.
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: أنه منقوض بأن كل واحد منا يعلم والقدرة على
الإيقاع أصل الوقوع والتبع للشيء لا يكون مانعا من الأصل. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أنه منقوض بأن كل واحد منا يعلم أن الشمس
غدًا تطلع من مشرقها لا من مغربها، وهذا المعدوم معلوم. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة: أنه تعالى إنما يعلم الشيء كما هو، فإن كان له علمه كذلك، وإن كان له عدد غير محصور كان علمه كذلك.
The fourth type of opponents are those who said: He (ta'ala) in eternity was knowing the realities of things and their essences. As for knowledge of individuals and states, that only occurs at the occurrence of those individuals. This is the doctrine of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and the doctrine of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. It seems it cannot be carried out except by adhering to this doctrine.
Hisham ibn al-Hakam argued for this doctrine with proofs:
First: If He (ta'ala) were knowing in eternity all the particulars that exist in what will always be, He would be knowing everything that issues from people of their actions and knowing what does not issue from them. And everything that God (ta'ala) knew would occur, its occurrence would be necessary; and everything that God knew would not occur, its occurrence would be impossible. So it would be necessary to say: all actions of creation are either necessary of occurrence or impossible of occurrence. If that were the case, no animal would have any power (qudra) over an action. Because that which God knew would exist is necessary of occurrence, and that which He knew would not issue from him is impossible of occurrence. There is absolutely no power over that which is necessary of occurrence nor over that which is impossible of occurrence. This would necessitate that God (ta'ala) has absolutely no power, and that no creature has any power at all, and that obligations and sending of messengers are all wasted burden, and that promise and threat, reward and punishment are all frivolity and injustice. This invalidates the doctrine of lordship (rububiyya). Because negating power from God invalidates the doctrine of lordship, and also invalidates the doctrine of servitude; for if the servant has no power over servitude, command and prohibition are frivolous. If that is the case,

It must be said: He (ta'ala) was knowing in eternity His essence, His attributes, the essences of things, their realities and their attributes. As for knowledge of individuals and their considered states, that does not occur except upon their entering into existence, so that the difficulties are removed.
Second doubt: Everything that is known is distinguished from other than it. Everything that has distinction, specification and determination is fixed and realized. That which is not fixed, not specified and not realized must not be known. These individuals, their attributes and states were pure negation and absolute non-existence before their entry into existence. Therefore they must not be known.
It is not said: Why is it not permissible to say: non-existence (al-ma'dum) is a thing and an essence, so therefore it is not prevented from being known? For we say: The statement that non-existence is a thing is false. And even if it were granted, what is fixed in non-existence is only the essences, realities and quiddities. As for the essances in terms of being composite, composed, and described by accidents, that is not fixed in non-existence by agreement. If such is the case, then these individuals and states must not be known before their actualization.
Third doubt: If He were knowing everything that will enter into existence, He would know the number of what enters into existence of the movements of the people of Paradise and the people of Hell. Everything whose number is known is finite. This necessitates establishing finitude for the reward of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hell, and that is impossible. So we know that He (ta'ala) does not know these changing things except at their occurrence.
The answer to the first doubt: It is refuted by the fact that each one of us knows that he has power to perform the act, and the origin of occurrence is power. What follows from something does not prevent the origin. The answer to the second doubt: It is refuted by the fact that each one of us knows that the sun will rise tomorrow from its east, not from its west, and this non-existent is known. The answer to the third doubt: He (ta'ala) only knows a thing as it is. So if it has a finite number, He knows it as such; and if it has an uncountable number, His knowledge is like that.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage combines the position of the fourth group (Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri) and their three proofs (shubah) against God's knowledge of particulars before they exist, along with the responses (ajwiba) to each. It begins by stating their claim: God in eternity knows essences and realities, but knowledge of individuals and their states only occurs at their occurrence. Then Hisham's first proof: if God knew all particulars in eternity, then what He knows will occur becomes necessary, and what He knows will not occur becomes impossible, thus negating all power (qudra) – both God's and creatures' – making commands, sending messengers, reward and punishment all futile. The conclusion is that God must only know essences and not particulars until they exist. Then the second proof: a thing known must be distinct and determinate, but particulars before existence are pure non-existence and thus cannot be known. It includes a rebuttal of the claim that 'non-existence is a thing'. The third proof: if God knew all future things, He would know the number of movements of the people of Paradise and Hell, which would be finite, implying a finite reward and punishment, which is impossible. The responses: the first proof is refuted by pointing out that our own knowledge that we have power to act does not contradict God's foreknowledge – the knowledge of what will happen does not prevent the act. The second proof is refuted by the example that we know the sun will rise from the east tomorrow, which is a non-existent thing that is known. The third proof is refuted by stating that God knows things as they are: if a thing has a finite number, He knows it as finite; if it has an infinite number, He knows it as infinite.

Key Terms
واجب الوقوع (necessary occurrence)
ممتنع الوقوع (impossible occurrence)
قدرة (power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge of future actions makes them inevitable, so humans have no real choice or power.
Correction: The refutation shows that foreknowledge does not cause the action; a person can still have power to act, and God's knowledge simply aligns with what will happen.

Checkpoint
What is Hisham's first argument against God's knowledge of particulars in eternity?
A) It would imply that God is subject to change.
B) It would lead to determinism, negating all power.
C) It would require an infinite number of sciences in God.
D) It would make God's knowledge imperfect.

Key Takeaway
God's foreknowledge does not compel actions; power remains with the agent, and knowledge is dependent on the reality of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever excuse your sins by thinking, 'God already knew I would do this, so I had no choice'? If so, how can you take full responsibility for your actions while still affirming that God knows all?
?
If you truly believed that God's foreknowledge does not compel your actions, how would that change the way you approach your daily decisions and prayers for guidance?
?
Reflect on a moment when you felt trapped by your past mistakes. How does the truth that knowledge follows the known free you from feeling predetermined, and what will you do today to exercise your God-given power to choose differently?

Hisham's Second Argument: Non-Existence Cannot Be Known
Hisham's second doubt and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Hisham's second proof that non-existent particulars cannot be objects of knowledge, and the refutation using the example of future sunrise.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires a determinate object; nonexistence lacks determination.

Hisham's second argument appeals to the principle that only determinate, existing things can be known. Since future particulars do not yet exist, they cannot be known. In this lesson, we analyze this argument and the response that shows we do know future events like the sunrise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second doubt and its answer in Arabic and English.
2
Understand the principle
Note the claim: what is known must be distinguished and fixed. Non-existence is neither.
3
Analyze the refutation
The response gives a counterexample: we know the sun will rise tomorrow, which is a non-existent future event. Therefore, non-existence can be known.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع من المخالفين الذين قالوا: إنه تعالى في الأزل كان عالما بحقائق الأشياء وماهياتها وأما العلم بالأشخاص والأحوال فذلك إنما يحصل عند حدوث تلك الأشخاص، وهذا مذهب هشام بن الحكم ومذهب أبي الحسين البصري، كأنه لا يتمشى إلا بالتزام هذا المذهب.
واحتج هشام بن الحكم على هذا المذهب بوجوه :
الأول : لو كان تعالى عالمًا في الأزل بجميع الجزئيات التي توجد في لا يزال لكان عالما بكل ما يصدر من الناس من أفعالهم وعالما بما لا يصدر عنهم، وكل ما علم الله تعالى وقوعه كان واجب الوقوع، وكل ما علم الله تعالى عدم وقوعه كان ممتنع الوقوع فيلزم أن يقال: جميع أفعال الخلق إما واجبة الوقوع، أو ممتنعة الوقوع، ولو كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن لشيء من الحيوانات قدرة على الفعل.
،

القول بالربوبية.

لأن نفي القدرة عن الله تعالى يبطل القول بالربوبية، ويبطل القول أيضًا بالعبودية ؛ لأن العبد إذا لم تكن له قدرة على العبودية، كان الأمر والنهي عبثاً، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك

وجب أن يقال: إنه تعالى كان عالما في الأزل بذاته، وبصفاته وبماهيات الأشياء وحقائقها وصفاتها، وأما العلم بالأشخاص وأحوالها المعتبرة فذلك لا يحصل إلا عند دخولها في الوجود حتى تندفع الإشكالات.
الشبهة الثانية : كل ما كان معلوما فهو متميز عن غيره وكل ما له تميز وتخصص وتعين فهو ثابت متحقق وما لا يكون ثابتا ولا متعينا ولا متحققا وجب أن لا يكون معلوماً، وهذه الأشخاص وصفاتها وأحوالها كانت نفيًا محضا وعدمًا صرفاً قبل دخولها في الوجود
فوجب أن لا تكون معلومة.
،
لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المعدوم شيء وذات فلا جرم لم يمتنع كونها
معلومة ؟
لأنا نقول : القول بأن المعدوم شيء هو باطل، وبتقدير تسليمه فالثابت في العدم إنما هي الذوات والحقائق والماهيات أما الذوات ينعت كونها مركبة مؤلفة موصوفة بالأعراض، فغير ثابتة في العدم بالاتفاق، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن لا تكون هذه الأشخاص والأحوال معلومة قبل تحققها.
،
الشبهة الثالثة : لو كان عالما بكل ما سيدخل في الوجود، لكان عالما بعدد ما يدخل في الوجود من حركات أهل الجنة وأهل النار وكل ما كان عدده معلومًا كان متناهيا فيلزم إثبات النهاية لثواب أهل الجنة ولعقاب أهل النار، وذلك محال، فعلمنا أنه تعالى لا يعلم هذه المتغيرات إلا عند وقوعها.
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: أنه منقوض بأن كل واحد منا يعلم والقدرة على
الإيقاع أصل الوقوع والتبع للشيء لا يكون مانعا من الأصل. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أنه منقوض بأن كل واحد منا يعلم أن الشمس
غدًا تطلع من مشرقها لا من مغربها، وهذا المعدوم معلوم. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة: أنه تعالى إنما يعلم الشيء كما هو، فإن كان له علمه كذلك، وإن كان له عدد غير محصور كان علمه كذلك.
The fourth type of opponents are those who said: He (ta'ala) in eternity was knowing the realities of things and their essences. As for knowledge of individuals and states, that only occurs at the occurrence of those individuals. This is the doctrine of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and the doctrine of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. It seems it cannot be carried out except by adhering to this doctrine.
Hisham ibn al-Hakam argued for this doctrine with proofs:
First: If He (ta'ala) were knowing in eternity all the particulars that exist in what will always be, He would be knowing everything that issues from people of their actions and knowing what does not issue from them. And everything that God (ta'ala) knew would occur, its occurrence would be necessary; and everything that God knew would not occur, its occurrence would be impossible. So it would be necessary to say: all actions of creation are either necessary of occurrence or impossible of occurrence. If that were the case, no animal would have any power (qudra) over an action. Because that which God knew would exist is necessary of occurrence, and that which He knew would not issue from him is impossible of occurrence. There is absolutely no power over that which is necessary of occurrence nor over that which is impossible of occurrence. This would necessitate that God (ta'ala) has absolutely no power, and that no creature has any power at all, and that obligations and sending of messengers are all wasted burden, and that promise and threat, reward and punishment are all frivolity and injustice. This invalidates the doctrine of lordship (rububiyya). Because negating power from God invalidates the doctrine of lordship, and also invalidates the doctrine of servitude; for if the servant has no power over servitude, command and prohibition are frivolous. If that is the case,

It must be said: He (ta'ala) was knowing in eternity His essence, His attributes, the essences of things, their realities and their attributes. As for knowledge of individuals and their considered states, that does not occur except upon their entering into existence, so that the difficulties are removed.
Second doubt: Everything that is known is distinguished from other than it. Everything that has distinction, specification and determination is fixed and realized. That which is not fixed, not specified and not realized must not be known. These individuals, their attributes and states were pure negation and absolute non-existence before their entry into existence. Therefore they must not be known.
It is not said: Why is it not permissible to say: non-existence (al-ma'dum) is a thing and an essence, so therefore it is not prevented from being known? For we say: The statement that non-existence is a thing is false. And even if it were granted, what is fixed in non-existence is only the essences, realities and quiddities. As for the essances in terms of being composite, composed, and described by accidents, that is not fixed in non-existence by agreement. If such is the case, then these individuals and states must not be known before their actualization.
Third doubt: If He were knowing everything that will enter into existence, He would know the number of what enters into existence of the movements of the people of Paradise and the people of Hell. Everything whose number is known is finite. This necessitates establishing finitude for the reward of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hell, and that is impossible. So we know that He (ta'ala) does not know these changing things except at their occurrence.
The answer to the first doubt: It is refuted by the fact that each one of us knows that he has power to perform the act, and the origin of occurrence is power. What follows from something does not prevent the origin. The answer to the second doubt: It is refuted by the fact that each one of us knows that the sun will rise tomorrow from its east, not from its west, and this non-existent is known. The answer to the third doubt: He (ta'ala) only knows a thing as it is. So if it has a finite number, He knows it as such; and if it has an uncountable number, His knowledge is like that.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage combines the position of the fourth group (Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri) and their three proofs (shubah) against God's knowledge of particulars before they exist, along with the responses (ajwiba) to each. It begins by stating their claim: God in eternity knows essences and realities, but knowledge of individuals and their states only occurs at their occurrence. Then Hisham's first proof: if God knew all particulars in eternity, then what He knows will occur becomes necessary, and what He knows will not occur becomes impossible, thus negating all power (qudra) – both God's and creatures' – making commands, sending messengers, reward and punishment all futile. The conclusion is that God must only know essences and not particulars until they exist. Then the second proof: a thing known must be distinct and determinate, but particulars before existence are pure non-existence and thus cannot be known. It includes a rebuttal of the claim that 'non-existence is a thing'. The third proof: if God knew all future things, He would know the number of movements of the people of Paradise and Hell, which would be finite, implying a finite reward and punishment, which is impossible. The responses: the first proof is refuted by pointing out that our own knowledge that we have power to act does not contradict God's foreknowledge – the knowledge of what will happen does not prevent the act. The second proof is refuted by the example that we know the sun will rise from the east tomorrow, which is a non-existent thing that is known. The third proof is refuted by stating that God knows things as they are: if a thing has a finite number, He knows it as finite; if it has an infinite number, He knows it as infinite.

Key Terms
المعدوم (the non-existent)
متميز (distinguished)
ثابت (fixed)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existent things cannot be known because they have no reality.
Correction: The refutation shows that we do know future events like the sunrise, so non-existence does not preclude knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Hisham's second argument, why are future particulars not known?
A) They do not exist.
B) They are not distinguished or determined.
C) God has no power over them.
D) They are infinite.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of future events is possible even though they do not exist, as demonstrated by our knowledge of the sunrise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know with certainty that the sun will rise tomorrow, yet you sometimes doubt God's knowledge of your personal future. How does this inconsistency reveal a weakness in your trust in divine foreknowledge? What will you do today to strengthen that trust?
?
If you were to argue that only existing things can be known, you would contradict your own daily certainty about tomorrow. Are you willing to accept that your reasoning about God's knowledge might be similarly flawed? What assumptions about God are you holding onto that you have not examined?
?
The refutation of Hisham's argument uses a simple everyday example. Where else in your life do you rely on knowledge of things that do not yet exist? Does this lesson challenge you to see God's knowledge as more comprehensive than your limited reasoning? How will you let this insight change your prayer and reliance on Him?

Hisham's Third Argument: The Finitude of Heavenly Reward
Hisham's third doubt and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Hisham's third proof that if God knew all future details, the number of actions in Paradise and Hell would be finite, but reward and punishment are infinite; and the refutation that God knows infinite numbers just as they are.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is not limited by finitude; He knows infinite numbers as infinite.

Hisham's third argument is a cosmological one: if God knew all particulars, He would know the number of movements in Paradise and Hell. Since numbers are finite when known, this would make reward finite, which is impossible. The response clarifies that God knows things as they are: if a number is infinite, He knows it as infinite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the third doubt and its answer.
2
Understand the logical necessity
Hisham assumes that known numbers must be finite, leading to contradiction.
3
Grasp the refutation
The response says God knows each thing as it is; if a number is infinite, He knows it as such, so no contradiction.

Source Text

النوع الرابع من المخالفين الذين قالوا: إنه تعالى في الأزل كان عالما بحقائق الأشياء وماهياتها وأما العلم بالأشخاص والأحوال فذلك إنما يحصل عند حدوث تلك الأشخاص، وهذا مذهب هشام بن الحكم ومذهب أبي الحسين البصري، كأنه لا يتمشى إلا بالتزام هذا المذهب.
واحتج هشام بن الحكم على هذا المذهب بوجوه :

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How would your actions change today if you deeply believed that God's knowledge of your future is as real as your present breath?
?

?

Denial of God's Knowledge of the Infinite: Their First Proof
Razi states the fifth group's position and their first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof used by those who deny that God knows infinite things, and how Razi presents their argument and a preliminary rebuttal.

Core Idea
Infinite known objects would require infinite sciences in God, which opponents deem impossible.

Razi continues his survey of objectors by introducing a fifth group who deny that God can know an infinite number of things. They present a proof based on the principle that knowledge is multiplied according to the multiplicity of known objects. In this passage, we see their argument and a brief dismissal of a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: identification of opponents, their doubt, and the objection-reply.
The passage is concise but contains a full argument.
2
Identify the key claim
The opponents assert that knowledge must be multiple according to the multiplicity of known objects. Underline this principle.
This is the core premise of their proof.
3
Understand the consequence
If known objects are infinite, then infinite sciences would exist in God's essence. This is presented as an absurdity (though not explicitly stated).
The opponents consider infinite sciences impossible for one essence.
4
Examine the objection and dismissal
Razi mentions a potential objection: one might affirm 'being knowing' (alimiyya) rather than multiple sciences. He dismisses this as mere dispute (niza').
Why is this only a verbal dispute? Consider the underlying issue.
5
Reflect on the argument's structure
Notice that the proof has two parts: a premise (multiplicity principle) and a conclusion (infinite sciences). The objection tries to avoid the conclusion by redefining terms.
Razi will later refute the premise itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس من المخالفين الذين ينكرون كونه تعالى عالما بما لا نهاية له من المعلومات ولهم فيه شبه :
الشبهة الأولى : أنا لما بينا أنه يجب تعدد العلم بتعدد المعلومات فلو كانت المعلومات غير متناهية لحصل في ذات الله تعالى علوم غير متناهية . ولو أن قائلا يقول : إنا لا نثبت العلم لله تعالى بل نثبت العالمية كان هذا نزاعا
The fifth type of opponents are those who deny that He (ta'ala) knows infinite things (ma la nihaya lahu min al-ma'lumat), and they have regarding it doubts. The first doubt: since we have explained that knowledge must be multiplied according to the multiplicity of known objects, if the known objects were infinite, then there would occur in the essence of God infinite sciences. And if someone should say: we do not affirm knowledge ('ilm) for God, but rather we affirm 'being knowing' ('alimiyya), this would be a dispute (niza').
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage introduces a fifth group of opponents who deny that God can have knowledge of infinite things. They present a proof (shubha) based on the principle that knowledge is multiple according to the multiplicity of known objects. If there were an infinite number of known objects, then there would be an infinite number of sciences in God's essence, which they consider impossible. The text also mentions a potential objection: one might argue that we do not affirm a multiplicity of sciences but only a single attribute of being knowing ('alimiyya). The author dismisses this as mere dispute (niza'), implying it is not a substantive refutation.

Key Terms
ما لا نهاية له من المعلومات
تعدد العلم
العالمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that infinite knowledge implies infinite attributes in God, but the real issue is whether knowledge is identical with the known or not.
Correction: The passage shows that the opponents' argument relies on a specific principle (multiplicity of knowledge) and that Razi dismisses a semantic objection as irrelevant.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof of those who deny that God knows infinite things?
A) Infinite knowledge would make God temporal.
B) Infinite knowns require infinite sciences in God.
C) God's knowledge is not multiple.
D) The infinite is impossible in any sense.

Key Takeaway
The opponents' proof rests on equating the number of knowns with the number of sciences, a premise that Razi will later refute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponents demanded that God's knowledge must be multiple because objects are multiple. In your own worship, do you treat God as limited by your own mental categories? Today, commit to one area where you will consciously affirm God's infinite knowledge—especially where you are tempted to think He doesn't see or know.
?
Razi calls the objection a 'verbal dispute' (niza'). When you debate matters of faith, are you sometimes more attached to winning an argument or using the right words than to the truth about Allah? What would it mean for you to let go of the need to be right and simply submit to what the Quran and Sunnah teach about Allah's attributes?

Razi introduces the fifth category of opponents on divine knowledge.

Second Objection on God's Knowledge of Non-Existent Particulars
Imam Razi presents and answers an objection that non-existent things cannot be known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that non-existent particulars cannot be known and how Imam Razi refutes it using the example of human knowledge of future events.

Core Idea
The objection fails because humans themselves know future non-existent events.

This lesson examines a second objection to God's knowledge of particulars before they exist: how can something non-existent be known, since knowledge requires a real object? The objection argues that non-existents are pure nothingness and thus unknowable. Imam Razi presents the objection, refutes a potential counter-response, and then gives a powerful answer using empirical human knowledge as a counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the objection and answer.
Notice the logical structure: objection → anticipated reply → refutation → final answer.
2
Understand the objection's logic
The objection states: knowledge requires the known to be real and determinate. Non-existent particulars are not real, so they cannot be known.
The key terms are 'established' (ثابت) and 'real' (متحقق).
3
Analyze the refutation of the anticipated reply
The objector anticipates the reply that the non-existent is a 'thing' (شىء). The response distinguishes between essential quiddities and concrete individuals with accidents, arguing that the latter are not established in non-existence.
This shows the objection's sophistication.
4
See how Imam Razi refutes the objection
Imam Razi refutes the objection by showing that humans know future events like the sunrise, which are currently non-existent. This demonstrates that knowledge of non-existents is possible.
This is a counterexample (manqūḍ) that undermines the universal claim of the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : كل ما كان معلوما فهو متميز عن غيره وكل ما له تميز وتخصص وتعين فهو ثابت متحقق وما لا يكون ثابتا ولا متعينا ولا متحققا وجب أن لا يكون معلوماً، وهذه الأشخاص وصفاتها وأحوالها كانت نفيًا محضًا وعدمًا صرفاً قبل دخولها في الوجود فوجب أن لا تكون معلومة. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : المعدوم شيء وذات فلا جرم لم يمتنع كونها معلومة ؟ لأنا نقول : القول بأن المعدوم شيء هو باطل، وبتقدير تسليمه فالثابت في العدم إنما هي الذوات والحقائق والماهيات أما الذوات ينعت كونها مركبة مؤلفة موصوفة بالأعراض، فغير ثابتة في العدم بالاتفاق، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن لا تكون هذه الأشخاص والأحوال معلومة قبل تحققها. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أنه منقوض بأن كل واحد منا يعلم أن الشمس غدًا تطلع من مشرقها لا من مغربها، وهذا المعدوم معلوم.
The second doubt: Everything that is known is distinguished from other things, and everything that has distinction, specification, and determination is established and real, and whatever is not established, specified, or real must not be known. These individuals and their attributes and states were pure negation and pure non-existence before their entry into existence, so they must not be known. One might say: why is it not permissible to say that the non-existent is a thing and an essence, so it is not impossible for it to be known? We say: the statement that the non-existent is a thing is false, and if granted, what is established in non-existence are only essences, realities, and quiddities, but essences characterized by being composed, constituted, and described by accidents are not established in non-existence by consensus. If that is the case, then these individuals and states must not be known before their actualization. The answer to the second doubt: it is refuted (manqūḍ) by the fact that each one of us knows that the sun will rise tomorrow from its east, not from its west, and this non-existent is known.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is from an earlier set of objections about God's knowledge of particulars before their existence. The objection claims that knowledge requires the known object to be real and determinate, but non-existent particulars are purely negative and lack reality, so they cannot be known. The objector anticipates a reply that the non-existent is something (shay'), and refutes it by distinguishing between essential quiddities and concrete individuals with accidental attributes. The answer given by Imam Razi refutes the objection by counterexample: we ourselves know that the sun will rise tomorrow, which is currently non-existent, yet we have knowledge of it. Thus, knowledge of non-existents is possible.

Key Terms
معلوم
معدوم
ثابت
متحقق
شخص
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is simply about whether non-existents are things (shay').
Correction: The objection is more nuanced: it argues that knowledge requires determination and reality, and non-existents lack both. The answer shifts to showing that we do have knowledge of future non-existents, bypassing the ontological classification.

Checkpoint

A) By proving that non-existents are actually real
B) By showing that humans know future events which are non-existent
C) By denying that God knows particulars
D) By accepting the objection but limiting it to created beings

Key Takeaway
The objection that non-existent particulars cannot be known is refuted by the fact that humans themselves have knowledge of future events, proving that knowledge of non-existents is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Imam Razi uses human knowledge of the future as a self-evident counterexample. When you make claims about what God can or cannot know, are you willing to test those claims against what you yourself acknowledge to be true? How would your life change if you applied this same logical standard to your own arguments about God?
?
The objection insists that only what is 'distinguished and real' can be known. But you count on many things that are not yet real: promises, fears, hopes. Third Objection: Infinite Future Acts and Finite Reward
Imam Razi presents and answers the objection that infinite future motions would necessitate finite reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that God's knowledge of infinite future motions would imply finite reward and punishment, and the answer that God knows things as they truly are.

Core Idea
God knows the infinite as infinite, the finite as finite.

Imam Razi addresses a subtle objection about divine omniscience and the eternity of reward. The objection argues that if God knows all future actions, then He must know their number, which would be finite—contradicting the eternal reward of Paradise. The answer clarifies how God's knowledge matches the reality of what is known.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical progression: if God knows all future, He knows the number; known number implies finitude; thus reward and punishment would be finite, which is impossible.
The objection assumes that a known number must be finite.
2
Identify the problematic conclusion
The objector concludes that God does not know future contingents until they occur. See how this conclusion follows from the premises.
3
Examine the answer
The answer states that God knows things as they are: if the number is finite, He knows it as finite; if infinite, He knows it as infinite. This resolves the problem because the actual number of motions may be unlimited.
The answer does not imply God's knowledge changes; it simply corresponds to reality.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this answer defends divine omniscience without compromising the eternity of reward and punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لو كان عالما بكل ما سيدخل في الوجود، لكان عالما بعدد ما يدخل في الوجود من حركات أهل الجنة وأهل النار وكل ما كان عدده معلومًا كان متناهيا فيلزم إثبات النهاية لثواب أهل الجنة ولعقاب أهل النار، وذلك محال، فعلمنا أنه تعالى لا يعلم هذه المتغيرات إلا عند وقوعها. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة: أنه تعالى إنما يعلم الشيء كما هو، فإن كان له علمه كذلك، وإن كان له عدد غير محصور كان علمه كذلك.
The third doubt: If He were knowing of everything that will enter into existence, He would know the number of motions that enter into existence of the people of Paradise and the people of Hell, and everything whose number is known is finite. This would necessitate establishing finitude for the reward of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hell, and that is impossible. Thus, we know that He, exalted, does not know these changing things except at their occurrence. The answer to the third doubt: He, exalted, only knows the thing as it is. If it has a finite number, His knowledge is such; if it has an unlimited number, His knowledge is such.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection from the perspective of future contingents. The objector argues that if God knew all future events, He would know the total number of actions of the inhabitants of Paradise and Hell. Since a known number must be finite, it would imply that reward and punishment are finite, contradicting their eternity in Islamic theology. Therefore, God must not know them until they occur. The answer is succinct: God knows things as they truly are. If a thing is finite in number, He knows it as finite; if it is infinite, He knows it as infinite. Thus, the problem of finitude does not arise because the actual number may be infinite in principle, and God knows that infinity as it is.

متناه
ثواب
عقاب
متغيرات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge changes over time because He knows future events only when they occur.
Correction: The answer says God knows things as they are; He does not change, but His knowledge matches the reality of each thing, whether finite or infinite.

Checkpoint

B) As they are, whether finite or infinite

God's knowledge perfectly corresponds to the nature of what is known, whether finite or infinite.

First Objection: Infinite Knowledge Implies Infinite Sciences in God's Essence
Imam Razi presents and answers the objection that infinite known things would entail infinite sciences in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that infinite known things would entail infinite sciences in God is invalid, and how the answer uses the distinction between a single attribute and its infinite relations.

Core Idea
Infinite objects do not imply infinite attributes; a single knowledge can have infinite relations.

This lesson examines the first objection raised against God's knowledge of infinite things. The objection argues that if God knows everything, including an infinite number of things, then His knowledge must be multiple and thus infinite, which is impossible. The response clarifies that God's knowledge is a single attribute whose relational attachments are infinite, and infinity in relations is not problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to how the objector argues that infinite known things lead to infinite sciences in God.
Notice the alternative formulation using 'ālimiyya and why it still leads to the same problem.
2
Understand the argument's core
The objection claims that a number that can increase or decrease must be finite, so infinite sciences are impossible. Grasp why this is presented as a problem.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer asserts that God's knowledge is one, but its attachments (taʿalluqāt) are infinite. These attachments are relations and additions, and infinity in relations is not impossible.
Reflect on the example of the number one having infinite relations (half of two, third of three, etc.).
4
Summarize the resolution
The key is that multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity of the attribute itself. God's knowledge remains one while encompassing infinite objects.
This resolves the objection without compromising God's omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى : أنا لما بينا أنه يجب تعدد العلم بتعدد المعلومات فلو كانت المعلومات غير متناهية لحصل في ذات الله تعالى علوم غير متناهية . ولو أن قائلا يقول : إنا لا نثبت العلم لله تعالى بل نثبت العالمية كان هذا نزاعا في العبارة، ويلزمه أن يثبت له تعالى عالميات لا نهاية لها ؛ لأنه لا يمكننا أن نعلم كونه تعالى عالمًا بأحد هذين المعلومين حال شكنا في كونه تعالى عالمًا بالمعلوم الآخر والمعلوم غير المشكوك. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كان الله تعالى عالمًا بما لا نهاية له لزم أن يحصل في ذاته علوم غير متناهية أو عالميات غير متناهية وذلك محال؛ لأن كل عدد يوجد فإنه قابل للزيادة والنقصان وكل ما كان كذلك وجب أن يكون متناهيا . والجواب عن الأولى : أن علم الله تعالى لِمَ لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه واحد، وإنما تعلقاته غير متناهية، وهذه التعلقات نسب وإضافات ودخول اللانهاية في النسب والإضافات غير ممتنع، بدليل ما ذكرناه أن الواحد نصف الاثنين وثلث الثلاثة، وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له ؟
The first doubt: Since we have shown that knowledge must be multiple with the multiplicity of known things, if the known things were infinite, then infinite sciences would occur in God's essence. And if someone says: we do not posit knowledge for God, but rather we posit being-knowing (al-ʿālimiyya), this is a dispute in wording, and it would require that He has infinite being-knowings; because we cannot know that He is knowing of one of these two known things while we doubt that He is knowing of the other known thing, and the unknown is not doubted. Once this is established, we say: if God were knowing of what has no end, then infinite sciences or infinite being-knowings would occur in His essence, and that is impossible, because every number that exists is capable of increase and decrease, and everything that is so must be finite. The answer to the first: why is it not permissible to say that God's knowledge is one, but its attachments (taʿalluqāt) are infinite, and these attachments are relations and additions, and the entry of infinity into relations and additions is not impossible, as indicated by our statement that the one is half of two and a third of three, and so on to infinity?
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection against God's knowledge of infinite things. The objector argues from the principle that knowledge is multiplied by the multiplicity of objects known. If there are infinite known things, then there must be infinite distinct sciences in God's essence, which is absurd. The objector also considers an alternative view that instead of 'knowledge' (ʿilm), one might posit 'being-knowing' (ʿālimiyya), but the same problem applies: infinite attributes. The answer refutes this by stating that God's knowledge is a single attribute, but its relations (taʿalluqāt) to objects are infinite. Infinity in relations is not impossible, as illustrated by the example of the number one being half of two, a third of three, and so on, thus demonstrating that multiplicity of relations does not necessitate multiplicity of the attribute itself.

Key Terms
علم
معلومات
عالم
عالمية
تعلق
نسب
إضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God knows infinite objects, then His knowledge must be infinite in number.
Correction: God's knowledge is a single attribute with infinite relational attachments; infinity of relations does not imply infinity of the attribute itself.

Checkpoint

A) God's knowledge is multiple like the objects
B) God's knowledge is one with infinite relations
C) God does not know infinite things
D) The objection is invalid because numbers are not relevant

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is one, but its relational attachments to infinite objects are infinite; this is not impossible.

Second Objection: Every Known Thing is Finite
Imam Razi presents and answers the objection that whatever is known must be finite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi answers the objection that infinite things cannot be known.

Core Idea
Infinite things are known as infinite, not as finite individuals.

In this passage, Imam Razi tackles a powerful objection: if knowledge requires distinctness and distinction implies finitude, then infinite things are unknowable. Razi's response is nuanced, distinguishing between knowledge of each individual finite part and knowledge of the infinite whole. The passage also features a counter-objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the opponent's claim: every known thing is distinct, distinct things are finite, so infinite things cannot be known.
Notice the logical chain: known -> distinct -> finite.
2
Examine the first part of the answer
Razi splits the objection: apply it to individuals (each is finite, so fine) or to the totality (no totality for infinite).
The second part is key: infinite things have no sum or whole.
3
Understand the counter-objection
The opponent insists that even without a totality, each individual known is still distinct and finite, so how can infinite be known?
This strengthens the original objection.
4
Grasp the final answer
Razi replies: it is known insofar as it is infinite, not insofar as it is a finite individual. This preserves both knowledge and infinity.
This is the critical resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : قالوا : كل معلوم فهو متميز عن غيره، وكل متميز عن غيره، فهو متناه؛ لأن المتميز هو الذي ينفصل عن غيره بحده وطرفه، فإذن كل ما كان معلومًا فهو متناه، وما لا يكون متناهيا يمتنع أن يكون معلوما. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن هذه الشبهة إما أن نوردها في كل واحد آحاد من المعلومات، أو في مجموعها، والأول باطل؛ لأن كل واحد من آحاد المعلومات متناه، والثاني باطل ؛ لأن هذا الكلام إنما يتجه لو كان للمعلومات التي لا نهاية لها مجموع وجملة، وذلك محال ؛ لأن المجموع والجملة يشعران بالتناهي، ووصف ما لا نهاية له بكونه مجموعا وجملة محال. لا يقال : هذا الذي ذكرتموه مما يؤكد السؤال، وذلك لأن كل ما كان معلوما فهو شيء مشار إليه بحسب إشارة العقل فله خصوصية وتعين وتميز، وكل ما كان كذلك فهو متناه، فإذن كل معلوم فهو متناه، وما لا يكون متناهيا لا يكون معلوما. لأنا نقول : إنه معلوم من حيث إنه غير متناه، وكونه معلومًا من هذا الاعتبار لا ينافي كونه غير متناه.
The second doubt: They said: every known thing is distinguished from others, and everything distinguished from others is finite, because the distinguished is that which is separated from others by its boundary and edge. Therefore, every known thing is finite, and whatever is not finite cannot be known. The answer to the second doubt: this doubt is either applied to each individual of the known things, or to their totality. The first is false because each individual of the known things is finite. The second is false because this statement would only apply if there were for the infinite known things a sum and a whole, and that is impossible, because sum and whole indicate finitude, and describing what is infinite as having a sum and a whole is impossible. One might say: what you have mentioned only strengthens the objection, because every known thing is something pointed to by intellectual indication, so it has specificity, determination, and distinction, and everything that is so is finite, so every known thing is finite, and what is not finite cannot be known. We say: it is known insofar as it is infinite, and its being known from this perspective does not contradict its being infinite.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage contains the second objection and its detailed answer. The objectors argue that knowledge requires distinctness, and distinctness implies finitude, so infinite things cannot be known. The answer is twofold: if we consider each individual known thing, each is indeed finite, so no problem. If we consider the infinite totality, there is no totality to speak of, because totality implies finitude. Thus, the objection fails. The passage also includes an embedded counter-objection (لا يقال) that even if there is no totality, each individual known thing is still distinguished and thus finite, so how can infinite things be known? The answer is that they are known from the aspect of being infinite, not from the aspect of being finite individuals. This nuanced response preserves both the finitude of each individual and the infinite extent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answer concedes that God does not know individuals because they are finite.
Correction: The answer affirms knowledge of each individual as finite, and also knowledge of the infinite as infinite.

Checkpoint
How does the answer respond to the claim that every known thing must be finite?
A) By denying that known things are distinct
B) By asserting that God's knowledge is beyond distinctions
C) By showing that the objection can be applied either to individuals or to the whole, and both fail
D) By arguing that infinite things are not known at all

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is between knowing each finite part and knowing the infinite whole; the infinite is known as infinite, not as a finite collection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah's knowledge of you, do you secretly assume He knows you only in a limited, manageable way? This lesson teaches that His knowledge encompasses your infinite complexity—not as a checklist but as a single, unencompassed reality. What fear or comfort does that bring?
?
The objection argued that to be known is to be finite. Have you ever tried to reduce God to what you can fully understand, effectively making Him finite in your mind? Confess a specific way you have limited your concept of Allah and commit to letting the truth of His infinite knowledge reshape that area.
?
The final answer says the infinite is known precisely as infinite—not by being broken down. Are you willing to be known by Allah in your entirety, including parts you hide from yourself? What is one thing you need to stop pretending is outside His knowledge, and how will you live differently today because He knows it?

Third Objection: Power vs. Knowledge
Imam Razi presents and answers the third objection about divine power and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the objection that God's knowledge must be finite because His power is limited to the possible.

Core Idea
Knowledge and power have different domains, not a quantitative relationship.

This lesson presents the third objection against the finitude of divine knowledge, based on an analogy with power. The objector argues that since God's power is finite, and His knowledge encompasses more, knowledge must also be finite. Imam Razi's answer clarifies that the comparison is not quantitative but categorical: knowledge covers the necessary, impossible, and possible, while power only covers the possible. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the nature of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical steps in the objection: maqdūrāt are less than maʿlūmāt, less than finite is finite, so maqdūrāt finite, and maʿlūmāt are multiples of maqdūrāt so also finite.
The objection seems plausible at first glance.
2
Identify the hidden assumption
The objection assumes that 'less than' implies a quantitative comparison in number. Ask yourself: is this necessarily true?
3
Study the answer: domain distinction
Read the answer: knowledge relates to the necessary, impossible, and possible, while power only to the possible. This shows the comparison is about domains, not sizes. The finitude of one domain does not entail finitude of the other.
This is a categorical, not quantitative, distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : مقدورات الله تعالى أقل من معلوماته والأقل من غيره متناه، فمقدوراته متناهية ومعلوماته أضعاف مقدوراته، وأضعاف المتناهي متناه، فمعلوماته متناهية. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أن قولنا : المقدورات أقل من المعلومات هو أن العلم يتعلق بالواجب والممتنع والجائز، والقدرة لا تعلق لها إلا بالجائزات.
The third doubt: The things God is capable of (maqdūrāt) are less than His known things (maʿlūmāt), and less than something finite is finite, so His capable things are finite, and His known things are multiples of His capable things, and multiples of the finite are finite, so His known things are finite. The answer to the third doubt: our statement that the capable things are less than the known things is because knowledge relates to the necessary, the impossible, and the possible, while power relates only to possible things.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection from an analogy between God's power and His knowledge. The objector argues that since what God can do (maqdūrāt) is a subset of what He knows (maʿlūmāt)—because He knows everything including impossibilities and necessary truths, but He only has power over possibles—therefore the set of known things is larger than the set of possible things. If the set of possible things is finite (as argued elsewhere), then the larger set must also be finite. The answer refutes this by redefining the premise: it is not that 'power's objects are less in number than knowledge's objects', but rather that knowledge encompasses a wider domain (the necessary, impossible, and possible) while power only pertains to the possible. This does not imply finitude of knowledge; it simply distinguishes their scopes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That because God's power is finite, His knowledge must also be finite.
Correction: The answer shows knowledge and power pertain to different categories of objects; finitude of one does not imply finitude of the other.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi reject the objection based on power?
A) Because God's power is infinite
B) Because knowledge and power have different objects, not a quantitative relation
C) Because the objector misunderstood the definition of power
D) Because finite power does not imply finite knowledge

Key Takeaway
The objection fails because it mistakenly treats the domain of knowledge as a quantitative superset of power, whereas they are categorically distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you feel limited in what you can do, do you remember that God's power is only limited to possible things? How does that shape your understanding of reliance on Him? Are you willing to accept that some things are beyond your power but not beyond His knowledge?
?
If God knows all impossibilities, what does that say about your own impossibilities? Are you willing to accept that some things are impossible for you but possible for God? How does that challenge your pride? Will you actively remind yourself of this distinction when you face obstacles?

Proof that infinite knowledges in God are impossible
Proof that infinite knowledges in God are impossible using finitude of numbers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that infinite knowledges in God are impossible based on the finitude of numbers.

Core Idea
Every number can be increased or decreased; therefore, any actual number is finite.

Imam Razi now presents a proof that God cannot have infinite knowledge. He uses a logical argument based on the nature of numbers. This lesson will walk you through that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the key terms: عالميات (knowns) and متناهي (finite).
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument is a conditional: if infinite knowledge, then infinite knowledges/knowns. Then it denies the consequent because every number is finite.
3
Understand the key premise
The author asserts that every existing number is capable of increase and decrease. Reflect on why that implies finitude.
This is a common argument in kalām against actual infinities.
4
Consider the conclusion
Since an infinite number is impossible, the initial assumption of infinite knowledge is false. This proves that God's knowledge is finite in number, though still vast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لو كان الله تعالى عالمًا بما لا نهاية له لزم أن يحصل في ذاته علوم غير متناهية أو عالميات غير متناهية وذلك محال؛ لأن كل عدد يوجد فإنه قابل للزيادة والنقصان وكل ما كان كذلك وجب أن يكون متناهيا .

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the claim that God knows an infinite number of things. It argues that if God knew an infinite number of things, then there would be an infinite number of knowledges or knowns in His essence. This is impossible because any existing number can be increased or decreased, implying it must be finite. Therefore, an actual infinite cannot exist in God's essence. The proof relies on the property of numbers: every number is finite because it can be incremented or decremented.

The Objection of Finitude from the Inequality of Power and Knowledge
Third objection and its refutation regarding divine power and knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that God's knowledge must be finite because His power is less than His knowledge is invalid, and how Imam Razi refutes it by distinguishing the domains of knowledge and power.

Core Idea
God's knowledge includes the impossible and necessary, which power cannot address, so inequality doesn't imply finitude.

A common objection against divine attributes is that if one attribute is quantitatively less than another, then the greater attribute must be finite. In this passage, Imam Razi presents such an objection regarding God's power and knowledge, then offers a clear refutation. By examining the categories each attribute applies to, Razi shows that the argument fails because the domains are not parallel.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow: from 'power less than knowledge' to 'knowledge finite'.
The objection uses the principle that a multiple of the finite is finite.
2
Identify the three categories of knowledge
In the answer, Razi lists three categories that knowledge covers. Find them in the text and note their names: necessary (واجب), impossible (ممتنع), possible (جائز).
3
Understand the refutation
Razi explains that power only applies to the possible (جائز), while knowledge applies to all three. Thus, the inequality does not prove finitude because the sets are different.
The objection mistakenly assumes power and knowledge have the same scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The answer to the third doubt: Our statement that the objects of power are less than the objects of knowledge is because knowledge pertains to the necessary, the impossible, and the possible, while power pertains only to the possible.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection argues that since the objects of God's power (what He can do) are fewer than the objects of His knowledge (what He knows), and since what is fewer than something finite is itself finite, then God's knowledge must be finite. The answer clarifies the reason for the inequality: knowledge encompasses three categories (necessary, impossible, possible), whereas power only applies to the possible. Thus, the comparison does not prove finitude because the domains are not parallel; knowledge includes things power cannot address.

Two Proofs Against God's Omniscience and Partial Answer
Two proofs denying God's omniscience and partial answer to the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two philosophical arguments against God's knowledge of all things and the beginning of the classical response that God's knowledge is one.

Core Idea
Infinite regress arguments are powerful but fail because God's knowledge is singular and not self-referential.

This passage presents two proofs used by those who deny that God knows all things. The first proof argues that omniscience leads to an infinite regress of self-knowledge. The second proof argues that knowing a single atom would require infinite knowledge of its potential states. The response to the first proof begins with the assertion that God's knowledge is one, though its relations are multiple.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Proofs
Read the passage and locate the two distinct proofs (wujuh) against God knowing all things. The first is about infinite regress of knowledge, the second about the indivisible atom.
The first proof occupies most of the passage; the second is shorter.
2
Analyse the First Proof and Its Refutation of an Objection
The first proof argues that knowing each thing requires knowing that one knows it, ad infinitum. Then the author refutes a possible objection that knowledge of knowledge is identical to knowledge itself, giving three reasons.
The three reasons are numbered in the commentary.
3
Examine the Second Proof
The second proof considers an indivisible atom (jawhar fard) and its infinite possible locations, times, and accidents, arguing that knowing it would require infinite knowledge at once, which is impossible.
This proof relies on the concept of potential infinite states.
4
Note the Partial Answer
The passage begins the answer to the first proof by stating that God's knowledge is one, but the ranks of its relations are multiple. This answer is incomplete in this chunk.
The answer continues on the next page.

النوع السادس من المخالفين الذين ينكرون كونه تعالى عالمًا بجميع المعلومات واحتجوا عليه بوجهين : الأول : لو كان عالما بجميع المعلومات لكان إذا علم شيئاً، علم كونه عالما به وعلم كونه تعالى عالما بكونه عالماً، وهكذا في المرتبة الثالثة والرابعة إلى ما لا نهاية له، فيكون له بحسب كل واحد من المعلومات علوم غير متناهية؛ لأنها أمور مترتبة لأن المرتبة الثالثة مرتبة على الثانية والثانية على الأولى، فإذا حصلت ههنا مراتب غير متناهية لزم حصول أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة، وذلك مما ظهر إبطاله في مسألة إثبات العلم بواجب الوجود. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إثبات العلم بالعالم بالشيء هو نفس العلم بذلك الشيء ؟ لأنا نقول : هذا باطل من وجوه أحدها: أن المعلوم والعلم مما يتغايران، فوجب أن يكون العلم بالمعلوم مغايرا للعلم بالعلم بذلك المعلوم. وثانيها : أنه لو كان العلم بالعلم بالشيء نفس العلم بذلك الشيء لكان مَنْ علم شيئًا حضر في ذهنه العلم بالعلم والعلم بالعلم به وهكذا هذه المراتب الغير متناهية، ولما علمنا بالضرورة أنه ليس كل من علم الشيء حصر في ذهنه هذه المراتب الغير متناهية علمنا أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء هو مغاير للعلم بذلك الشيء. وثالثها : أنه يمكننا أن نعلمه عالما بذلك الشيء، وإن كنا لا نعلمه عالما بكونه عالما بذلك الشيء، والمعلوم مغاير للمشكوك. فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء يمتنع أن يكون نفس العلم بذلك الشيء. الوجه الثاني : أنه لو كان عالما بجميع المعلومات سواء كانت واقعة أو ممكنة الوقوع، فإذا علم الله تعالى جوهرا فردًا فذلك الجوهر الفرد يمكن وقوعه في أحياز غير متناهية على البدل، وفي أزمنة غير متناهية على البدل وموصوفة من كل نوع من أنواع الأعراض بأفراد لا نهاية لها على البدل فهذه المراتب لا نهاية لها، لا مرة واحدة بل مرارًا لا نهاية لها، وكل ذلك محال في جوهر فرد، وجزء لا يتجزأ، ومعلوم أن استحضار العلم المنفصل بهذه المراتب دفعة واحدة مما لا يقبله العقل. والجواب عن الوجه الأول: أن علم الله تعالى واحد، إلا أن مراتب تعلقاته
The sixth type of opponents are those who deny that He Almighty is knowledgeable of all known things. They argued with two proofs: The first: If He were knowledgeable of all known things, then when He knew something, He would know that He knows it, and He would know that He knows that He knows it, and so on in the third and fourth rank ad infinitum. Thus, for each individual known thing, there would be infinite knowledges because they are ordered things, since the third rank is dependent on the second and the second on the first. If infinite ranks occur here, then there would be an occurrence of infinite causes and effects at once, and that has been shown to be impossible in the proof of knowledge of the Necessary Being. It cannot be said: Why is it not permissible to say that the knowledge of knowing a thing is the same as the knowledge of that thing? We say: This is false on several counts: First, the known and knowledge are different, so knowledge of the known must be different from knowledge of the knowledge of that known. Second, if knowledge of knowledge of a thing were the same as knowledge of that thing, then whoever knows something would have in his mind knowledge of knowledge and knowledge of knowledge of it, and so on infinitely. Since we know necessarily that not everyone who knows something has these infinite ranks in his mind, we know that knowledge of knowledge of a thing is different from knowledge of that thing. Third, we can know that He knows that thing without knowing that He knows that He knows that thing, and the known is different from the unknown. Thus, it is established by these proofs that knowledge of knowledge of a thing cannot be the same as knowledge of that thing. The second proof: If He were knowledgeable of all known things, whether actual or possible, then if God knows an indivisible atom, that atom can occur in infinite locations alternately, in infinite times alternately, and be described with infinite individuals of each kind of accidents alternately. These ranks are infinite, not once but an infinite number of times. All that is impossible for a single indivisible atom. It is known that the mind's presenting separate knowledge of these ranks at once is unacceptable to reason. And the answer to the first proof: That God's knowledge is one, but the ranks of its relations...
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs (wujuh) used by opponents who deny that God knows all things. The first proof argues that if God knows everything, then for each thing He knows, He must also know that He knows it, and so on infinitely, leading to an infinite regress of knowledges occurring simultaneously, which is impossible. The author then refutes a potential objection that knowledge of knowledge is identical to knowledge itself, giving three reasons: (1) the known and knowledge are distinct, so knowledge of the known is different from knowledge of that knowledge; (2) if identical, then anyone who knows something would have infinite knowledges in mind, which is not the case; (3) we can know something without knowing that we know it. The second proof argues that a single indivisible atom (jawhar fard) can be associated with infinite possible locations, times, and accidents, so knowing it would require infinite knowledge at once, which is impossible. The answer to the first proof begins but is cut off at the end of the page: it states that God's knowledge is one, but the ranks of its relations are multiple.

مراتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the infinite regress argument conclusively disproves God's omniscience.
Correction: The passage shows that the argument fails because knowledge of knowledge is distinct from knowledge of the object, and God's knowledge is one in essence, though its relations are multiple—thus infinite regress is avoided.

C) God's knowledge is limited to essential things

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress arguments against God's knowledge are refuted by distinguishing the object of knowledge from the act of knowing, and by affirming that God's knowledge is one in essence.

The Objection of Infinite Regress and Its Answer
Razi presents and refutes the first objection of infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against God's omniscience—the infinite regress of knowledge—and Razi's refutation of the counterargument and his answer that God's knowledge is one with infinite relations.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one, but its relations are infinite—infinitude in relations is not impossible.

Razi begins by presenting the first objection raised by those who deny God's all-encompassing knowledge. They argue that if God knows everything, that would entail an infinite regress of levels of knowledge, leading to an impossibility. Razi then refutes a potential counterargument and finally gives his answer, showing that infinite relations are possible within the unity of divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage where Razi states the objection: that an infinite regress of knowledge would occur if God knows all things.
2
Understand the counterargument
Notice that Razi considers a possible response: that knowing that you know is the same as the original knowledge. This is the view he will refute.
3
Analyze the three proofs
Razi provides three arguments to show that knowledge of knowledge is distinct from the original knowledge. Read each proof carefully.
These proofs are numbered: one, second, third.
4
Read the final answer
Razi answers the objection by stating that God's knowledge is one but its relations (talluqat) are infinite, and infinity in relations is not impossible.
He uses the example of a unity encompassing infinite relations.
