عالماً، وهكذا في المرتبة الثالثة والرابعة إلى ما لا نهاية له، فيكون له بحسب كل واحد من المعلومات علوم غير متناهية؛ لأنها أمور مترتبة لأن المرتبة الثالثة مرتبة على الثانية والثانية على الأولى، فإذا حصلت ههنا مراتب غير متناهية لزم حصول أسباب ومسببات لا نهاية لها دفعة واحدة، وذلك مما ظهر إبطاله في مسألة إثبات العلم بواجب الوجود. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إثبات العلم بالعالم بالشيء هو نفس العلم بذلك الشيء ؟ لأنا نقول : هذا باطل من وجوه أحدها: أن المعلوم والعلم مما يتغايران، فوجب أن يكون العلم بالمعلوم مغايرا للعلم بالعلم بذلك المعلوم. وثانيها : أنه لو كان العلم بالعلم بالشيء نفس العلم بذلك الشيء لكان مَنْ علم شيئًا حضر في ذهنه العلم بالعلم والعلم بالعلم به وهكذا هذه المراتب الغير متناهية، ولما علمنا بالضرورة أنه ليس كل من علم الشيء حصر في ذهنه هذه المراتب الغير متناهية علمنا أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء هو مغاير للعلم بذلك الشيء. وثالثها : أنه يمكننا أن نعلمه عالما بذلك الشيء، وإن كنا لا نعلمه عالما بكونه عالما بذلك الشيء، والمعلوم مغاير للمشكوك. فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن العلم بالعلم بالشيء يمتنع أن يكون نفس العلم بذلك الشيء. الوجه الثاني : أنه لو كان عالما بجميع المعلومات سواء كانت واقعة أو ممكنة الوقوع، فإذا علم الله تعالى جوهرا فردًا فذلك الجوهر الفرد يمكن وقوعه في أحياز غير متناهية على البدل، وفي أزمنة غير متناهية على البدل وموصوفة من كل نوع من أنواع الأعراض بأفراد لا نهاية لها على البدل فهذه المراتب لا نهاية لها، لا مرة واحدة بل مرارًا لا نهاية لها، وكل ذلك محال في جوهر فرد، وجزء لا يتجزأ، ومعلوم أن استحضار العلم المنفصل بهذه المراتب دفعة واحدة مما لا يقبله العقل. والجواب عن الوجه الأول: أن علم الله تعالى واحد، إلا أن مراتب تعلقاته غير متناهية والتعلقات من باب النسب والإضافات، ودخول ما لا نهاية له فيها غير ممتنع كما ضربنا في المثال من الوحدة المشتملة على النسب والإضافات التي لا نهاية لها. والجواب عن الوجه الثاني : أنها محض التعجب ولا عبرة بذلك في صفات الله تعالى؛ فإن كمالها وجلالها أعظم من أن يحيط به عقول البشر. وهذا ما انتهى إليه العقل الضعيف وجلال الله تعالى منزه عن غايات عقول العقلاء، ونهايات علوم العلماء، وبالله التوفيق.
The sixth type of opponents who deny that He, exalted, is knowing of all known things. They argued against it with two points: First: If He were knowing of all known things, then if He knows something, He knows that He is knowing of it, and He knows that He, exalted, is knowing that He is knowing, and so on in the third and fourth levels to infinity. So for each one of the known things He would have infinite sciences because they are ordered matters, since the third level depends on the second and the second on the first. So if there occur here infinite levels, there would necessarily occur causes and effects without end all at once, and that is something whose refutation has appeared in the issue of establishing knowledge of the Necessary Existent. It may not be said: Why is it not permissible to say that establishing knowledge of the Knower of the thing is the same as the knowledge of that thing? For we say: This is false from several aspects. One of them: That the known and the knowledge are different, so knowledge of the known must be different from knowledge of the knowledge of that known. Second: That if knowledge of the knowledge of a thing were the same as knowledge of that thing, then whoever knows something would have in his mind knowledge of knowledge and knowledge of knowledge of it, and so on these infinite levels. And since we know necessarily that not everyone who knows a thing has in his mind these infinite levels, we know that knowledge of the knowledge of a thing is different from knowledge of that thing. Third: That we can know Him to be knowing of that thing while we do not know Him to be knowing that He is knowing of that thing, and the known is different from the doubted. Thus it is established by these aspects that knowledge of the knowledge of a thing cannot be the same as knowledge of that thing. The second point: That if He were knowing of all known things, whether occurring or possible to occur, then if God knows a single atom, that single atom could occur in infinite locations alternately, and in infinite times alternately, and described from every type of accidents with infinite individuals alternately. So these levels are infinite, not once but repeatedly without end, and all of that is impossible for a single atom and an indivisible part, and it is known that bringing to mind separate knowledge of these levels all at once is something the intellect does not accept. The answer to the first point: That God's knowledge is one, except that the levels of its relations are infinite, and relations are of the category of relations and additions, and the inclusion of the infinite in them is not impossible, like the example we gave of a unity that encompasses relations and additions that have no end. The answer to the second point: That it is pure amazement, and no consideration is given to that in the attributes of God, exalted; for His perfection and majesty are too great for the minds of humans to encompass. This is what the weak intellect has reached, and the majesty of God, exalted, is free from the limits of the minds of intellectuals and the endpoints of the sciences of scholars. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections raised by a group who deny that God has all-encompassing knowledge. Razi first states the objection: if God knows all things, there would be an infinite regress of knowledge—knowing that He knows, knowing that He knows that He knows, and so on. This would lead to an infinite number of simultaneous causes and effects, which is impossible. Then Razi addresses a potential counterargument: someone might say that knowing that one knows is the same as the original knowledge. Razi refutes this with three proofs: (1) the known and knowledge are distinct, so knowledge of the known is different from knowledge of the knowledge; (2) if they were identical, everyone who knows something would have infinite levels of knowledge in mind, which is false by necessity; (3) we can know that God knows something without knowing that He knows that He knows, so the two items of knowledge are distinct. Thus the first objection stands. The second objection concerns God's knowledge of a single atom: if God knows an atom, He would have to know its infinite possible locations, times, and accidents, which is impossible for an atom. Then Razi gives the answers: (1) For the first objection, God's knowledge is one but its relations are infinite, and infinitude in relations is not impossible, like a unity with infinite relations. (2) For the second objection, the objection is mere amazement; God's attributes are beyond human comprehension. Razi ends by humbly stating the limits of human reason and submitting success to God.

Key Terms
علم
معلومات
مراتب
تعلقات
نسب
إضافات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that an infinite regress in knowledge disproves God's omniscience, because infinite causes and effects cannot exist simultaneously.
Correction: Razi shows that the regress is not a regress of separate knowledges but of relations within a single knowledge, and infinitude in relations is not impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why can't knowledge of knowledge be identical to the original knowledge?
A) Because God's knowledge is infinite.
B) Because the known and knowledge are distinct, so knowledge of one is different from knowledge of the other.
C) Because human intellect cannot grasp infinite levels.
D) Because that would imply multiple gods.

Key Takeaway
Razi resolves the infinite regress objection by distinguishing between God's unitary knowledge and its infinite relational aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God knows everything, but do you truly accept that His knowledge is utterly unlike yours—so much so that what looks like a logical impossibility to you (infinite regress) is simply a matter of relations? Are you willing to set aside your own limited reasoning and submit to a reality beyond your mental grasp?
?
When you recite 'Allahu Akbar' (God is greater), do you really mean it—including that His knowledge cannot be trapped by the logical puzzles your mind invents? What would it mean to live today as if God's knowledge is one, unified, and infinitely relational, and your own knowledge is finite and fragmented?
?
Razi shows that even the greatest scholars must find resolutions to deep theological problems. Have you ever wrestled personally with a doubt about God's attributes, or do you simply accept what you've been taught? What will you do to actively strengthen your understanding of God's perfection, starting now?

The Objection of the Atom's Infinite Possibilities and Its Answer
Razi presents and dismisses the second objection based on the atom's infinite possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against God's omniscience—that knowing an atom's infinite possible states is impossible—and Razi's answer that such arguments are based on human limitation and are mere amazement.

Core Idea
God's perfection is beyond human intellectual limits; objections based on finite reasoning are mere amazement.

The second objection concerns God's knowledge of a single atom, which could have infinite possible locations, times, and accidents. The objectors argue this is impossible. Razi dismisses this objection as pure amazement, reminding us that God's attributes are far beyond human comprehension.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about the atom
Read the second part of the passage where Razi presents the objection: that if God knows an atom, He would have to know its infinite possible locations, times, and accidents, which is impossible for a single atom.
2
Understand the reasoning
Notice that the objection is based on the idea that an atom cannot simultaneously have infinite actual states, and that the intellect cannot conceive of such knowledge.
3
Reflect on Razi's answer
Razi's answer is simple: this objection is pure amazement and should not be considered against God's attributes. He emphasizes that God's majesty is beyond human comprehension.

The sixth type of opponents who deny that He, exalted, is knowing of all known things. ?

The Definition of Will and the Mu'tazilite Position on the Drive
Razi defines will and presents the Mu'tazilite identification with the drive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite definition of will as the drive (dā'iya) and how it applies to humans and Allah.

Core Idea
Will is the drive that necessitates preference, grounded in knowledge of benefit for Allah.

Imam Razi begins the thirteenth issue by defining will as a self-evident state that determines our choices before action. He then introduces the Mu'tazilite scholars who identify this state as the 'drive' (al-dā'iya) and explain it differently for humans and Allah. This lesson unpacks that definition and the initial disagreement it sparked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the self-evident state
Read the first sentences where Razi describes the state that appears in the heart before action or abandonment. Note that he says knowledge of this state is necessary (self-evident).
This is the foundation for the whole debate.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite concept of drive
Focus on their explanation: how equal capability becomes inclined through knowledge, belief, or conjecture of benefit, and why for Allah only knowledge applies.
The drive is the composite of capability and cognitive state.
3
Preview the opposing view
Notice that some argue will is an additional state beyond the drive. Read the beginning of their first proof (the thirsty person example) as a hint of what will follow.
The proof is incomplete here, but it sets up the debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة عشرة في إثبات أن الله تعالى مريد. وهذه المسألة مشتملة على فصول : الفصل الأول : في شرح حقيقة الإرادة. اعلم أنه متى صدر عنا فعل أو ترك فقبل ذلك الفعل وذلك الترك يظهر في قلوبنا حالة تقتضي ترجيح ذلك الفعل على ذلك الترك أو بالعكس، والعلم بحصول تلك الحالة المقتضية للترجيح علم ضروري. ؟ فقال قوم من محققي المعتزلة : إنما هي الداعية. وتحقيق الكلام في الداعي أن الإنسان قادر على الفعل وعلى الترك فنسبة قدرته إلى طرفي الفعل والترك على السوية وما دامت القدرة على هذا الاستواء يمتنع حصول الرجحان ؛ لأن الاستواء والرجحان متنافيان، فإذا حصل في القلب علم أو اعتقاد أو ظن باشتمال ذلك الفعل على نفع زائد حصل الرجحان بسبب ذلك، وصار والمجموع الحاصل من تلك القدرة ومن ذلك العلم أو الظن أو الاعتقاد مؤثرًا في وقوع ذلك الفعل، وأما في حق الباري سبحانه فالاعتقاد والظن ممتنعان، فلم يبق الداعي في حق الله تعالى إلا العلم باشتمال ذلك الفعل على مصلحة راجحة. هذا هو الكلام في حقيقة الداعية. ثم قالوا : تلك الحالة المقتضية للترجيح التي نجدها من قلوبنا ليست إلا هذه الداعية. ومن الناس من قال : الميل والإرادة حالة زائدة على هذه الداعية، واحتجوا عليه بوجهين : الحجة الأولى : أن الميل قد يوجد بدون هذه الداعية، وذلك لأن العطشان إذا
The thirteenth issue: On proving that Allah the Most High is Willing. And this issue contains chapters: Chapter One: On explaining the reality of will. Know that whenever an action or abandonment issues from us, before that action and that abandonment there appears in our hearts a state that necessitates the preference of that action over that abandonment, or vice versa, and knowledge of the occurrence of that state necessitating preference is necessary knowledge. Then a group of the investigative Mu'tazila said: It is only the drive (al-dā'iya). And the precise explanation of the term 'drive' is that a human being is capable of action and of abandonment; so his capability is equally related to both sides of action and abandonment, and as long as capability is in this state of equality, preference is impossible to occur, because equality and preference are mutually exclusive. So when there occurs in the heart knowledge, belief, or conjecture that the action involves an extra benefit, preference occurs because of that, and the composite of that capability and that knowledge, conjecture, or belief becomes effective in the occurrence of that action. As for the case of the Exalted Creator, belief and conjecture are impossible, so the drive in the case of Allah the Most High is nothing but knowledge that the action involves a preponderant benefit. This is the discourse on the reality of the drive. Then they said: That state necessitating preference that we find in our hearts is nothing but this drive. And among people are those who said: Inclination (al-mayl) and will are an additional state beyond this drive, and they argued for it with two proofs: The first proof: That inclination may exist without this drive, because a thirsty person if...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage begins the thirteenth issue on proving that Allah is Willing (Murīd). It first defines will in human experience: before an action or its abandonment, a state appears in the heart that determines preference. This knowledge is necessary (self-evident). Then it introduces disagreement: the Mu'tazilite investigators identify this state as the 'drive' (al-dā'iya). The text explains the drive: humans have equal capability for action and inaction; preference occurs when knowledge, belief, or conjecture of extra benefit arises, making the composite effective. For Allah, belief and conjecture are impossible, so His drive is only knowledge of benefit. The passage then notes that others argue that will (irāda) is an additional state beyond the drive, and they give two proofs. The first proof begins here but is cut off (the thirsty person example continues on the next page). Thus, the passage sets up the definition of will and the two main positions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think will is merely a drive (dā'iya) with no additional reality.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites held that the drive itself is will, while others argue will is an additional state beyond the drive. This passage presents both views, clarifying that the debate is about whether the state of preference is identical to the drive or distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites in this passage, what is the drive (al-dā'iya) for Allah?
A) Belief in benefit
B) Conjecture of benefit
C) Knowledge of benefit
D) The capability itself

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites defined will as the drive (dā'iya), which for humans is a composite of capability and cognition of benefit, and for Allah is simply knowledge of benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a decision, do you honestly seek knowledge of what is truly beneficial, or do you follow desires and then rationalize? How does this passage’s definition of will expose the state of your heart?
?
If Allah’s will is simply His knowledge of what is best, how should that change your response when His decree seems difficult or painful? Are you able to trust that there is a preponderant benefit even when you cannot see it?
?
Consider a time you were equally capable of two choices. What tipped the scale? Was it a genuine conviction of benefit or something else? What does that reveal about your understanding of your own will?

The Definition of Will and the Mu'tazilite Theory of the Motive
Razi defines will and explains the Mu'tazilite interpretation as the motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's definition of will as a psychological state of preponderance and the Mu'tazilite identification of that state with the motive (dāʿiya), including its components in human and divine cases.

Core Idea
Will is the inner state that tips the balance; the Mu'tazilites call it the motive.

This passage opens the thirteenth issue on proving that God has will. Razi first describes will as a universal human experience: before we act or refrain, we feel an inner pull. This experience is known necessarily. He then reports the Mu'tazilite view that this pull is the 'motive'—a composite of power plus a cognitive element that makes one option preferable. For humans, the cognitive element can be knowledge, belief, or conjecture; for God, it is only knowledge. This sets the stage for Razi's later critique.

Guided Path
1
Understand the psychological description of will
Read the first part where Razi describes the state that necessitates preponderance in human actions. Note that he calls this knowledge necessary.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite position
Read the second part where Razi presents the Mu'tazilite view that this state is the motive (dāʿiya). Note their argument about the symmetry of power and how a cognitive factor breaks the symmetry.
3
Compare human and divine cases
Notice how the motive works differently for humans and God. For humans, the cognitive element can be knowledge, belief, or conjecture. For God, only knowledge is possible because belief and conjecture are impossible for Him.
4
Summarize the key terms
Make sure you understand the Arabic terms: إرادة, داعية, ترجيح, قدرة, and the three cognitive elements: علم, ظن, اعتقاد.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة عشرة في إثبات أن الله تعالى مريد وهذه المسألة مشتملة على فصول : الفصل الأول : في شرح حقيقة الإرادة اعلم أنه متى صدر عنا فعل أو ترك فقبل ذلك الفعل وذلك الترك يظهر في قلوبنا حالة تقتضي ترجيح ذلك الفعل على ذلك الترك أو بالعكس، والعلم بحصول تلك الحالة المقتضية للترجيح علم ضروري. ثم اختلف العقلاء في أن تلك الحالة المقتضية للترجيح ما هي فقال قوم من محققي المعتزلة : إنما هي الداعية. وتحقيق الكلام في الداعي أن الإنسان قادر على الفعل وعلى الترك فنسبة قدرته إلى طرفي الفعل والترك على السوية وما دامت القدرة على هذا الاستواء يمتنع حصول الرجحان ؛ لأن الاستواء والرجحان متنافيان، فإذا حصل في القلب علم أو اعتقاد أو ظن باشتمال ذلك الفعل على نفع زائد حصل الرجحان بسبب ذلك، وصار والمجموع الحاصل من تلك القدرة ومن ذلك العلم أو الظن أو الاعتقاد مؤثرًا في وقوع ذلك الفعل، وأما في حق الباري سبحانه فالاعتقاد والظن ممتنعان، فلم يبق الداعي في حق الله تعالى إلا العلم باشتمال ذلك الفعل على مصلحة راجحة. هذا هو الكلام في حقيقة الداعية.
The thirteenth issue: On proving that God Almighty is willing (murīd). This issue contains chapters: The first chapter: On explaining the reality of will (irāda). Know that whenever an act or abandonment occurs from us, before that act and that abandonment, a state appears in our hearts that necessitates the preponderance (tarjīḥ) of that act over that abandonment or vice versa, and the knowledge of the occurrence of that state necessitating preponderance is necessary knowledge (ʿilm ḍarūrī). Then the rational people differed over what that state necessitating preponderance is: a group of the verified Muʿtazilites said: It is the motive (dāʿiya). And the verification of the discussion on the motive is that a human is capable of the act and of abandonment, so his relation of power (qudra) to the two sides of act and abandonment is symmetrical. As long as power remains in this equality, the occurrence of preponderance is impossible, because equality and preponderance are mutually exclusive. So when a knowledge (ʿilm), belief (iʿtiqād), or conjecture (ẓann) arises in the heart that the act contains an extra benefit, preponderance occurs due to that, and the composite of that power and that knowledge, conjecture, or belief becomes effective in the occurrence of that act. As for the case of the Exalted Creator, belief and conjecture are impossible, so the motive in the case of God Almighty is nothing but knowledge that the act contains a preponderant benefit (maṣlaḥa rājiḥa). This is the discussion on the reality of the motive.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage begins the thirteenth issue on proving that God is willing (murīd). Razi first explains the reality of will in general. He describes a psychological state in humans: before we perform or leave an act, we experience an inner state that tilts us toward one option over the other. This state is known by necessary knowledge. Then Razi presents the difference of opinion: some rational thinkers (specifically the Muʿtazilites) say that this state is the 'motive' (dāʿiya). He then elaborates on the motive: a human has equal power over both act and abandonment, so without a factor to tip the balance, no action occurs. The factor is a cognitive element—knowledge, belief, or conjecture—that the act brings an extra benefit. This combination of power and cognition causes the action. For God, since belief and conjecture are impossible, the motive can only be His knowledge that an act contains a preponderant benefit. Razi thus sets up the Muʿtazilite position that will is reducible to this motive, which he will later critique.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazilites equate will with mere knowledge or belief, ignoring the motive concept.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites define will as the motive (dāʿiya), which is a composite of power and a cognitive element (knowledge, belief, or conjecture) that tips the balance. For God, it is knowledge alone.

Checkpoint

A) The will (irāda)
B) The motive (dāʿiya)
C) Power (qudra)
D) Knowledge (ʿilm)

Key Takeaway
Razi presents the Mu'tazilite view that will is the motive—a combination of power and a cognitive element that tips the balance, with the divine motive being only knowledge of benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi says the inner state of preponderance is known necessarily and immediately. When was the last time you ignored that inner nudge? What was the consequence, and what will you do differently tomorrow?
?
If God's motive is only based on benefit, while yours often relies on flawed beliefs or conjectures, how does that recalibrate your trust in your own will? Will you commit today to examine the basis of your strongest desires and correct them with knowledge?

Two Proofs That Will is Distinct from the Motive
Razi provides two proofs that will is distinct from motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that the inner state of preponderance (will) is not identical to the motive (dāʿiya), and the two proofs he offers from introspection and a thought experiment.

Core Idea
Will is not the same as motive: inclination can exist without a cognitive differential.

Imam Razi presents a crucial distinction in Islamic theology: the will (irāda) is not merely the motive (dāʿiya) that arises from knowledge of benefit. He provides two clear proofs—one from a thought experiment about equal options, and another from introspection—to show that the inclination within us is an additional state beyond the cognitive motive. This lesson unpacks those proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the debate
Recognize the Mu'tazilite claim that the inner state of preponderance is just the motive, and Razi's opposing view that will is an additional state.
The passage begins by stating the opposing view.
2
Examine the first proof
Analyze the thought experiment of a thirsty person choosing between two equal cups of water, showing that preponderance occurs without a cognitive differential.
Note that the inclination (will) must be distinct from the shared knowledge/conjecture.
3
Examine the second proof
Consider the introspective observation that when we know an act has extra benefit, that knowledge generates a distinct inclination or desire.
The opponent's own view that knowledge 'calls' supports the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قالوا : تلك الحالة المقتضية للترجيح التي نجدها من قلوبنا ليست إلا هذه الداعية. ومن الناس من قال : الميل والإرادة حالة زائدة على هذه الداعية، واحتجوا عليه بوجهين : الحجة الأولى : أن الميل قد يوجد بدون هذه الداعية، وذلك لأن العطشان إذا خير بين شرب قدحين متساويين من الماء فإنه لا بد وأن يرجح أحدهما على الآخر من أجل أنه لا بد وأن يحدث في قلبه ميل إلى أحد القدحين دون الثاني، وهذا الترجيح حاصل، وليس هذا الترجيح إلا عبارة عن الداعية بالتفسير الذي ذكرناه ؛ لأنه لما استوى القدحان في جميع المنافع المعلومة والمظنونة امتنع أن يكون ذلك المبل الذي هو غير مشترك فيه بينهما هو عين هذا العلم والظن الذي هو مشترك فيه بينهما. الحجة الثانية : أنا نجد من أنفسنا أنا متى علمنا أو اعتقدنا أو ظننا اشتمال الفعل على هذه المصلحة الزائدة فإنه يتولد عن ذلك العلم ميل ورغبة وترجيح ويكون ذلك الميل كالأمر اللازم لذلك الفعل وكالأمر المتولد منه، وذلك فإن الخصم يقول : إن هذا العلم يدعو الفاعل إلى الفعل، فجعل كون هذا العلم داعيًا كالأمر المتولد منه ، فثبت بهذين الوجهين أن الداعي مغاير للإرادة في حقنا.
Then they said: That state necessitating preponderance which we find in our hearts is nothing but this motive. And among people are those who said: Inclination (mayl) and will (irāda) are an additional state beyond this motive, and they argued for it with two proofs: The first proof: Inclination may exist without this motive, because if a thirsty person is given a choice between two equal cups of water, he must give preponderance to one over the other, because an inclination must arise in his heart towards one of the two cups rather than the other, and this preponderance exists, and this preponderance is only an expression of the motive according to the interpretation we mentioned; because when the two cups are equal in all known and conjectured benefits, it is impossible that that inclination which is not shared between them is the very knowledge and conjecture which is shared between them. The second proof: We find from ourselves that when we know, believe, or conjecture that the act contains this extra benefit, there arises from that knowledge an inclination, desire, and preponderance, and that inclination is like something necessary for that act and like something generated from it. And this is because the opponent says that this knowledge calls the agent to the act, so he makes being a motive of this knowledge like something generated from it. So it is established by these two proofs that the motive is different from will in our case.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs that the inner state of preponderance (namely, the will) is not identical to the motive (dāʿiya) as defined by the Muʿtazilites. The first proof uses a thought experiment: a thirsty person choosing between two equal cups of water must still have an inclination to one, even though the cognitive motive (knowledge of benefit) is identical for both cups (no extra benefit). Therefore the inclination (will) cannot be the same as that motive. The second proof argues from introspection: when we know an act has an extra benefit, this knowledge generates an inclination and desire that is distinct from the knowledge itself. The opponent (the Muʿtazilite) says the knowledge 'calls' the agent—this implies the knowledge acts as a cause, but the effect (the inclination) is different. Thus, in humans, the motive (the cognitive factor) is not the same as the will (the inclination).

إرادة
داعية
ترجيح
علم
ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the two proofs conflate different types of preponderance; some might think the first proof fails because the cups are truly equal.
Correction: The argument clarifies that in equal options, preponderance still occurs without a cognitive differential, proving that the inclination is not the same as the cognitive motive.

Checkpoint
What does the first proof about the thirsty person demonstrate?
A) That will is the same as knowledge
B) That the motive is always present
C) That inclination can exist without a cognitive motive differential
D) That God has no will

Key Takeaway
The two proofs demonstrate that the will (inclination) is an additional state beyond the cognitive motive, establishing their distinctness in human experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your will is not merely a product of your reasoning, then why do you sometimes find yourself choosing against what you know to be better? What does that reveal about the state of your heart—and what are you going to do about it today?
?
The second proof shows that knowledge of good ought to generate inclination toward it. Is there a good you know but do not desire? Ask yourself: Am I suppressing the natural inclination that should arise from knowledge? How will you reopen your heart to that inclination?
?
Razi argues that even when options are equal, your will still inclines. This means you are constantly making choices that cannot be fully justified by reason alone. Are you taking ownership of these uncaused inclinations, or are you deceiving yourself into thinking they are inevitable? What will you do to align your will with what pleases Allah?

Proving Allah's Will as an Additional Attribute
Razi presents differing views on will and proves it is an additional attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that Allah's will is a distinct attribute beyond knowledge and power, and how he refutes alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
Will is the attribute that specifies the timing of God's actions.

In this passage, Razi tackles a key question: what does it mean to say Allah is willing? After presenting the different sectarian views, he offers a philosophical proof for the Ash'ari position that will is an additional, positive attribute. The argument hinges on the need for a preponderator to explain why some things occur earlier and others later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the main views
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the three interpretations of 'will' mentioned at the beginning.
Notice that Razi starts by stating consensus on the word but disagreement on meaning.
2
Understand the problem of temporal specification
Focus on Razi's observation that some of God's actions are earlier and some later, though each could rationally have occurred at a different time. Ask why that is. This is the premise for the proof.
3
Follow Razi's elimination of power
Razi argues that power cannot be the preponderator because power is equally related to all times. Consider why bringing into existence is not enough to determine timing.
4
Follow Razi's elimination of knowledge
Next, Razi argues that knowledge cannot be the preponderator because knowledge of occurrence follows the occurrence; if occurrence depended on knowledge, circularity would result.
This is a key logical move.
5
Grasp the necessity of a distinct attribute
After ruling out power, knowledge, and other attributes like life, Razi concludes that a distinct attribute must exist whose function is preponderance and specification. That attribute is will.
Connect this to the lesson's core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في بيان أن الله تعالى مريد اتفقت الأمة على إطلاق هذا اللفظ إلا أنهم اختلفوا في معناه، فذهب حسين النجار على أن معناه أنه تعالى غير مغلوب ولا مستكره فجعل كونه تعالى مريدا وصفا سلبياً، وقال أبو القاسم البلخي معنى كونه مريدا لأفعال نفسه : أنه موجد لها، ومعنى كونه مريدًا لأفعال غيره أنه آمر بها، وقال أبو الحسين البصري : معنى كونه مريدا لأفعال غيره أنه دعاه الداعي إلى الحث عليها والترغيب في فعلها، ولعل مذهب أبي القاسم البلخي هو هذا. ومذهبنا : أن كونه تعالى مريدا صفة زائدة على كونه تعالى عالما، وهذا مذهب جمهور البصريين من المعتزلة. ولنا : أنا وجدنا بعض أفعال الله تعالى متقدمة وبعضها متأخرة مع أن ما تقدم كان يجوز في العقل أن يتأخر، وما تأخر كان يجوز في العقل أن يتقدم، وإذا كان كذلك افتقر ذلك التقدم والتأخر إلى مرجح ومخصص لامتناع حصول الرجحان لا عن مرجح، ثم نقول : ذلك المرجح إما القدرة أو العلم أو صفة أخرى لا جائز أن يكون هو القدرة، لأن خاصية القدرة الإيجاد، وذلك بالنسبة إلى جميع الأوقات على السوية، ولا جائز أن يكون هو العلم ؛ لأن العلم بالوقوع تبع للوقوع ، فلو كان هو تبعا لذلك العلم لزم الدور، فثبت أنه لا بد من شيء آخر، يكون مخصصا ومرجحًا سوى القدرة والعلم، وظاهر أن الحياة والكلام والسمع والبصر لا تصلح لذلك، ولا بد من إثبات صفة وراء هذه الصفات خاصيتها الترجيح والتخصيص، وتلك الصفة المسماة بالإرادة.
The second chapter: On proving that God Almighty is willing (murīd). The community (umma) agreed on applying this term, but they differed over its meaning. Ḥusayn al-Najjār held that its meaning is that He is not overpowered nor compelled, making His being willing a negative attribute. Abū al-Qāsim al-Balkhī said: Its meaning that He is willing regarding His own actions is that He brings them into existence, and its meaning that He is willing regarding the actions of others is that He commands them. Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī said: Its meaning that He is willing regarding the actions of others is that the motive calls Him to urge and encourage them to do it. Perhaps the doctrine of Abū al-Qāsim al-Balkhī is this. Our doctrine: That He is willing is an additional attribute beyond His being knowing, and this is the doctrine of the majority of the Basrans among the Muʿtazilites. And we have [the proof]: We find that some of God's actions are earlier and some later, while what was earlier could rationally have been later, and what was later could rationally have been earlier. This being so, that precedence and posteriority require a preponderator (murjīḥ) and specificator (mukhaṣṣiṣ), because preponderance cannot occur without a preponderator. Then we say: That preponderator is either power, knowledge, or another attribute. It cannot be power, because the property of power is bringing into existence, and that is symmetrical with respect to all times. It cannot be knowledge, because knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence; if it were a follower of that knowledge, a vicious circle would result. So it is established that there must be another thing that is a specificator and preponderator besides power and knowledge. It is obvious that life, speech, hearing, and sight are not suitable for that. So it is necessary to posit an attribute beyond these attributes, whose property is preponderance and specification, and that attribute is called will (irāda).
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is the second chapter on proving that God is willing. Razi first notes the consensus on the term but disagreement on its meaning. He summarizes three views: (1) al-Najjār takes it as a negative attribute (not being coerced). (2) al-Balkhī interprets it as bringing into existence for His own acts and commanding for others' acts. (3) al-Baṣrī says it means the motive prompting Him to urge others. Razi then states his own doctrine (shared by the majority of Basran Muʿtazilites) that will is an additional attribute beyond knowledge. He provides a proof: some of God's actions occur earlier, some later, though each could have occurred at a different time. This temporal distinction requires a preponderator. Power cannot be the preponderator because it is equally related to all times. Knowledge cannot be it because knowledge of occurrence depends on the occurrence, leading to circularity if knowledge caused the occurrence. Life, speech, hearing, and sight are unsuitable. Therefore, a distinct attribute must exist whose function is to specify and preponderate, and that attribute is will.

Key Terms
إرادة
مرجح
مخصص
قدرة
علم
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah's will is reducible to His power or knowledge, meaning that whatever He knows or is able to do automatically occurs at a specific time.
Correction: Razi's proof shows that neither power (which is symmetrical to all times) nor knowledge (which follows occurrence) can account for the timing of events. A distinct attribute, will, is necessary to specify when actions occur.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's proof, why cannot knowledge be the preponderator for the timing of God's actions?
A) Knowledge is not an attribute of God
B) Knowledge is symmetrical for all times
C) Knowledge of occurrence follows the occurrence, causing a circular argument
D) Knowledge only pertains to necessary things

Key Takeaway
Will is the attribute that preponderates and specifies the timing of God's actions, distinct from knowledge and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's will is distinct from His power? When you think about the timing of events in your life, do you attribute the exact moment to His will or just His power? How does that change how you respond to delays or unexpected times? Let this lesson convict you to trust in His will's specification, not just His ability.
?
If God's will were just His knowledge of what will happen, would that make you feel that your choices are predetermined? But Razi proves will is additional and specifies timing. How does that affect your understanding of God's sovereignty and your own responsibility? Are you willing to accept that God's will is a distinct attribute that chooses precisely when things occur? What does that mean for how you pray for timing? Are you asking for His will or just His power?
?
The proof shows that will cannot be reduced to power or knowledge. Does your mind try to reduce God's will to one of these? Why do you resist the concept of an extra attribute? The lesson calls you to affirm what the Qur'an and reason demand. Will you intellectually submit to this truth even if it humbles your desire to simplify? Or will you continue to conflate God's attributes? Examine your heart: do you really want to know God as He is, or as you prefer Him to be?

The Parity Argument: Why Will Needs a Preponderant Like Power
Razi presents the parity argument and infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that if divine power requires a preponderant (murajjih) to specify the time of creation, then divine will also requires a preponderant, leading to an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Power and will are symmetric: both need a preponderant to avoid infinite regress.

In this passage, Razi addresses an objection: why can't divine power itself be the preponderant that determines when an event occurs? He counters that will has the same kind of indifference, so if power needs something else, will does too. This sets up a crucial symmetry that exposes a flaw in the objector's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says: why can't power (qudra) be the preponderant? Razi accepts the concept of priority and posteriority but challenges this.
2
Identify the symmetry argument
Razi points out that both power and will are capable of different specifications, so if one needs a preponderant, the other does too, leading to infinite regress.
This is a parity argument.
3
Follow the complete formulation
Razi details the symmetry: power can create in this time or that, will can specify to this time or that. So the need for a preponderant is the same.
4
Conclude the first part
Thus there is no difference between the two cases; either both need a preponderant or neither does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا صحة التقدم والتأخر، ولكن لم لا يجوز أن يكون المرجح هو القدرة؟ قوله : "خاصية القدرة الإيجاد وهذا بالنسبة إلى الأوقات مما يختلف " قلنا : وكذلك خاصية الإرادة التخصيص بوقت معين لا بهذا الوقت المعين، فلو افتقرت القدرة إلى مرجح آخر لافتقرت الإرادة أيضًا إلى مرجح آخر، ولزم التسلسل. وتمام تقرير هذا السؤال : أن القدرة كما أنها صالحة للإيجاد في هذا الوقت بدلا عن ذلك الوقت، وفي ذلك الوقت بدلا عن هذا الوقت فكذا الإرادة صالحة للتخصيص بذلك الوقت بدلا عن هذا الوقت، وبهذا الوقت بدلا عن ذلك الوقت، فإن كانت تلك الصلاحية في القدرة تحوجها إلى الإرادة فهذه الصلاحية في الإرادة تحوجها إلى مخصص آخر، وإن استغنى ههنا عن المرجح فكذا هناك، فظهر أنه لا فرق بين الصورتين. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إرادة الله تعالى من شأنها تخصيص كل حادث بالوقت الذي حدث فيه، وليس لها صلاحية أن تخصص إحداث ما حدث في وقت بوقت آخر، وعلى هذا التقدير تستغني الإرادة عن مرجع آخر ؛ لأنا نقول: هذا باطل من وجوه : الأول : أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون صانع العالم فاعلا مختاراً، بل علة موجبة بالذات ؛ لأنه لما كان بحال أوجب أن يكون مؤثرًا في الإيجاد في هذا الوقت ويمتنع عقلا أن يكون موجبا له في وقت آخر ، لم يكن له اختيار البتة، بل كان موجبًا بالذات. الثاني : لو جاز هذا الكلام في الإرادة فلم لا يجوز مثله في القدرة ؟ يقال : قدرة الله لها صلاحية الإيجاد في ذلك الوقت المعين، وليس لها صلاحية الإيجاد في سائر الأوقات، وعلى هذا التقدير تستغني هذه القدرة عن الإرادة. الثالث: أن الأوقات متساوية، فلو جاز أن يقال هذا الوقت المعين له خاصية، وإرادة الله تعالى لا تصلح لتخصيص الحادث المعين إلا به، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : لكل واحد من الأوقات خاصة، والمؤثر في حدوث هذه الحوادث هو خواص هذه الأوقات؟ وعلى هذا التقدير يكون المؤثر في حدوث الحوادث هو الأوقات لا الصانع، ويلزم منه نفي الصانع.
We accept the validity of priority and posteriority, but why may not the preponderant be power? He says: 'The property of power is creation, and this varies with respect to times.' We say: Likewise, the property of will is specification to a particular time, not to this particular time. So if power needs another preponderant, then will also needs another preponderant, and infinite regress follows. The complete formulation of this question: Power is capable of creating in this time instead of that time, and in that time instead of this time; similarly, will is capable of specifying to that time instead of this time, and to this time instead of that time. If that capability in power necessitates will, then this capability in will necessitates another specificator; and if it dispenses with a preponderant here, then so there. Thus it becomes clear that there is no difference between the two cases. It is not said: Why may it not be said that the will of Allah is such that it specifies every event to the time in which it occurred, and it does not have the capability to specify the occurrence of what occurred in a time to another time; on this assumption the will dispenses with another preponderant? For we say: This is false from several aspects: First: On this assumption the Maker of the world would not be a free agent, but a self-necessitating cause; because when He is in a state that necessitates His being effective in creating in this time and it is intellectually impossible for Him to cause it in another time, He has no choice at all; rather, He is a self-necessitating cause. Second: If this statement is allowed regarding will, why is the like not allowed regarding power? It is said: God's power has the capability to create in that specific time, and it does not have the capability to create in other times; on this assumption this power dispenses with will. Third: Times are equal. So if it is allowed that this specific time has a property, and Allah's will is not suited to specify the particular event except to it, then why is it not allowed that each time has a property, and the effect in the occurrence of these events is the properties of these times? On this assumption the effect in the occurrence of events would be times, not the Maker, and it entails the negation of the Maker.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage continues the argument about the murajjih (preponderant) for temporal specification. The objector suggests that divine power (qudra) itself could be the preponderant. Razi counters that will (irada) also has the same kind of indifferent capability, so if power requires a preponderant, will would too, leading to infinite regress. He details the symmetry: both power and will can be directed to different times. Then a counter-objection is raised: maybe will is inherently specific to each event's time, so it needs no further preponderant. Razi refutes this with three arguments: (1) It would make God a necessary agent (mujib bi al-dhat), not a free chooser; (2) the same could be said of power, making will unnecessary; (3) if times have inherent properties, then times themselves would be the cause, negating the Creator.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإرادة
المرجح
التخصيص
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that divine power alone is sufficient to determine the specific time of creation, without needing will.
Correction: Razi shows that will too has indifference, so power alone cannot be the preponderant; a separate preponderant is required.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what happens if power needs a preponderant?
A) Will also needs a preponderant
B) Power does not need a preponderant
C) Infinite regress is avoided
D) Will is unnecessary

Key Takeaway
Razi's parity argument shows that the same reasoning applied to power applies to will, undermining the claim that power alone suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your own ability (power) to do something is enough to decide the right time, without seeking God's will? How does Razi's argument about the symmetry between power and will remind you that both are subject to God's decree? Will you pause before your next major decision to explicitly acknowledge that God is the preponderant over both your capability and your choice?
?
In your daily plans, do you treat your own will as sufficient, forgetting that both power and will need a preponderant from God? Reflect on a recent decision where you relied solely on your own capability without acknowledging God's specification. How can you cultivate a habit of attributing both your power and your will to Allah?
?
If infinite regress is absurd, then our actions must trace back to a preponderant. When you act, do you consciously trace that action back to God's will and power, or do you live as if your power and will are self-sufficient? What concrete practice can you adopt today to remind yourself of your utter dependence on Allah for both the ability and the specific choice?

Three Refutations: Why Will Cannot Be Inherently Specific
Razi provides three refutations of the counter-objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three arguments against the claim that divine will is inherently specific to each event's time without needing another preponderant.

Core Idea
If will is inherently specific, God becomes a necessary cause, not a free agent.

After establishing the parity, Razi considers a counter-objection: perhaps will is inherently tied to each particular time. He refutes this with three sharp arguments. The first shows it undermines divine free choice, the second extends the same logic to power, and the third leads to a denial of the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the counter-objection
The objector says will is inherently specific; it can only specify each event to its actual time, so no need for another preponderant.
2
First refutation: Free will vs. necessity
If will cannot specify otherwise, God would be a necessary cause (mujib bi al-dhat), not a free chooser.
This is a key theological point.
3
Second refutation: Symmetry with power
If will is inherently specific, why not say the same for power? That would make will unnecessary.
4
Third refutation: Times as causes
If times have special properties that determine specificity, then times themselves would be the cause, negating the Creator.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why Knowledge Cannot Be the Preponderant
Razi refutes that knowledge could be the preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that divine knowledge of an act's goodness, not its occurrence, serves as the ground for preference.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of an act's goodness, not its occurrence, tilts the balance.

This passage tackles a key objection: if power cannot be the preponderant, why not knowledge? Razi responds by distinguishing between knowledge of the act's occurrence and knowledge of its moral qualities. Only the latter, he argues, can be a cause for preference without circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and concession
Begin by reading the opening lines where Razi concedes that power cannot be the preponderant but questions why knowledge cannot.
Notice the structure: concession then new objection.
2
Understand the crucial distinction
Razi refutes by distinguishing between 'knowledge of occurrence' (which follows the act) and 'knowledge of the act's goodness and freedom from evil' (which precedes preference).
This is the key move: knowledge of goodness is not knowledge of occurrence.
3
See how the objection is rebutted
Once the distinction is accepted, the objection collapses because the preponderant is not knowledge of occurrence but knowledge of moral qualities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أنه لا يجوز أن يكون المرجح هو القدرة، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المرجح هو العلم ؟ قوله : "العلم بالوقوع تبع للوقوع، فلا يجوز أن يكون مؤثراً في الوقوع" قلنا : نحن لا نقول : المؤثر في الوقوع هو العلم بالوقوع بل تقول : علم الله تعالى باشتمال هذا الفعل على الإحسان إلى الغير مع كونه عاريًا عن جميع القبح، سبب لرجحان الفعل على الترك، وعلى هذا التقدير يندفع ما ذكرتم. جواب السؤال الأول : أن الزمان وإن كان محدثًا لكن لنفرض أن من أول حدوث الزمان إلى هذا اليوم دار فلك الثوابت مائة ألف مرة وكان يجوز في العقل أن يوجد العالم بحيث يكون من أول حدوث الزمان إلى هذا اليوم قد دار فلك الثوابت مائة وخمسين ألف مرة ويجوز أيضًا بحيث يكون من أول الحدوث إلى هذا اليوم دار فلك الثوابت خمسين ألف مرة، فهذا هو المراد من التقدم والتأخر، وإذا تلخص هذا ظهر الاحتياج إلى المخصص والمرجح. وجواب السؤال الثاني : أن المفهوم من التخصيص غير المفهوم من التكوين وإذا اختلف المفهومان وتغاير الاعتباران سمينا مفهوم مبدأ التخصيص بالإرادة، وسمينا مفهوم مبدأ الإيجاد بالقدرة.
We accept that power cannot be the preponderant, so why may not knowledge be the preponderant? He says: 'Knowledge of occurrence is consequent upon occurrence, so it cannot be effective in occurrence.' We say: We do not say that the effective in occurrence is knowledge of occurrence; rather, we say: Allah's knowledge that this act contains beneficence to others while being free from all evil is a cause for the preference of the act over omission, and on this assumption your objection is rebutted. The answer to the first question: Time, although originated, but let us suppose that from the first origination of time until today the sphere of the fixed stars has revolved one hundred thousand times, and it is intellectually possible that the world exists such that from the first origination of time until today the sphere of the fixed stars has revolved one hundred fifty thousand times, and also possible such that from the first origination until today it has revolved fifty thousand times; this is what is meant by priority and posteriority. Once this is clarified, the need for a specifier and preponderant becomes evident. And the answer to the second question: The concept of specification (takhsis) is different from the concept of creation (takwin); when the two concepts differ and the two considerations are distinct, we name the concept of the principle of specification 'will' (irada) and we name the concept of the principle of creation 'power' (qudra).
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion with two further points. First, an objection is raised that knowledge could be the preponderant. Razi responds by distinguishing between knowledge as a mere reflection of the act and as a reason for preference: God's knowledge of the act's goodness and lack of evil is what tilts the balance, not the knowledge of the occurrence itself. Then Razi gives two final answers to earlier questions. The first answer clarifies what is meant by 'priority and posteriority' when time is originated: even if time began, we can conceive of different numbers of celestial rotations between that beginning and now, which gives a meaningful sense of temporal priority. This shows the need for a murajjih. The second answer distinguishes between specification (takhsis) and creation (takwin), assigning will to the former and power to the latter.

المرجح
الوقوع
الإحسان
القبح
الرجحان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's knowledge directly causes events. Razi clarifies that it is not knowledge of the event but knowledge of its moral qualities that functions as a preponderant.
Correction: Razi distinguishes between knowledge of occurrence (which follows the event) and knowledge of the event's goodness (which is a reason for preference).

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what kind of knowledge serves as the cause for an act's preference over omission?
A) Knowledge of the act's occurrence
B) Knowledge that the act is good and free from evil
C) Knowledge of the act's temporal location
D) Knowledge of the agent's power

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge of an act's moral qualities, not its occurrence, is what grounds divine preference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of the goodness in an act is what causes Him to prefer it, what does that mean for your own pursuit of good deeds? Are you seeking to understand what truly makes an act good in His eyes, or are you content with outward compliance?
?
This passage shows that God's knowledge of your actions is not just about whether they occur, but about their moral quality. When you face a choice, do you consider that God knows not only your action but also the goodness or evil within it? How should that awareness change your deliberation?
?
Razi distinguishes between knowledge that follows the act and knowledge that precedes and causes it. How often do you let your own knowledge of past failures dictate your present choices rather than focusing on the goodness of the act itself? What would it take for you to let knowledge of moral value, not past occurrence, be your preponderant?

Time, Specification, and the Distinction Between Will and Power
Razi gives two conclusive clarifications on time and specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how temporal priority is meaningful in an originated time and why specification and creation are distinct concepts assigned to will and power.

Core Idea
Time's origin doesn't erase priority; will is for specification, power for creation.

After refuting the knowledge objection, Razi presents two final clarifications. The first explains how we can meaningfully speak of 'before' and 'after' even if time is created, using a thought experiment about celestial rotations. The second distinguishes the concepts of specification (takhsis) and creation (takwin), assigning each to a different divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer on time
Start by reading Razi's answer to the first question about time. He asks you to suppose different numbers of stellar revolutions from the beginning of time to now.
This shows that different possible durations are conceivable, making priority meaningful.
2
Grasp the need for a preponderant
Because multiple durations are possible, the actual duration requires a specifier (murajjih). This shows time does not eliminate the need for a preponderant.
3
Read the second answer on specification
Now read Razi's distinction between specification and creation. He says they are different concepts.
Note that 'specification' refers to determination of attributes, while 'creation' is bringing into existence.
4
Understand the assignment to will and power
Razi assigns the principle of specification to will (irada) and the principle of creation to power (qudra). This resolves the earlier confusion.
This distinction prevents conflating the roles of will and power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

We accept that power cannot be the preponderant, so why may not knowledge be the preponderant? He says: 'Knowledge of occurrence is consequent upon occurrence, so it cannot be effective in occurrence.' We say: We do not say that the effective in occurrence is knowledge of occurrence; rather, we say: Allah's knowledge that this act contains beneficence to others while being free from all evil is a cause for the preference of the act over omission, and on this assumption your objection is rebutted. The answer to the first question: Time, although originated, but let us suppose that from the first origination of time until today the sphere of the fixed stars has revolved one hundred thousand times, and it is intellectually possible that the world exists such that from the first origination of time until today the sphere of the fixed stars has revolved one hundred fifty thousand times, and also possible such that from the first origination until today it has revolved fifty thousand times; this is what is meant by priority and posteriority. Once this is clarified, the need for a specifier and preponderant becomes evident. And the answer to the second question: The concept of specification (takhsis) is different from the concept of creation (takwin); when the two concepts differ and the two considerations are distinct, we name the concept of the principle of specification 'will' (irada) and we name the concept of the principle of creation 'power' (qudra).
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion with two further points. First, an objection is raised that knowledge could be the preponderant. Razi responds by distinguishing between knowledge as a mere reflection of the act and as a reason for preference: God's knowledge of the act's goodness and lack of evil is what tilts the balance, not the knowledge of the occurrence itself. Then Razi gives two final answers to earlier questions. The first answer clarifies what is meant by 'priority and posteriority' when time is originated: even if time began, we can conceive of different numbers of celestial rotations between that beginning and now, which gives a meaningful sense of temporal priority. This shows the need for a murajjih. The second answer distinguishes between specification (takhsis) and creation (takwin), assigning will to the former and power to the latter.

Key Terms
الزمان
التقدم
التأخر
التخصيص
التكوين
الإرادة
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that in an originated time, there is no real 'before' and 'after', so no need for a preponderant.
Correction: Razi shows that different possible durations (e.g., different stellar revolutions) give a meaningful sense of priority, requiring a specifier.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second answer, what is the principle of specification called?
A) Power (qudra)
B) Will (irada)
C) Knowledge ('ilm)
D) Time (zaman)

Key Takeaway
Time's creation does not eliminate meaningful priority, and specification (will) is conceptually distinct from creation (power).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the events of your day, do you consciously recognize that God's will specifies the exact manner and time of each occurrence, while His power brings it into being? How does this distinction change the way you pray or trust in Him for both direction and ability?
?
Think of a recent situation that felt random or pointless. In light of this lesson, can you now see it as deliberately specified by God's will? What would it mean for you to trust that even the details are from Him, not just the general outcome?
?
This lesson separates specification from creation. In your own seeking of God's help, do you more often ask for the 'power' to overcome or the 'guidance' for the right path? Starting today, will you commit to asking for both—specification of the best course and the power to execute it?

The First Objection: God as Necessary Cause
First objection against denying preponderant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how denying the need for a preponderant leads to the conclusion that God is not a free agent but a necessary cause.

Core Idea
Without a preponderant, God's will would make Him a cause by essence, not a free agent.

Imam Razi begins his refutation by stating the opponent's position: that the divine will alone, by being associated with a specific time, eliminates the need for any additional preponderant. He then presents his first objection, arguing that this assumption would make God a necessitating cause, stripping Him of free choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's claim and the first objection
Read the Arabic passage once. The opponent claims that the will, when tied to a time, suffices without a preponderant. Razi says this is false, starting with the first aspect.
2
Identify the logical consequence
Notice that Razi argues that without a preponderant, the Maker would be a cause by essence, not a free agent. Why is this a problem for Islamic theology?
3
Reflect on the importance of free agency
Ask yourself: why is God being a free agent a foundational belief in Kalam? How does this objection protect that belief?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة عشرة بالوقت الذي حدث فيه، وليس لها صلاحية أن تخصص إحداث ما حدث في وقت بوقت آخر، وعلى هذا التقدير تستغني الإرادة عن مرجع آخر؛ لأنا نقول: هذا باطل من وجوه: الأول: أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون صانع العالم فاعلا مختاراً، بل علة موجبة بالذات؛ لأنه لما كان بحال أوجب أن يكون مؤثرًا في الإيجاد في هذا الوقت ويمتنع عقلا أن يكون موجبا له في وقت آخر، لم يكن له اختيار البتة، بل كان موجبًا بالذات. الثاني: لو جاز هذا الكلام في الإرادة فلم لا يجوز مثله في القدرة؟ يقال: قدرة الله لها صلاحية الإيجاد في ذلك الوقت المعين، وليس لها صلاحية الإيجاد في سائر الأوقات، وعلى هذا التقدير تستغني هذه القدرة عن الإرادة. الثالث: أن الأوقات متساوية، فلو جاز أن يقال هذا الوقت المعين له خاصية، وإرادة الله تعالى لا تصلح لتخصيص الحادث المعين إلا به، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: لكل واحد من الأوقات خاصة، والمؤثر في حدوث هذه الحوادث هو خواص هذه الأوقات؟ وعلى هذا التقدير يكون المؤثر في حدوث الحوادث هو الأوقات لا الصانع، ويلزم منه نفي الصانع. سلمنا أنه لا يجوز أن يكون المرجح هو القدرة، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المرجح هو العلم؟ قوله: "العلم بالوقوع تبع للوقوع، فلا يجوز أن يكون مؤثراً في الوقوع" قلنا: نحن لا نقول: المؤثر في الوقوع هو العلم بالوقوع بل نقول: علم الله تعالى باشتمال هذا الفعل على الإحسان إلى الغير مع كونه عاريًا عن جميع القبح، سبب لرجحان الفعل على الترك، وعلى هذا التقدير يندفع ما ذكرتم. جواب السؤال الأول: أن الزمان وإن كان محدثًا لكن لنفرض أن من أول حدوث الزمان إلى هذا اليوم دار فلك الثوابت مائة ألف مرة وكان يجوز في العقل أن يوجد العالم بحيث يكون من أول حدوث الزمان إلى هذا اليوم قد دار فلك الثوابت مائة وخمسين ألف مرة ويجوز أيضًا بحيث يكون من أول الحدوث إلى هذا اليوم دار فلك الثوابت خمسين ألف مرة، فهذا هو المراد من التقدم والتأخر، وإذا تلخص هذا ظهر الاحتياج إلى المخصص والمرجح. وجواب السؤال الثاني: أن المفهوم من التخصيص غير المفهوم من التكوين وإذا اختلف المفهومان وتغاير الاعتباران سمينا مفهوم مبدأ التخصيص بالإرادة، وسمينا مفهوم مبدأ الإيجاد بالقدرة. وجواب السؤال الثالث: أنا سنقيم الدلالة على أنه تعالى خالق لجميع العباد، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك بطل تعليل أفعال الله تعالى بالحسن والقبح، ورعاية المصالح.
The Thirteenth Problem: By the time in which it occurred, and it has no capacity to allocate the occurrence of what occurred at a time over another time, and on this assumption the will becomes independent of another preponderant; then we say: This is false in three aspects: First: On this assumption the Maker of the world would not be a free agent (faʿilan mukhtāran), but rather a cause necessitating by essence (ʿillatan mūjibatan bi-dh-dhāt); because since He was in a state that necessitates His being effective in bringing into existence at this time, and it is intellectually impossible for Him to bring it into existence at another time, He would have no choice at all, but would be a necessitator by essence. Second: If this speech is permitted in the will, why is the like not permitted in power (al-qudra)? It would be said: God's power has the capacity to bring into existence at that specific time, and it has no capacity to bring into existence in other times, and on this assumption this power becomes independent of the will. Third: Times are equivalent, so if it is permitted to say that this specific time has a particular property (khāṣṣiyya), and the will of God Most High is not suitable to specify the particular event except by it, then why is it not permitted to say: Each time has a particular property, and the effective cause (al-muʾaththir) in the occurrence of these events is the properties of these times? On this assumption, the effective cause in the occurrence of events would be the times, not the Maker, and the negation of the Maker would follow. We concede that it is not permitted that the preponderant be power, so why is it not permitted that the preponderant be knowledge? His statement: "Knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence, so it cannot be effective in occurrence." We say: We do not say that the effective in occurrence is knowledge of occurrence; rather we say: God's knowledge that this act includes beneficence to others while being free of all ugliness is a cause for the preponderance of the act over omission, and on this assumption what you mentioned is repelled. Answer to the first question: That time, though originated, let us suppose that from the first origination of time until this day the sphere of the fixed stars has completed one hundred thousand rotations, and it is intellectually possible that the world could exist such that from the first origination of time until this day the sphere of the fixed stars has completed one hundred fifty thousand rotations, and also possible such that from the first origination until this day the sphere of the fixed stars has completed fifty thousand rotations; this is the meaning of priority and posteriority. When this is clear, the need for a specifier (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ) and a preponderant (al-murajjiḥ) appears. Answer to the second question: That the concept of specification (al-takhṣīṣ) is different from the concept of origination (al-takwīn); when the two concepts differ and the considerations vary, we name the concept of the principle of specification "will" (al-irāda), and we name the concept of the principle of origination "power" (al-qudra). Answer to the third question: We will establish the proof that He Most High is the Creator of all servants; when this is so, the explanation of God's acts by good and evil and observance of interests becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection claiming that the divine will alone is sufficient to specify the time of creation without an external preponderant (murajjiḥ). Imam Razi first states the opponent's position: that the will, by being associated with a particular time, renders a preponderant unnecessary. He then refutes this with three aspects (wujūh). First, if the will necessarily determines a specific time, the Maker would be a necessary cause by essence, not a free agent (mukhtār), which negates choice. Second, by the same logic, one could claim that power (qudra) alone suffices, making will redundant. Third, if times have inherent properties, then times themselves would be the cause of events, leading to atheism. He then transitions to a concession: even if power is not the preponderant, could knowledge (ʿilm) be? He refutes this by distinguishing between knowledge of occurrence (which follows occurrence) and God's knowledge of the act's goodness, which serves as a preponderant. Finally, he answers three specific questions (asʾila). The first answer explains that time, though created, allows for different possibilities (e.g., variations in celestial rotations), hence a specifier is needed. The second answer distinguishes specification (takhṣīṣ) from origination (takwīn), assigning the former to will and the latter to power. The third answer invokes God's creation of all acts, thereby nullifying moral explanations for His actions.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
عِلَّة مُوجِبَة بالذات (cause necessitating by essence)
فاعل مختار (free agent)
مرجح (preponderant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will alone is sufficient to determine the time of creation without any other factor, making an additional preponderant unnecessary.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that this leads to God being a necessary cause, not a free agent, which is theologically problematic.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what would be the consequence if the will alone, without a preponderant, determined the time of creation?
A) God would be a free agent
B) God would be a cause necessitating by essence
C) The world would be eternal
D) Time would be uncreated

Key Takeaway
Denying the need for a preponderant reduces God from a free agent to a necessary cause, contradicting divine omnipotence and volition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever think of God as a 'necessary cause' – that He must do certain things, like punishing sin or rewarding good – rather than as a personal Lord who freely chooses to forgive, delay, or test you? How does that impact your hope in His mercy?
?
If you truly believed that every event is determined by God's free will, not by any necessity, would you still grumble at your circumstances? Would you surrender your complaints and trust that He could have done otherwise but chose this for you?
?
This lesson shows that without the preponderant, God becomes a mere cause, not a free agent. In your prayer life, do you treat God as if His response is automatic based on your actions, or do you speak to Him as a free King who can answer as He pleases?

The Second and Third Objections: Power and Times as Causes
Second and third objections against denying preponderant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional arguments from Imam Razi: that the same logic applied to will could also apply to power, and that if times have inherent properties, they could replace God as the cause.

Core Idea
If will alone suffices, then power could as well; and if times have special properties, they could be the true causes.

Imam Razi continues his refutation with two more objections. The second objection applies the opponent's logic to divine power (qudra), showing that if the will can determine a time without a preponderant, then power could do the same, making will redundant. The third objection argues that if times have inherent special properties that necessitate events, then times themselves would be the effective causes, leading to atheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the Arabic and translation for the second objection. Razi asks: why not say the same about power? Notice the parallel argument.
2
Read the third objection
Read the third objection. Razi points out that if times are equivalent, then claiming a specific time has a special property leads to attributing causality to time itself.
3
Compare the two arguments
Both objections use parity of reasoning to expose absurd consequences. Reflect on how these strengthen the case for needing a preponderant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

The Knowledge Objection and Three Answers
Refutation of knowledge as preponderant and three answers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's response to the objection that knowledge could serve as a preponderant, and his answers to three related questions about time, specification, and moral reasoning.

Core Idea
God's knowledge of an act's goodness can serve as the preponderant, and specification and origination are distinct concepts.

Imam Razi now addresses a concession: even if power is not the preponderant, why not knowledge? He refutes this by distinguishing between knowledge of occurrence (which follows the occurrence) and God's knowledge of the act's goodness, which can be a cause for preponderance. He then provides answers to three questions: the first explains how time allows for different possibilities, necessitating a specifier; the second distinguishes specification from origination, assigning will and power accordingly; the third invokes God's creation of all acts to nullify moral explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the knowledge objection and response
Read the part about knowledge. Note the opponent's claim and Razi's reply that God's knowledge of goodness can serve as a preponderant.
2
Read the first answer about time
Razi explains that time, though created, allows for different possibilities (like different numbers of celestial rotations), showing the need for a specifier.
3
Read the second and third answers
The second answer distinguishes specification (takhṣīṣ) from origination (takwīn). The third states that since God creates all acts, moral explanations for His actions are invalid.
