المسألة الثالثة عشرة بالوقت الذي حدث فيه، وليس لها صلاحية أن تخصص إحداث ما حدث في وقت بوقت آخر، وعلى هذا التقدير تستغني الإرادة عن مرجع آخر؛ لأنا نقول: هذا باطل من وجوه: الأول: أن على هذا التقدير لا يكون صانع العالم فاعلا مختاراً، بل علة موجبة بالذات؛ لأنه لما كان بحال أوجب أن يكون مؤثرًا في الإيجاد في هذا الوقت ويمتنع عقلا أن يكون موجبا له في وقت آخر، لم يكن له اختيار البتة، بل كان موجبًا بالذات. الثاني: لو جاز هذا الكلام في الإرادة فلم لا يجوز مثله في القدرة؟ يقال: قدرة الله لها صلاحية الإيجاد في ذلك الوقت المعين، وليس لها صلاحية الإيجاد في سائر الأوقات، وعلى هذا التقدير تستغني هذه القدرة عن الإرادة. الثالث: أن الأوقات متساوية، فلو جاز أن يقال هذا الوقت المعين له خاصية، وإرادة الله تعالى لا تصلح لتخصيص الحادث المعين إلا به، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: لكل واحد من الأوقات خاصة، والمؤثر في حدوث هذه الحوادث هو خواص هذه الأوقات؟ وعلى هذا التقدير يكون المؤثر في حدوث الحوادث هو الأوقات لا الصانع، ويلزم منه نفي الصانع. سلمنا أنه لا يجوز أن يكون المرجح هو القدرة، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون المرجح هو العلم؟ قوله: "العلم بالوقوع تبع للوقوع، فلا يجوز أن يكون مؤثراً في الوقوع" قلنا: نحن لا نقول: المؤثر في الوقوع هو العلم بالوقوع بل نقول: علم الله تعالى باشتمال هذا الفعل على الإحسان إلى الغير مع كونه عاريًا عن جميع القبح، سبب لرجحان الفعل على الترك، وعلى هذا التقدير يندفع ما ذكرتم. جواب السؤال الأول: أن الزمان وإن كان محدثًا لكن لنفرض أن من أول حدوث الزمان إلى هذا اليوم دار فلك الثوابت مائة ألف مرة وكان يجوز في العقل أن يوجد العالم بحيث يكون من أول حدوث الزمان إلى هذا اليوم قد دار فلك الثوابت مائة وخمسين ألف مرة ويجوز أيضًا بحيث يكون من أول الحدوث إلى هذا اليوم دار فلك الثوابت خمسين ألف مرة، فهذا هو المراد من التقدم والتأخر، وإذا تلخص هذا ظهر الاحتياج إلى المخصص والمرجح. وجواب السؤال الثاني: أن المفهوم من التخصيص غير المفهوم من التكوين وإذا اختلف المفهومان وتغاير الاعتباران سمينا مفهوم مبدأ التخصيص بالإرادة، وسمينا مفهوم مبدأ الإيجاد بالقدرة. وجواب السؤال الثالث: أنا سنقيم الدلالة على أنه تعالى خالق لجميع العباد، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك بطل تعليل أفعال الله تعالى بالحسن والقبح، ورعاية المصالح.
The Thirteenth Problem: By the time in which it occurred, and it has no capacity to allocate the occurrence of what occurred at a time over another time, and on this assumption the will becomes independent of another preponderant; then we say: This is false in three aspects: First: On this assumption the Maker of the world would not be a free agent (faʿilan mukhtāran), but rather a cause necessitating by essence (ʿillatan mūjibatan bi-dh-dhāt); because since He was in a state that necessitates His being effective in bringing into existence at this time, and it is intellectually impossible for Him to bring it into existence at another time, He would have no choice at all, but would be a necessitator by essence. Second: If this speech is permitted in the will, why is the like not permitted in power (al-qudra)? It would be said: God's power has the capacity to bring into existence at that specific time, and it has no capacity to bring into existence in other times, and on this assumption this power becomes independent of the will. Third: Times are equivalent, so if it is permitted to say that this specific time has a particular property (khāṣṣiyya), and the will of God Most High is not suitable to specify the particular event except by it, then why is it not permitted to say: Each time has a particular property, and the effective cause (al-muʾaththir) in the occurrence of these events is the properties of these times? On this assumption, the effective cause in the occurrence of events would be the times, not the Maker, and the negation of the Maker would follow. We concede that it is not permitted that the preponderant be power, so why is it not permitted that the preponderant be knowledge? His statement: "Knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence, so it cannot be effective in occurrence." We say: We do not say that the effective in occurrence is knowledge of occurrence; rather we say: God's knowledge that this act includes beneficence to others while being free of all ugliness is a cause for the preponderance of the act over omission, and on this assumption what you mentioned is repelled. Answer to the first question: That time, though originated, let us suppose that from the first origination of time until this day the sphere of the fixed stars has completed one hundred thousand rotations, and it is intellectually possible that the world could exist such that from the first origination of time until this day the sphere of the fixed stars has completed one hundred fifty thousand rotations, and also possible such that from the first origination until this day the sphere of the fixed stars has completed fifty thousand rotations; this is the meaning of priority and posteriority. When this is clear, the need for a specifier (al-mukhaṣṣiṣ) and a preponderant (al-murajjiḥ) appears. Answer to the second question: That the concept of specification (al-takhṣīṣ) is different from the concept of origination (al-takwīn); when the two concepts differ and the considerations vary, we name the concept of the principle of specification "will" (al-irāda), and we name the concept of the principle of origination "power" (al-qudra). Answer to the third question: We will establish the proof that He Most High is the Creator of all servants; when this is so, the explanation of God's acts by good and evil and observance of interests becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection claiming that the divine will alone is sufficient to specify the time of creation without an external preponderant (murajjiḥ). Imam Razi first states the opponent's position: that the will, by being associated with a particular time, renders a preponderant unnecessary. He then refutes this with three aspects (wujūh). First, if the will necessarily determines a specific time, the Maker would be a necessary cause by essence, not a free agent (mukhtār), which negates choice. Second, by the same logic, one could claim that power (qudra) alone suffices, making will redundant. Third, if times have inherent properties, then times themselves would be the cause of events, leading to atheism. He then transitions to a concession: even if power is not the preponderant, could knowledge (ʿilm) be? He refutes this by distinguishing between knowledge of occurrence (which follows occurrence) and God's knowledge of the act's goodness, which serves as a preponderant. Finally, he answers three specific questions (asʾila). The first answer explains that time, though created, allows for different possibilities (e.g., variations in celestial rotations), hence a specifier is needed. The second answer distinguishes specification (takhṣīṣ) from origination (takwīn), assigning the former to will and the latter to power. The third answer invokes God's creation of all acts, thereby nullifying moral explanations for His actions.

Key Terms
علم (knowledge)
إرادة (will)
قدرة (power)
تخصيص (specification)
تكوين (origination)
مرجح (preponderant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge of an event before it occurs is the cause of that event.
Correction: Razi clarifies that knowledge of occurrence follows occurrence and cannot be the cause; rather, God's knowledge of the act's goodness can be a cause for preponderance.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what is one function of God's knowledge in relation to an act?
A) It causes the act to occur
B) It follows the act and records it
C) It knows the act's goodness, which can lead to preponderance of the act over omission
D) It determines the time of the act

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of an act's moral qualities can serve as a preponderant, and specification and origination are distinct attributes assigned to will and power respectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever justified your own actions by saying 'I knew it was good, so I did it'? How does Razi’s distinction between knowledge of moral qualities and knowledge of occurrence challenge you to examine whether your knowledge truly makes your actions good, or if you are simply rationalizing after the fact?
?
The lesson says God’s knowledge of an act’s beneficence and freedom from ugliness is the cause of its preponderance. If you truly believed that God’s knowledge—not your own evaluation—determines the worth of your actions, would you still persist in sins that you know are ugly? What would you need to change starting today?
?
Razi argues that God’s actions cannot be judged by human moral standards because He creates all acts. Is there an area of your life where you secretly judge God’s decrees as 'unfair' or 'bad'? Will you now submit to the truth that His knowledge and will alone determine what is good, and repent of that hidden criticism?

Refuting the First Objection: Divine Will Implies No Need
Razi presents and refutes the first philosophical objection to divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that divine will implies deficiency and how Imam Razi refutes it by showing the error in their premises.

Core Idea
Divine will does not imply need; the objection collapses on its own assumptions.

The philosophers argue that if God wills, He must consider the act preferable, implying deficiency before the act. But this only applies to beings that are completed by their acts. Imam Razi dissects this argument and shows it fails even when one tries to salvage it by appealing to beneficence to others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the structure: objection, two premises, a counter, and a refutation.
Notice the dialectical style: Imam Razi presents the objection first, then refutes it step by step.
2
Identify the two premises
The philosophers' objection rests on two premises. Find them in the passage: (1) A willing agent must have a preference for the act over its omission. (2) Having such a preference implies deficiency before the act and completion through it.
These premises are explained with their own proofs in the passage.
3
Understand the counter-argument and refutation
Imam Razi anticipates a counter: maybe the act is chosen for the benefit of others, not for oneself. Read how he refutes this: if the act and its omission are equivalent for the agent, preference is impossible; if not, the act becomes preferable for the agent, leading back to the same problem.
4
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider how this argument upholds the doctrine that God is free from need and deficiency. His will does not arise from a need to perfect Himself, but from His own sovereignty and wisdom.
This connects to the broader theme of tanzih (divine transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الأولى: أن كل من قصد إلى إيجاد شيء فلا بد وأن يكون تحصيل ذلك الفعل أولى في علمه واعتقاده من تركه وكل من كان كذلك كان قبل ذلك الفعل ناقصا، ويصير مستكملا بسبب ذلك الفعل، وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال. أما بيان المقدمة الأولى - وهي: أن كل من قصد إلى شيء فلا بد وأن يكون ذلك الفعل أولى في اعتقاده من تركه - أنه لو لم تحصل هذه الأولوية في اعتقاد ذلك الفاعل لكان الفعل والترك بالنسبة إليه سيان، ولو كان كذلك امتنع كونه مرجحًا للفعل على الترك؛ لأن حصول الترجيح بدون المرجح محال. وأما بيان المقدمة الثانية - وهي: أن كل من كان وجود ذلك الفعل أولى به من عدمه فهو ناقص - فدليله أنه إذا فعل ذلك الفعل حصلت تلك الأولوية، وإذا لم يفعل لم تحصل، فكان ناقصا بذاته، مستكملا بغيره. لا يقال: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: الفعل والترك - وإن استويا بالنسبة إليه - إلا أن الفعل أصلح للغير من الترك، وهذا الفاعل يرجح الفعل لا لأنه أنفع له بل لأنه إحسان إلى الغير، وإيصال للنفع إلى الغير؟ لأنا نقول: الإحسان إلى الغير وتركه إن استويا بالنسبة إليه امتنع الترجيح وإن لم يستويا كان الإحسان إلى الغير أولى به، فيكون الإحسان إلى الغير سببًا لاستكماله وتركه يصير سببًا لنقصانه، فيعود المحذور المذكور.
The first sophism: That everyone who intends to bring something into existence must have the attainment of that act preferable in his knowledge and belief over its omission, and everyone who is like that was before that act deficient (nāqiṣan), and becomes perfecting himself (mustakmilan) by means of that act; and this is impossible with respect to God Most High. As for the explanation of the first premise — which is that everyone who intends something must have that act preferable in his belief over its omission — [it is] that if this preference does not obtain in the belief of that agent, then act and omission would be equivalent for him; if that were so, it would be impossible for him to be a preponderant of the act over omission, because the occurrence of preponderance without a preponderant is impossible. As for the explanation of the second premise — which is that everyone for whom the existence of that act is preferable to non-existence is deficient — its proof is that when he does that act, that preference obtains, and when he does not do it, it does not obtain; so he was deficient in himself (nāqiṣan bi-dhātihī), perfecting himself by others (mustakmilan bi-ghayrihī). It may not be said: Why is it not permissible to say that act and omission — though equivalent for him — except that the act is more beneficial for others than omission, and this agent preponderates the act not because it is beneficial to him but because it is beneficence to others and conveyance of benefit to others? For we say: Beneficence to others and its omission, if they are equivalent for him, preponderance is impossible; and if they are not equivalent, then beneficence to others is preferable for him; thus beneficence to others becomes a cause for his completion (istikmālihi) and its omission becomes a cause for his deficiency; thus the mentioned difficulty returns.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first philosophical objection against divine will. The philosophers argue that if God wills an act, He must consider that act preferable to its omission in His knowledge, which implies that before the act He was deficient and became perfect through the act — impossible for God. Imam Razi explains the two premises: first, a willing agent must have a preference for the act; second, having a preference for something one lacks implies deficiency fulfilled by that thing. He then anticipates a counter: that the act might be chosen for the benefit of others, not for oneself. The refutation shows that even if the act is for others, if it is equivalent to omission for the agent, no preponderance occurs; if it is not equivalent, then the agent still prefers it for himself, leading again to the conclusion that he is completed by the act. Thus the objection stands refuted.

Key Terms
قصد (intention)
أولوية (preference)
ناقص (deficient)
مستكمل (perfecting)
إحسان (beneficence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God acts for the benefit of others, then His will does not imply any need or deficiency in Him.
Correction: Even if the act is for others, the agent still must have a preference between the act and its omission. If they are equal, no preponderance; if not equal, the agent prefers one, which still implies that the preferred alternative completes him. So the objection remains.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the philosophers' objection?
A) Every agent must have a purpose
B) Every agent must prefer the act over its omission in his belief
C) Every agent must be complete before acting
D) Every act must be beneficial

Key Takeaway
Divine will does not imply deficiency; the philosophers' objection fails because it assumes that preference implies completion, which does not apply to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do something good, do you look for personal benefit or recognition? How does Razi's refutation of the philosophers' objection challenge the assumption that all actions are self-interested? Commit to one act of worship this week solely for God's sake, with no expectation of return.
?
The philosophers argued that any preference implies deficiency. Think of a time when you preferred something and later felt it completed you. Does that mean you were deficient before? How does this lesson help you understand that God's perfection is entirely different from human perfection? What does that mean for your trust in Him?
?
Imam Razi shows that even acts for others can imply a need if there is a preference. Examine your own acts of charity: do you do them because it is better for you to do them? Or do you do them purely for God? Which motive is more pleasing to God? Write down one change you will make to purify your intention.

Refuting the Objection: Will Requires a Temporal Will
Imam Razi presents and refutes the second philosophical objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's refutation of the philosophical claim that if God were willing, His will would have to be a temporal creation.

Core Idea
A resolution without awareness of time is not an intention.

The philosophers argued that if God were willing (murīd), He would need a temporal will, which is impossible, therefore God cannot be willing. Imam Razi refutes this by clarifying the difference between intention (qaṣd) and resolution (ʿazm). He explains that intention to create can only occur at the moment of creation; before that, it is merely a resolution. Moreover, he shows that even a persistent resolution does not become intention without knowledge that the appointed time has arrived. This lesson presents that argument and a vivid example.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument. Notice the logical structure: objection, entailment, counter, refutation.
The passage ends mid-sentence due to a page break.
2
Identify the objection and entailment
Find where the philosophers state their objection and where Razi explains the entailment (mulāzama). Note the key terms: irāda muḥdatha, qaṣd, ʿazm.
3
Understand the distinction
Razi says intention to bring into existence only occurs at the moment of existence. Before that, it's just resolution. Why is that distinction important?
This is central to the refutation.
4
Analyze the refutation example
Razi gives an example of a person in a dark room who resolves to act tomorrow. Even if the resolution persists, without knowing that tomorrow has arrived, he does not become an intender. Ask: What does this show about the need for knowledge?
5
Connect to divine will
Reflect on how this example defeats the objection. If God's will were temporal, it would require knowledge of the moment of creation, which is impossible for a created thing in the divine context.
The passage cuts off, but the argument's direction is clear.

الشبهة الثانية لهم قالوا: لو كان الله تعالى مريدا لكان مريدا بإرادة محدثة، وهذا محال، فذلك محال. أما بيان الملازمة فهو أن القصد إلى الإيجاد يمتنع حصوله إلا عند حصول ذلك الإيجاد فأما قبل حصول ذلك الإيجاد فذلك لا يكون قصدًا إلى الإيجاد بل يكون ذلك عزمًا على أنه سيوجد في الوقت الفلاني. لا يقال: لم لا يجوز أن يكون العزم على أنه سيفعل غدًا يكون نفس القصد إلى الإيجاد عند حضور الغد؟ لأنا نقول: إن من عزم على أن يفعل بكرة الغد ثم جلس في بيت مظلم لا يميز فيه بين الليل والنهار واستمر ذلك العزم في قلبه إلى أن جاء الغد لكنه لم يعلم مجيء الغد فإنه لا يصير قاصدًا إلى الفعل، ولو كان العزم
The second sophism of them: They said: If God Most High were willing (murīdan), He would be willing by a temporal will (irādatan muḥdathatan); this is impossible, so that is impossible. As for the explanation of the entailment (al-mulāzama): that the intention (al-qaṣdu) to bring into existence is impossible to occur except at the occurrence of that bringing into existence; as for before the occurrence of that bringing into existence, that is not an intention to bring into existence, but rather that is a resolution (ʿazm) to the effect that it will exist at such-and-such time. It may not be said: Why is it not permissible that the resolution to do tomorrow be itself the intention to bring into existence at the arrival of tomorrow? For we say: Whoever resolves to do early tomorrow, then sits in a dark room in which he cannot distinguish between night and day, and that resolution persists in his heart until tomorrow arrives, but he does not know the arrival of tomorrow, then he does not become an intender (qāṣidan) of the act; and if the resolution were
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophical objection: that if God were willing (murīd), His will would have to be temporal (muḥdatha), which is impossible, therefore God cannot be willing. The entailment (mulāzama) is explained: intention (qasd) to create can only occur at the moment of creation; before that, it is merely a resolution (ʿazm). The philosophers anticipate a counter: could the resolution itself become the intention at the appointed time? Imam Razi refutes this with an example: someone resolves to act tomorrow but sits in a dark room unaware of time's arrival; despite having the resolution, he does not become an intender. The answer implies that knowledge of the time's arrival is necessary for intention, which is impossible for a temporal will in God's case, hence the objection fails. The passage cuts off before completing the refutation, but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
إرادة محدثة
قصد
عزم
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A resolution (ʿazm) to perform an action at a future time is the same as intending (qaṣd) that action when the time arrives.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that for intention to occur, one must have knowledge that the time has arrived. A mere persistent resolution without awareness of time does not constitute intention, as demonstrated by the dark room example.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's refutation, what distinguishes a resolution (عزم) from an intention (قصد) to act at a specific time?
A) The resolution must be accompanied by knowledge that the time has arrived
B) The resolution must be strong
C) The intention must be eternal
D) The intention must be free of any change

Key Takeaway
Intention requires knowledge of the moment of action, so a mere resolution does not become intention, thereby refuting the claim that divine will would have to be temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you resolve to do something for Allah—like praying on time or giving up a sin—do you rely on your own awareness of the 'right moment,' or have you ever missed that moment because you were like the person in the dark room, unaware that the time had come? What does this teach you about the need for active consciousness in your worship?
?
The philosophers reduced God's will to a temporal accident. But Razi shows that resolution can persist without becoming intention. In your life, how many of your resolutions toward Allah remain just that—resolutions that never cross into real intention and action? What is stopping you from making that crossing right now?
?
If you truly believed that your intention (qaṣd) to obey Allah requires not just a resolution but knowledge of the moment, would you prepare yourself differently for acts of worship? What change in your daily habits would that demand starting today?

First Argument Against God's Will
Razi presents and refutes the first philosophical argument against God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical argument that will implies deficiency and how Imam Razi refutes it by exposing its underlying logical flaw.

Core Idea
Will does not imply deficiency; the argument confuses motive with need.

The philosophers argued that if God wills to create, then creating must be better for Him than not creating, implying He was deficient before creating. Imam Razi presents this argument and then refutes it by showing that even if the motive is benefiting others, the same logic applies. This lesson unpacks the argument's two premises and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: two premises, then an objection, then a reply.
The argument has a clear logical flow.
2
Identify the two premises
The first premise states that intending something requires seeing it as better; the second states that lacking that better makes one deficient. Understand how each is justified.
3
Examine the objection
The objection suggests the act might be chosen because it benefits others, not the agent. Consider why this seems to avoid the problem.
4
Follow the refutation
Razi shows that the objection fails: if benefiting others is equal to not benefiting, no preference; if it is better, then the agent still seeks completion. Thus the original problem remains.
The refutation is a dilemma.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the argument does not disprove God's will; it only shows that the philosophers' assumption that intention implies deficiency is flawed.
This is a classic kalam refutation.

الشبهة الأولى : أن كل من قصد إلى إيجاد شيء فلا بد وأن يكون تحصيل ذلك الفعل أولى في علمه واعتقاده من تركه وكل من كان كذلك كان قبل ذلك الفعل ناقصا ، ويصير مستكملا بسبب ذلك الفعل، وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال. أما بيان المقدمة الأولى - وهي : أن كل من قصد إلى شيء فلا بد وأن يكون ذلك الفعل أولى في اعتقاده من تركه - أنه لو لم تحصل هذه الأولوية في اعتقاد ذلك الفاعل لكان الفعل والترك بالنسبة إليه سيان، ولو كان كذلك امتنع كونه مرجحًا للفعل على الترك ؛ لأن حصول الترجيح بدون المرجح محال. كان وجود ذلك الفعل أولى به من [؟] وأما بيان المقدمة الثانية - وهي : أن كل من عدمه فهو ناقص - فدليله أنه إذا فعل ذلك الفعل حصلت تلك الأولوية، وإذا لم يفعل لم تحصل، فكان ناقصا بذاته، مستكملا بغيره. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : الفعل والترك - وإن استويا بالنسبة إليه - إلا أن الفعل أصلح للغير من الترك، وهذا الفاعل يرجح الفعل لا لأنه أنفع له بل لأنه إحسان إلى الغير، وإيصال للنفع إلى الغير ؟ لأنا نقول : الإحسان إلى الغير وتركه إن استويا بالنسبة إليه امتنع الترجيح وإن لم يستويا كان الإحسان إلى الغير أولى به، فيكون الإحسان إلى الغير سببًا لاستكماله وتركه يصير سببا لنقصانه، فيعود المحذور المذكور.
The first doubt: That everyone who intends to bring something into existence must necessarily have the attainment of that act be better in his knowledge and belief than its omission, and everyone who is like that was, before that act, deficient, and becomes complete by that act, and this is impossible with respect to God. As for the explanation of the first premise—namely, that everyone who intends something must necessarily have that act be better in his belief than its omission—that if this superiority were not present in the belief of that agent, then the act and the omission would be equal for him, and if that were the case, it would be impossible for him to be a preferrer of the act over omission, because the occurrence of preference without a preferrer is impossible. His existence of that act is better for him than [؟]. And as for the explanation of the second premise—namely, that everyone who lacks it is deficient—its proof is that if he does that act, that superiority occurs, and if he does not do it, it does not occur, so he is deficient in himself, becoming complete by another. It should not be said: Why is it not permissible to say that the act and the omission, although equal for him, except that the act is more beneficial for others than omission, and this agent prefers the act not because it is more beneficial for him but because it is beneficence to others and conveying benefit to others? For we say: Beneficence to others and its omission—if they are equal for him, preference is impossible; and if they are not equal, then beneficence to others is better for him, so beneficence to others becomes a cause for his completion and its omission becomes a cause for his deficiency, so the mentioned problem returns.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the first philosophical proof against God having will. It begins by stating the argument: whenever someone intends to produce something, that act must be considered better in his knowledge than its omission; before the act he is deficient, and through the act he becomes complete. This cannot apply to God. The author then explains the first premise: if the agent did not see the act as better, he would be indifferent and unable to prefer it. The second premise: if he performs the act, he attains the better; if not, he remains deficient. Then an objection is raised: perhaps the agent prefers the act because it benefits others, not himself. The answer is that if benefiting others and its omission are equal, there is no preference; if benefiting others is better, then that becomes the agent's own betterment, again implying deficiency. Thus the original difficulty remains.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That will necessarily entails need or change in God.
Correction: The argument assumes that intending something requires the act to be better for the agent, but it does not prove that God's will implies deficiency because the same logic applies even if the motive is altruistic, showing the flaw is in the assumption.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' first argument, what does the intention to create something imply about the agent before the act?
A) He is complete
B) He is indifferent
C) He is deficient
D) He is eternal

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument that will implies deficiency fails because the same reasoning applies even if the motive is altruistic, showing the assumption is flawed.

?
Think of a time you felt inadequate or 'lacking' before achieving a goal. How does knowing that God is free from all deficiency change your perspective on your own struggles? Does it make you more grateful or more anxious about your own incompleteness?

The Second Philosophers' Argument Against God's Will
Razi presents the second argument that God's will would be temporal, leading to infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that if God were willing, His will would have to be temporal, leading to impossible consequences, and the rebuttal to the objection that God's knowledge is similar.

Core Idea
Intention to create an act can only exist at the moment of the act itself.

This lesson presents the second major philosophical argument against the doctrine that God has a will. The philosophers argue that if God willed, His will would be a temporal accident, requiring another will ad infinitum. Razi then refutes an attempted analogy between knowledge and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's argument sink in before dissecting it.
This is a dense passage; take your time.
2
Understand the main entailment
Identify the core claim: intention to produce an act can only occur at the moment of the act. Notice the example about the person in a dark room.
The example clarifies that determination (azm) is not the same as intention (qasd).
3
Trace the regress argument
Follow the reasoning: if God's will is temporal, it needs another will to create it, leading to circularity or infinite regress.
This is the classic argument against temporal divine attributes.
4
Examine the objection and response
Read the objection about God's knowledge being temporal. Razi responds by distinguishing knowledge (follows the known) from will (precedes the act).
This highlights a key difference between divine knowledge and will.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Synthesize: The philosophers conclude God cannot be willing because a temporal will leads to impossible consequences.
But note that Razi himself does not necessarily endorse this argument; he is presenting it as a challenge to be answered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية لهم قالوا : لو كان الله تعالى مريدا لكان مريدا بإرادة محدثة، وهذا محال، فذلك محال. أما بيان الملازمة فهو أن القصد إلى الإيجاد يمتنع حصوله إلا عند حصول ذلك الإيجاد فأما قبل حصول ذلك الإيجاد فذلك لا يكون قصدًا إلى الإيجاد بل يكون ذلك عزمًا على أنه سيوجد في الوقت الفلاني. لا يقال : لم لا يجوز أن يكون العزم على أنه سيفعل غدًا يكون نفس القصد إلى الإيجاد عند حضور الغد؟ لأنا نقول : إن من عزم على أن يفعل بكرة الغد ثم جلس في بيت مظلم لا يميز فيه بين الليل والنهار واستمر ذلك العزم في قلبه إلى أن جاء الغد لكنه لم يعلم مجيء الغد فإنه لا يصير قاصدًا إلى الفعل، ولو كان العزم على الفعل غدًا يكون عين القصد إلى الفعل عند مجيء الغد لصار عند مجيء الغد قاصدًا للفعل، بل إذا كان عازما على الفعل غدًا ثم أحس مجيء الغد تولد من هذا العلم قصد إلى هذا الفعل، فثبت أن القصد إلى إحداث الفعل لا يتحقق إلا حال حدوث الفعل، فثبت أنه تعالى لو كان يفعل الأفعال بالقصد والإرادة لكانت إرادته لا محالة محدثة. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن تكون إرادته محدثة ؛ لأنها لو كانت محدثة لافتقر في خلق تلك الإرادة إلى إرادة أخرى، فيلزم إما الدور، وإما التسلسل، وكل ذلك محال، فثبت أنه يمتنع كونه تعالى مريدا. لا يقال : أليس من الناس من قال : إن علم الله تعالى بالمتغيرات متغير متجدد؟ وقال : إن ذاته تعالى توجب العلم بذلك المعلوم عند حدوث ذلك المعلوم، فلا جرم استغنى حدوث ذلك العلم عن علم آخر، فلم لا يجوز مثله في الإرادة ؟ لأنا نقول : العلم بالشيء تبع لوقوع ذلك الشيء، فإذا حدث ذلك الشيء أمكن أن يقال : ذاته تعالى توجب ذلك العلم بشرط وقوع ذلك المعلوم، أما إرادة الوقوع فمؤثرة في الوقوع ومتقدمة عليه، فيمتنع أن يقال : إن ذاته تعالى توجب إرادة الوقوع بشرط الوقوع، وإذا لم يمكن وقوع المراد شرطا لكون ذاته موجبا لحدوث الإرادة لم يبق إلا أن يقال: إنه تعالى أحدث تلك الإرادة على سبيل الاختيار، وحينئذ يلزم التسلسل، فظهر الفرق بين العلم والإرادة.
The second doubt: They said: If God, exalted, were willing, He would be willing with a temporal will, and this is impossible, so that is impossible. As for the explanation of the entailment: that intention to produce cannot occur except at the occurrence of that production. For before the occurrence of that production, that is not intention to produce but rather it is a determination that it will exist at a certain time. It should not be said: Why is it not permissible that the determination to do tomorrow be the very intention to produce when tomorrow arrives? For we say: Whoever determines to do tomorrow, then sits in a dark room where he cannot distinguish between night and day, and that determination continues in his heart until tomorrow comes, but he does not know that tomorrow has come, he does not become an intender of the act. And if the determination to do tomorrow were exactly the intention to do when tomorrow arrives, then when tomorrow arrives he would become an intender of the act. Rather, if he is determined to do tomorrow then perceives the arrival of tomorrow, there is generated from this knowledge an intention to this act. Thus it is established that intention to produce an act does not occur except at the moment of the act's occurrence. Thus it is established that if He, exalted, were to do acts by intention and will, His will would necessarily be temporal. And we say: It is impossible that His will be temporal, because if it were temporal, it would need another will to create that will, leading to either circularity or infinite regress, and all that is impossible. Thus it is established that it is impossible for Him, exalted, to be willing. It should not be said: Is there not among people one who says that God's knowledge of changing things is changing and renewed? And he says: His essence, exalted, necessitates knowledge of that known thing at the occurrence of that known thing, so the occurrence of that knowledge does not need another knowledge. So why is the like not permissible in will? For we say: Knowledge of a thing follows the occurrence of that thing; when that thing occurs, it can be said that His essence necessitates that knowledge on condition of the occurrence of that known. As for the will for occurrence, it is effective in the occurrence and precedes it, so it is impossible to say that His essence necessitates the will for occurrence on condition of the occurrence. And if it is not possible for the occurrence of the desired to be a condition for His essence necessitating the occurrence of will, nothing remains but to say that He, exalted, caused that will to occur by choice, and then infinite regress follows. Thus the difference between knowledge and will becomes clear.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophical argument against God having will. The argument states: if God were willing, His will would be temporal (hādith), but a temporal will is impossible because it would require another will to create it, leading to circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The explanation of the entailment: intention to produce an act can only occur at the moment of the act; before that, it is merely determination. An objection is raised: can't determination be the same as intention when the time arrives? The answer is no, using the example of a person who resolves to do something tomorrow but remains unaware of time; he does not become an intender until he actually knows the time has come. Thus, intention occurs only at the act. Therefore, if God acted by intention, His will would be temporal. Then an objection is raised about the possibility of God's knowledge being temporal (as some held), and why will cannot be similar. The answer distinguishes knowledge from will: knowledge follows the known, so God's essence can necessitate knowledge upon the condition of the occurrence of the known. But will precedes and causes the occurrence; thus it cannot be conditioned by the occurrence. Hence, if will is temporal, it requires another will ad infinitum. This refutes the analogy between knowledge and will.

Key Terms
إرادة محدثة
دور
تسلسل
علم
تبعية
قصد الإيجاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will could be temporal like His knowledge without leading to regress.
Correction: Knowledge follows the known; will precedes and causes the act. Therefore will cannot be temporal without requiring another will, unlike knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the main consequence the philosophers draw from the idea that God's will would be temporal?
A) It would be eternal
B) It would require another will, leading to circularity or infinite regress
C) It would prove the world's eternity
D) It would necessitate God's knowledge being temporal

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that a temporal divine will leads to an infinite regress, and the analogy with temporal knowledge fails because will precedes the act while knowledge follows it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will were temporary and changing like yours, how would your trust in His eternal decree be shaken? Do you live as though God's will is settled or as if it shifts with your circumstances? What would change in your life if you truly believed His will is unchanging?
?
The passage shows that a firm determination (azm) does not become actual intention (qasd) without knowledge of the time's arrival. How often do you make good intentions but fail to act when the moment comes? What does that reveal about your own will compared to God's perfect will that always accomplishes what it intends?
?
The philosophers argued that a temporal will leads to an infinite regress, making it impossible for God to have a will at all. When you worry about the future as if there is no fixed divine will, are you not behaving as though God's will is subject to the same regress? How can reflecting on God's eternal, effective will free you from anxiety about tomorrow?

The Dilemma of God's Will and Creation
Razi presents a dilemma against God's will for creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that God's will for creation leads to an impossible dilemma, and how Razi presents this argument.

Core Idea
God's will for creation faces an irresolvable dilemma: eternal will or a regress.

This passage presents the third philosophical argument against God having will. It poses a dilemma: if God willed to create the world, either He willed it eternally or at a specific time. Both alternatives lead to absurdities—eternal will implies a pre-eternal world, undermining the purpose of creation, while a specific time leads to an infinite regress. Imam Razi here challenges the philosophers' assumption.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma structure
The argument begins by setting up a dilemma: if God wills creation, either He wills it at all times (eternally) or at a particular time. Read the opening sentence to identify this choice.
Notice the Arabic phrase 'إما ... أو ...' signaling the two alternatives.
2
Analyze the first horn
Examine the first alternative: eternal will. What does it imply? According to the text, it leads to the pre-eternity of the world, making the intention to bring into existence impossible. Why is that absurd?
The key phrase: 'قصد الإيجاد إلى الموجود محال'.
3
Analyze the second horn
Now look at the second alternative: will directed at a specific time. The text argues that this leads to an infinite regress because the specific time itself must be created, requiring another time, and so on. Trace this reasoning step by step.
Watch for the term 'التسلسل' (infinite regress) which is deemed impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لو كان الباري تعالى مريدا لخلق العالم لكان إما يريد خلق العالم في جميع الأوقات أزلا وأبدًا، أو يريد تخصيص خلق العالم بوقت معين، والأول يقتضي قدم العالم، وإذا كان العالم قديما دائما موجودا امتنع القصد إلى الإيجاد ؛ لأن القصد إلى إيجاد الموجود محال، وهذا القسم يفضي ثبوته إلى عدمه، فيكون باطلا. والثاني أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن ذلك الوقت ما كان موجودًا في الأزل، وإلا عاد التقسيم الأول، وهو أن ذلك الوقت قد حدث بعد أن لم يكن، فيعود التقسيم الأول أنه تعالى إما أن يقال: إنه أراد خلق ذلك الوقت أزلا وأبدًا أو أراد خلقه في وقت معين، والأول يلزم منه القدم، والثاني يلزم منه اشتراط كل وقت بوقت آخر، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال.
The third doubt: If the Eternal One, exalted, were willing to create the world, then either He wills to create the world at all times eternally and forever, or He wills to specify the creation of the world at a particular time. The first entails the pre-eternity of the world, and if the world is pre-eternal and always existing, then intention to bring into existence is impossible, because intention to bring into existence what is already existent is impossible. This case leads from its affirmation to its negation, so it is false. The second is also false, because that time did not exist in pre-eternity; otherwise the first division would return, namely that that time occurred after not being, so the first division returns: either it is said that He, exalted, willed to create that time eternally and forever, or that He willed to create it at a particular time. The first entails pre-eternity, and the second entails the condition of each time by another time, and infinite regress follows, and it is impossible.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the third philosophical argument against God having will. It poses a dilemma: if God willed to create the world, either He willed it eternally (always) or at a specific time. The first alternative implies the world is eternal; but then intending to bring into existence what already exists is absurd. The second alternative leads to an infinite regress: the specific time itself is created, so either it was willed eternally (again implying eternity) or at another time, and so on ad infinitum, which is impossible. Thus, God cannot be willing to create the world.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
قصد الإيجاد
وقت معين
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will for creation can be directed to a specific time without further regress.
Correction: The argument shows that any specific time must itself be created, leading to an infinite regress of times, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
In the third argument, what follows from the alternative that God wills to create the world at all times eternally?
A) The world would be temporal
B) The world would be pre-eternal, making intention to create impossible
C) The world would be created at a specific time
D) An infinite regress would follow

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument that God's will for creation leads to an impossible dilemma, with both horns refutable, is presented by Razi as a challenge to the attribute of will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument suggests that speaking of God's will in temporal terms leads to contradiction. When you pray and ask God for something at a specific time, do you sense a disconnect between your temporal request and God's eternal will? How do you personally reconcile that in your heart?
?
If you were to face an atheist who uses this dilemma to deny God's will, how would your conviction in God's attributes respond? Does this argument shake your belief, or do you have a firm answer? What is that answer?
?
Razi presents this as a doubt from the philosophers. After reading it, do you feel a need to deepen your understanding of how God's will relates to time and creation? What concrete step will you take to seek that understanding?

The Difference Between Knowledge and Will in God
Razi defends his view by distinguishing divine will from divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine will must precede its object while divine knowledge follows its object, and why this distinction prevents an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Divine will precedes what it wills; divine knowledge follows what it knows.

Razi addresses a potential objection: if some theologians hold that God's knowledge of changing things changes, then why can't His will also change? The answer lies in a fundamental distinction between the functions of knowledge and will. This passage clarifies that distinction and shows why it preserves God's sovereignty without leading to regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully, noting the analogy between knowledge and will that the opponent tries to draw.
2
Analyze Razi's distinction
Razi distinguishes: knowledge follows its object, will precedes and causes its object. Find the Arabic phrases that express this.
Key phrases: 'العلم بالشيء تبع لوقوعه', 'إرادة الوقوع مؤثرة في الوقوع ومتقدمة عليه'.
3
Reflect on the consequence
Understand why this distinction prevents an infinite regress. If will were conditioned on its object, we would need a prior will - ad infinitum.
This shows the unique nature of divine will as a direct attribute of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : أليس من الناس من قال : إن علم الله تعالى بالمتغيرات متغير متجدد؟ وقال : إن ذاته تعالى توجب العلم بذلك المعلوم عند حدوث ذلك المعلوم، فلا جرم استغنى حدوث ذلك العلم عن علم آخر، فلم لا يجوز مثله في الإرادة ؟ لأنا نقول : العلم بالشيء تبع لوقوع ذلك الشيء، فإذا حدث ذلك الشيء أمكن أن يقال : ذاته تعالى توجب ذلك العلم بشرط وقوع ذلك المعلوم، أما إرادة الوقوع فمؤثرة في الوقوع ومتقدمة عليه، فيمتنع أن يقال : إن ذاته تعالى توجب إرادة الوقوع بشرط الوقوع، وإذا لم يمكن وقوع المراد شرطا لكون ذاته موجبا لحدوث الإرادة لم يبق إلا أن يقال: إنه تعالى أحدث تلك الإرادة على سبيل الاختيار، وحينئذ يلزم التسلسل، فظهر الفرق بين العلم والإرادة.
It cannot be said: Is there not among people one who said that God's knowledge of changing things is changing and renewed? And he said: His essence necessitates knowledge of that known thing at the occurrence of that known thing, so that the occurrence of that knowledge does not require another knowledge. Then why is the like not permissible in will? We say: Knowledge of a thing is consequent upon its occurrence; so when that thing occurs, it is possible to say: His essence necessitates that knowledge on condition of the occurrence of that known object. As for the will for occurrence, it is effective in occurrence and precedes it; so it is impossible to say: His essence necessitates the will for occurrence on condition of the occurrence. And when it is impossible for the occurrence of the willed object to be a condition for His essence being necessitating of the occurrence of the will, nothing remains but to say that He, exalted, originated that will by way of choice, and then there would be an infinite regress. Thus the difference between knowledge and will becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector raises a potential counterexample: some people say God's knowledge of changing things is itself changing, so why cannot His will be similarly temporal? The answer draws a sharp distinction: knowledge is consequent upon its object (العلم بالشيء تبع لوقوعه), so when the object occurs, God's essence knows it without need for another knowledge. Will, however, is effective in bringing about the object and precedes it (إرادة الوقوع مؤثرة في الوقوع ومتقدمة عليه). Therefore, will cannot be conditioned on the occurrence; if it were, we would need another will for its own occurrence, leading to an infinite regress. Thus the two attributes differ fundamentally.

Key Terms
علم
إرادة
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's knowledge of change is itself changing, then His will could also be changing and temporal like knowledge.
Correction: Razi shows that knowledge follows its object, so it can be conditioned on the object's occurrence without problem. Will, however, must precede its object, so it cannot be similarly conditioned; otherwise there would be an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between divine knowledge and divine will according to Razi in this passage?
A) Knowledge is eternal while will is temporal.
B) Knowledge follows the object while will precedes it.
C) Knowledge is from God's essence while will is from His action.
D) Both are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Divine will must precede its object to avoid infinite regress, whereas divine knowledge follows its object without such consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will necessarily precedes every event, including your own choices, does that make you feel trapped or liberated? What does that imply about your responsibility for actions you will tomorrow?
?
Consider a moment you desperately wanted something to happen, but it did not. Razi says God's will is effective and precedes events, not dependent on their occurrence. How does that truth challenge your assumption that God 'owes' you an outcome?
?
The passage contrasts knowledge (which follows) and will (which precedes). In your life, when you learn about a past sin, do you treat God's knowledge as mere information, or do you let it shape your repentance, knowing His will for that sin preceded it?

The Third Doubt: God's Will Leads to Pre-Eternity or Regress
Razi presents the third doubt and its answer regarding God's will for the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma about God's will for the world and how Razi resolves it by denying the need for a prior time.

Core Idea
God's will for a specific time is not trapped in infinite regression.

Razi presents a powerful objection against those who claim God creates the world at a specific moment. The doubt argues that either God's will is eternal (making the world eternal) or temporal (leading to infinite regress). Razi's answer cuts the knot by asserting that the specific time itself does not require another time for its creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two options presented in the first sentence.
2
Identify the two horns
The doubt presents two possibilities: either God wills the world eternally, or He wills it at a specific time. See how each leads to a problem.
The first horn makes the world eternal, the second leads to regress.
3
Examine the answer
Razi's answer is short: the specific time's creation is not dependent on another time, so the will for it does not need another time. Understand why this stops the regress.
The key point is that time is not a dependent entity in this context.
4
Reflect on the logic
Consider why the doubt is compelling and why the answer works. This illustrates a central issue in kalām: how to conceptualize divine will without falling into absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : لو كان الباري تعالى مريدا لخلق العالم لكان إما يريد خلق العالم في جميع الأوقات أزلا وأبدًا، أو يريد تخصيص خلق العالم بوقت معين، والأول يقتضي قدم العالم، وإذا كان العالم قديما دائما موجودا امتنع القصد إلى الإيجاد ؛ لأن القصد إلى إيجاد الموجود محال، وهذا القسم يفضي ثبوته إلى عدمه، فيكون باطلا. والثاني أيضًا باطل ؛ لأن ذلك الوقت ما كان موجودًا في الأزل، وإلا عاد التقسيم الأول، وهو أن ذلك الوقت قد حدث بعد أن لم يكن، فيعود التقسيم الأول أنه تعالى إما أن يقال: إنه أراد خلق ذلك الوقت أزلا وأبدًا أو أراد خلقه في وقت معين، والأول يلزم منه القدم، والثاني يلزم منه اشتراط كل وقت بوقت آخر، ويلزم التسلسل، وهو محال. والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أنه لما كان إيجاد هذا الزمان المعين غير موقوف على زمان آخر فلم لا يجوز أن تكون إرادة إحداثه لا تفتقر إلى زمان آخر ؟
The third doubt: If the Creator wills the creation of the world, then either He wills the creation of the world in all times eternally and forever, or He wills the specification of the creation of the world to a particular time. The first entails the pre-eternity of the world, and if the world is eternal, always existing, then the intention to bring into existence is impossible, because intending to bring about an existent is absurd. This division leads its affirmation to negation, so it is false. The second is also false, because that time did not exist in pre-eternity; otherwise the first division would return, i.e., that time came to be after not being. Then the first division returns: that He, exalted, either willed the creation of that time eternally and forever, or willed its creation at a particular time. The first entails pre-eternity, and the second entails the condition of each time by another time, and an infinite regress follows, which is impossible. The answer to the third doubt: Since the bringing into existence of this specific time is not dependent on another time, why is it not permissible that the will for its occurrence does not need another time?
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the third doubt and its answer. The doubt argues that if God will create the world at a specific time, either He wills it always (eternally) or at that particular time. The first makes the world eternal, eliminating the need to create it. The second leads to an infinite regress because that time itself requires a prior time. The answer denies that the specific time's creation depends on another time; thus the will for it does not need another time.

Key Terms
إرادة
قدم
تسلسل
زمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills at a time, that time itself requires a will leading to infinite regress.
Correction: Razi argues that the specific time's creation is not dependent on another time, so the will for it does not require a prior time, thus avoiding regress.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the third doubt and its answer?
A) God cannot will because willing eternally makes the world eternal.
B) God's will must be eternal.
C) God's will for a specific time is possible because that time's creation does not require another time.
D) God has no will.

Key Takeaway
The third doubt is resolved by recognizing that the specific time of creation does not require a prior time, thus avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's will for your life, do you sometimes doubt that He can act at a specific time without needing a perfect chain of prior causes? How does this lesson's answer challenge your impatience or anxiety about God's timing?

If the specific time of creation does not depend on another time, then God's will is not trapped in an infinite regress. Are there areas where you treat God's will as if it were limited by time, like your own? What would it mean for you to trust that God's will is direct and free, not needing your careful planning?
?
The doubt assumes that God's will must either be eternal or temporal, but the answer shows a third way: the will is attached to a specific time without requiring a prior time. How often do you fall into false binaries in your understanding of God's attributes? Commit to examining one such assumption today.

The Fourth Doubt: God's Will Cannot Be Eternal or Temporal
Razi presents the fourth doubt: God's will cannot be eternal or temporal, with two arguments against eternal will and one against temporal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that God's will cannot be eternal or temporal, and how this leads to the conclusion that God's agency is not dependent on will.

Core Idea
The fourth doubt aims to disprove divine will by showing both eternal and temporal will lead to absurdities.

Razi presents the fourth doubt against affirming divine will. He argues that if God wills, His will must be either eternal or temporal—both of which entail impossible consequences. Understanding this doubt clarifies the nature of God's agency and the limits of human reasoning about divine attributes. This lesson walks through each argument and the final conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the doubt and its two branches
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Razi frames the doubt: God's will must be either eternal or temporal, both claimed to be impossible.
Pay attention to the phrase 'القسمان محالان' (both divisions are impossible).
2
Understand the first argument against eternal will
Follow Razi's first reason: if the will is eternal, the action at the specific time becomes a necessary consequence, making God a necessitating cause rather than a free agent.
The key point is that a necessary consequent makes the agent not free.
3
Understand the second argument against eternal will
Examine the second reason: once the action occurs, the will no longer relates to it, so an eternal relation would imply the non-existence of the eternal.
This is a subtle point about the timing of will and its object.
4
Grasp the argument against temporal will and the conclusion
The answer is a reference to an earlier proof, but the main point is the impossibility of both types of will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : لو كان الباري تعالى مريدا لإحداث العالم لكانت تلك الإرادة إما أن تكون قديمة أو محدثة، والقسمان محالان، فثبوت الإرادة في حق الله تعالى محال. إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون مريدا بإرادة قديمة لوجهين : الأول : أن على هذا التقدير يكون حصول الفعل في ذلك الوقت المنعين من لوازم تلك الإرادة، وتلك الإرادة لكونها قديمة أزلية ممتنعة التغير والزوال، ولازم اللازم لازم، فيكون عدم وقوع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت ممتنعا، وإذا كان ذلك كان الصالح موجبًا بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار، فإذن القول بقدم الإرادة يفضي إلى نفي الإرادة ، والصفة إذا أدت إلى نفي الذات كان القول بتلك الصفة باطلا، فبطل القول يكون تلك الإرادة قديمة. الثاني : أن بدخول ذلك الفعل في الوجوب، لا تبقى الإرادة متعلقة بإيجاده؛ لأن إيجاد الموجود محال، فلو كان ذلك التعلق قديما لزم عدم القديم، وهو محال . فثبت بهذين الوجهين أنه يمتنع كونه تعالى مريدا بإرادة قديمة. وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كونه تعالى مريدا بإرادة محدثة لما ذكرنا أنه يلزم التسلسل في الإرادات، ولما بطل القسمان ثبت أن فاعلية الله تعالى غير موقوفة على الإرادة. والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة ما قدمناه في مسألة إثبات القدرة.
The fourth doubt: If the Creator wills the creation of the world, then that will is either eternal or temporal. Both divisions are impossible, so affirming will for God is impossible. We say: It is not permissible that He be willing with an eternal will for two reasons. First: On this estimation, the occurrence of the action at that specific time would be a consequence of that will, and that will, being eternal, is impossible to change or cease. The consequent of a necessary consequent is necessary, so the non-occurrence of that action at that time would be impossible. If that is so, then the Agent would be a necessitating cause by essence, not a free agent. So affirming the eternity of will leads to negation of will, and an attribute that leads to negation of the essence makes the affirmation of that attribute false. Thus the claim that that will is eternal is false. Second: With the entry of that action into existence, the will is no longer related to its bringing into existence, because bringing the existent into existence is absurd. So if that relation were eternal, the non-existence of the eternal would be entailed, which is absurd. Thus it is established by these two points that it is impossible for Him to be willing with an eternal will. And we say: It is impossible for Him to be willing with a temporal will for the reason we mentioned that an infinite regress of wills would follow. And since both divisions are false, it is established that God's agency is not dependent on will. The answer to the fourth doubt: What we have already presented in the issue of proving power.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth doubt and its answer. The doubt argues that God's will must be either eternal or temporal; both are impossible. Then it gives two reasons against eternal will: (1) It would make the action necessary, negating free choice; (2) After the action, the will becomes irrelevant, so if eternal, the non-existence of the eternal would follow. Against temporal will, it repeats the infinite regress argument. The answer simply refers to an earlier proof on power.

Key Terms
إرادة قديمة
إرادة محدثة
تسلسل
فاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that an eternal will is necessary for God and implies free agency, but Razi shows it would make God a necessitating cause.
Correction: An eternal will would make the action necessary, negating free choice; thus eternal will cannot be attributed to God.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the possibility of an eternal will?
A) Because it would make God's will changeable.
B) Because it would make the action necessary and after the action the will becomes redundant.
C) Because eternal things cannot have attributes.
D) Because the will would be temporal by definition.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that both eternal and temporal will are impossible, concluding that God's agency is not dependent on will as humans understand it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classification of Divine Will: Negative or Positive
Razi classifies the attribute of will as negative or positive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute of divine will can be interpreted either as a negative attribute (absence of coercion) or as a positive attribute (further subdivided into essential or derived).

Core Idea
Divine will is either a negative or positive attribute, with subdivisions.

In this opening of the third chapter, Razi presents a classification that frames the debate over the nature of God's will. He introduces two broad camps: those who interpret 'will' as a negative attribute (absence of being overpowered) and those who take it as a positive attribute, which then splits into essential and derived. This sets the stage for the detailed schools that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Note the technical terms used.
The passage is incomplete but presents a clear classification.
2
Identify the classification

Look for the key terms 'صفة سلبية' and 'صفة ثبوتية'.
3
Reflect on the two possible interpretations
Consider why some scholars (al-Najjar) would interpret 'will' negatively, and why others interpret it positively. What difference does it make?
Think about how these interpretations affect the understanding of God's attributes.

As for the claim that it is a negative matter, this is what is transmitted from al-Najjar, that he said: 'The meaning of His being willing is that He is not overpowered nor conquered.' And as for those who interpreted it as positive, that meaning is either grounded in His essence or derived from a meaning.

Commentary
This passage is a classification at the start of the third chapter. It states that the attribute of will can be understood negatively or positively. The negative interpretation, attributed to al-Najjar, is that God being willing means He is not subdued. The positive interpretation is then subdivided: either it is essential (grounded in God's essence) or derived (from another meaning). The passage is incomplete but sets up the discussion.

Two Proofs Against an Eternal Will for God
Razi gives two proofs that God's will cannot be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two arguments against the possibility of an eternal will for God and why an eternal will would negate free choice and the very concept of will.

Core Idea
An eternal will would eliminate God's free choice, thus negating will itself.

Razi tackles a subtle theological problem: if God's will is eternal, does that mean everything He wills must happen necessarily, stripping Him of choice? In this passage, he presents two concise but powerful proofs showing that an eternal will leads to absurdities. Let's work through them step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its flow. Notice that Razi first states the impossibility of will being eternal or originated, then gives two reasons against an eternal will.
The passage uses technical terms like 'lāzim' (necessary consequence).
2
Trace the first proof
The first proof argues that an eternal will would make the act necessary at a specific time, eliminating choice and turning God into a necessitator, which negates will. Identify how Razi uses the concept of 'necessary of necessary'.
3
Trace the second proof
The second proof observes that after an act exists, the will to bring it about cannot remain attached to it. If that attachment were eternal, it would require bringing into existence something already existent, which is impossible.
4
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that both proofs establish that an eternal will is impossible for God. Reflect on why this matters for divine attribute theology.
Think about how this connects to God's freedom and choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن تكون قديمة أو محدثة، والقسمان محالان، فثبوت الإرادة في حق الله تعالى محال. إنما قلنا : إنه لا يجوز أن يكون مريدا بإرادة قديمة لوجهين : الأول : أن على هذا التقدير يكون حصول الفعل في ذلك الوقت المنعين من لوازم تلك الإرادة، وتلك الإرادة لكونها قديمة أزلية ممتنعة التغير والزوال، ولازم اللازم لازم، فيكون عدم وقوع ذلك الفعل في ذلك الوقت ممتنعا، وإذا كان ذلك كان الصالح موجبًا بالذات لا فاعلا بالاختيار، فإذن القول بقدم الإرادة يفضي إلى نفي الإرادة ، والصفة إذا أدت إلى نفي الذات كان القول بتلك الصفة باطلا، فبطل القول يكون تلك الإرادة قديمة. الثاني : أن بدخول ذلك الفعل في الوجوب، لا تبقى الإرادة متعلقة بإيجاده؛ لأن إيجاد الموجود محال، فلو كان ذلك التعلق قديما لزم عدم القديم، وهو محال . فثبت بهذين الوجهين أنه يمتنع كونه تعالى مريدا بإرادة قديمة.
That it be eternal or originated, and both divisions are impossible, so the affirmation of will in respect of God is impossible. We say: it is not permissible that He be willing with an eternal will for two aspects: First: that on this estimation, the occurrence of the act at that specific time would be from the necessities of that will, and that will, because it is eternal and pre-eternal, is impossible of change and cessation, and the necessary of the necessary is necessary, so the non-occurrence of that act at that time would be impossible, and if that were so, then the One who is good would be a necessitator by essence, not an agent by choice. So then, the statement of the eternity of will leads to the negation of will, and when an attribute leads to the negation of the essence, the statement of that attribute is false, so the statement that that will is eternal is false. Second: that by the entering of that act into existence, the will does not remain attached to its bringing into existence, because bringing into existence what already exists is impossible. So if that attachment were eternal, the non-existence of the eternal would follow, which is impossible. So it is established by these two aspects that it is impossible for God Most High to be willing with an eternal will.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs against the possibility of God having an eternal will. Razi begins by stating that any positing of will for God must be either eternal or originated, and both are impossible. He then focuses on showing the impossibility of an eternal will with two detailed arguments. The first argument uses the concept of necessity: if the will is eternal, then what is willed must necessarily occur at the specific time intended. Since the will is eternal and unchanging, the non-occurrence of the act becomes impossible. This would eliminate choice and make God a mere necessitator, which contradicts the very idea of will, thus negating the attribute. The second argument observes that once an act comes into existence, the will to bring it about can no longer be attached to it. If the attachment were eternal, it would require that the already-existent act still be willed into existence, which is absurd. Therefore, an eternal will is impossible.

Key Terms
قديمة (eternal)
محدثة (originated)
إرادة (will)
لازم (necessary consequence)
موجب بالذات (necessitator by essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think an eternal will is necessary for God's knowledge and power.
Correction: Razi shows an eternal will would negate choice and lead to absurdities, so it cannot be an attribute of God.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does an eternal will lead to?
A) It affirms God's choice.
B) It necessitates the act eternally and negates free choice.
C) It allows for temporal acts.
D) It is the only logical possibility.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that an eternal will for God is impossible because it would necessitate the act and negate choice, and because will cannot attach to an already existent act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something you prayed for does not happen at the time you expected, do you ever treat God as if His will is an unchangeable script that leaves no room for timing? How does Razi's first proof—that an eternal will would necessitate a fixed time—challenge you to trust that God's will is free and wise in its timing?
?

?
Think of a time you used the phrase 'it was God's will' without careful thought. Did you imply that God was a mere necessitator? How can Razi's distinction between a necessitator by essence and an agent by choice reshape the way you speak about divine decree in your daily life?

God's Agency Beyond Temporal Will
Razi's concluding argument against a temporal will for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi argues that a temporal will leads to infinite regress and how he concludes that God's agency is independent of will.

Core Idea
A temporal will for God leads to an infinite regress, so agency stands alone.

In this concluding part of his argument, Razi completes his refutation of the notion that God acts through a will. Having earlier shown that an eternal will is impossible, he now demonstrates that a temporal will is equally impossible because it would require an endless chain of wills. The result is a bold theological claim: God's agency is not dependent on any will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi presents a single, condensed argument.
This passage is the conclusion of a longer discussion.
2
Identify the two rejected possibilities
Razi mentions 'the two divisions are nullified'. Recall that the two divisions are eternal will (rejected earlier) and temporal will (rejected here).
The two divisions exhaust all possibilities for a will.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that God's agency is not dependent on will. Consider what this means for God's freedom and power.
This is a distinctive Ash'ari position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع كونه تعالى مريدا بإرادة محدثة لما ذكرنا أنه يلزم التسلسل في الإرادات، ولما بطل القسمان ثبت أن فاعلية الله تعالى غير موقوفة على الإرادة.
And we say: it is impossible for God Most High to be willing with a temporal will because of what we mentioned: that an infinite regress in wills would follow. And when the two divisions are nullified, it is established that the agency of God Most High is not dependent on will.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This very brief passage completes the argument that began in the previous lesson. Having shown that an eternal will is impossible, Razi now argues that a temporal will is also impossible because it would lead to an infinite regress: the will to create an act would itself need a will, and so on. Since both possibilities are exhausted and both are impossible, the conclusion is that God's agency (fa'iliyya) is not dependent on any will whatsoever. This is a strong Ash'ari position that emphasizes God's absolute freedom and power without requiring a separate attribute of will.

Key Terms
محدثة
إرادة
تسلسل
فاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must have a will in order to act or create.
Correction: Razi argues that a will, whether eternal or temporal, leads to rational impossibility. Therefore, God's agency is not dependent on a will; God acts directly and freely without needing a separate attribute of will.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject a temporal will for God?
A) It would make God dependent on creation.
B) It leads to infinite regress in wills.
C) It contradicts eternal attribute.
D) It is not mentioned in revelation.

Key Takeaway
God's agency is not dependent on a will, whether eternal or temporal, as both lead to impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s agency does not depend on any will, then His actions are not preceded by deliberation or change. How does this truth challenge your impulse to doubt God when your prayers seem unanswered? Will you now trust that His actions are perfect and unchangeable, even when you do not understand them?
?
Razi’s argument removes any notion of God ‘changing His mind’ or being influenced by events. Examine your heart: do you ever pray as if you can persuade God to alter His decrees? How does this lesson demand that you purify your intention in supplication, making it an act of submission rather than negotiation?
?
This passage teaches that God’s will is not like human will—it is not a faculty that can be described as ‘temporal’ or ‘eternal’. Are you willing to let go of anthropomorphic ideas about God, even if they give you a comforting sense of familiarity? What specific habit of thinking about God must you change because of this lesson?
