Answer to the First Objection on Divine Will
Razi refutes the first objection by referencing an earlier argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that a willer must have a reason to prefer one option over another.

Core Idea
Preference without reason is possible, as shown by the case of two identical options.

The first objection against Razi's denial of a separate divine will claims that any willer must have a reason—knowledge, opinion, or belief—to prefer one of two identical options. Razi dismisses this by pointing to his earlier discussion of cases where no distinguishing reason exists, such as two loaves or two paths. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
First read the Arabic and translation of the first objection and Razi's response. Notice how he does not provide a new proof but refers back to an earlier discussion.
The earlier discussion is a classic kalam argument about preference without a reason.
2
Understand the counterexample
Razi mentions 'the two arrows, two loaves, two paths'—these are scenarios where a person can choose one without any reason, showing that preference does not require a reason.
3
Apply the refutation to divine will
Recognize that if humans can choose without reason, then God, who is all-powerful, can certainly will without needing a reason. Thus the objection is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: قوله : 'المريد لا يرجح أحد الطرفين على الآخر إلا إذا كان أحد الطرفين أولى به في علمه أو ظنه أو اعتقاده' قلنا: هذا مدفوع، ودليله ما ذكرنا في مسألة القدحين والرغيفين والطريقين. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن إرادة إيقاع الفعل عند مجيء القصد لإيقاع الفعل عند مجيء الغد، والكلام فيه عين ما ذكرناه في مسألة أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. الغد هو نفس والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أنه لما كان إيجاد هذا الزمان المعين غير موقوف على زمان آخر فلم لا يجوز أن تكون إرادة ،إحداثه لا تفتقر إلى زمان آخر ؟ والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة ما قدمناه في مسألة إثبات القدرة.
And the answer to the first doubt: his statement: 'The willer does not prefer one of the two sides over the other except if one of the two sides is more appropriate in his knowledge, or his opinion, or his belief.' We say: this is refuted, and its proof is what we mentioned in the issue of the two arrows, the two loaves, and the two paths. And the answer to the second doubt: that the will to perform the act at the coming of the intention is the will to perform the act at the coming of tomorrow, and the discussion regarding it is the same as what we mentioned in the issue that knowledge that something will exist is identical to the knowledge of its existence when it exists. Tomorrow is the same? And the answer to the third doubt: that since the bringing into existence of this specific time is not dependent on another time, why would it not be permissible that the will to originate it not be in need of another time? And the answer to the fourth doubt is what we have already presented in the issue of proving power.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's responses to four objections (shubah) that could be raised against his denial of an attribute of will in God. The first objection claims that a willer must have a reason to prefer one option over another (knowledge, opinion, or belief). Razi dismisses this, referring to his earlier discussion of 'the two arrows, two loaves, two paths' where such preference without a reason is possible. The second objection concerns the temporal coincidence of the will with the act; Razi equates it to the identity of foreknowledge with knowledge when the thing exists. The text here is slightly garbled but the point is clear. The third objection asks why the will to create a specific time cannot be free from another time, given that the creation itself is not time-dependent. Razi implies that the same logic applies. The fourth objection is answered by reference to an earlier proof of God's power. These responses are terse and assume the reader's familiarity with earlier sections of the work.

Key Terms
شبهة (doubt/objection)
ترجيح (preference)
القدحين (two arrows)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A willer must always have a reason to prefer one option over another.
Correction: Razi shows that preference without a reason is possible, using the example of two identical options; humans can choose arbitrarily, and God can do so even more.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the first objection about preference needing a reason?
A) He provides a new logical proof.
B) He says it is refuted by an earlier discussion of two arrows, loaves, and paths.
C) He concedes but says God's knowledge is infinite.
D) He says the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because preference does not require a reason, as shown by scenarios of identical options.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a choice between two equally good options, do you ever feel the need to rationalize your decision after the fact? How does Razi's argument expose your discomfort with the idea that some preferences have no reason? Will you accept that your choices—and God's—can be without a reason you can articulate?
?
If God's will does not require a reason, how does that confront your desire to always understand His decrees? Are you willing to trust Him even when you see no wisdom in what He commands or allows? What would it mean for you to submit without demanding explanations?
?
The objector insists that will without reason is irrational. Are there areas of your life where you demand that God's commands make sense to you before you obey? How can this lesson move you from intellectual objection to wholehearted surrender?

Answer to the Second Objection on Divine Will
Razi answers the second objection by equating will to future act with will at the time of act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's analogy between the will to act at a future time and the identity of foreknowledge and knowledge.

Core Idea
The will to act at a future time is identical to the will to act when the time comes, just as foreknowledge is identical to knowledge at the moment of existence.

The second objection concerns the timing of divine will: if God wills an act to occur tomorrow, does that will exist now or only when the act occurs? Razi's answer draws on his earlier discussion that knowledge of something's future existence is the same as knowledge of its existence when it exists. This lesson explains that concise analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the objection deals with the timing of the will relative to the act. Razi's answer is very condensed.
The Arabic text may have a slight corruption; we work with what is given.
2
Understand the analogy with knowledge
Razi says the discussion here is the same as in the issue that 'knowledge that something will exist is identical to the knowledge of its existence when it exists.' This means the will to do something tomorrow is identical to the will to do it at the moment it is done.
3
Apply to divine will
Just as God's foreknowledge is the same as His knowledge when the event happens, God's will to create at a future time is the same as His will at that time. So there is no temporal gap in the will itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: قوله : 'المريد لا يرجح أحد الطرفين على الآخر إلا إذا كان أحد الطرفين أولى به في علمه أو ظنه أو اعتقاده' قلنا: هذا مدفوع، ودليله ما ذكرنا في مسألة القدحين والرغيفين والطريقين. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن إرادة إيقاع الفعل عند مجيء القصد لإيقاع الفعل عند مجيء الغد، والكلام فيه عين ما ذكرناه في مسألة أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. الغد هو نفس والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أنه لما كان إيجاد هذا الزمان المعين غير موقوف على زمان آخر فلم لا يجوز أن تكون إرادة ،إحداثه لا تفتقر إلى زمان آخر ؟ والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة ما قدمناه في مسألة إثبات القدرة.
And the answer to the first doubt: his statement: 'The willer does not prefer one of the two sides over the other except if one of the two sides is more appropriate in his knowledge, or his opinion, or his belief.' Tomorrow is the same? And the answer to the third doubt: that since the bringing into existence of this specific time is not dependent on another time, why would it not be permissible that the will to originate it not be in need of another time? And the answer to the fourth doubt is what we have already presented in the issue of proving power.

Misconception: God's will to do something tomorrow is a separate act of will from His will at the time of doing it.
Correction: Razi shows that the will is identical across time, just as foreknowledge is identical to knowledge at the moment of existence.

Checkpoint

A) The analogy of two arrows.
B) The analogy that knowledge of a future event is the same as knowledge when it occurs.
C) The analogy of creating time without dependence.
D) The analogy of proving power.

Key Takeaway
The will to act at a future time is identical to the will at that time, just as future knowledge is identical to present knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Answer to the Third Objection on Divine Will
Razi answers the third objection by arguing from creation to will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the will to create a specific time does not need another time.

Core Idea
If creation of a time does not require another time, then the will to create it also needs no time.

The third objection asks: if God's will to create something at a specific time must itself exist at some time, then there would be an infinite regress. Razi counters by noting that the creation of a specific time does not depend on another time, so why should the will to create it be any different? This lesson examines that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic and translation. The objection is presented indirectly; Razi's answer is a rhetorical question that exposes the inconsistency.
2
Analyze the point about time creation
Razi says 'the bringing into existence of this specific time is not dependent on another time.' This means that when God creates a moment, that moment's creation does not require a prior moment. It is immediate.
3
Apply the same to the will
Since the created time is independent, the will that originates it should also be independent of another time. So the will to create at a specific time does not need to be at a different time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الشبهة الأولى: قوله : 'المريد لا يرجح أحد الطرفين على الآخر إلا إذا كان أحد الطرفين أولى به في علمه أو ظنه أو اعتقاده' قلنا: هذا مدفوع، ودليله ما ذكرنا في مسألة القدحين والرغيفين والطريقين. والجواب عن الشبهة الثانية : أن إرادة إيقاع الفعل عند مجيء القصد لإيقاع الفعل عند مجيء الغد، والكلام فيه عين ما ذكرناه في مسألة أن العلم بأن الشيء سيوجد نفس العلم بوجوده إذا وجد. الغد هو نفس والجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة : أنه لما كان إيجاد هذا الزمان المعين غير موقوف على زمان آخر فلم لا يجوز أن تكون إرادة ،إحداثه لا تفتقر إلى زمان آخر ؟ والجواب عن الشبهة الرابعة ما قدمناه في مسألة إثبات القدرة.
And the answer to the first doubt: his statement: 'The willer does not prefer one of the two sides over the other except if one of the two sides is more appropriate in his knowledge, or his opinion, or his belief.' We say: this is refuted, and its proof is what we mentioned in the issue of the two arrows, the two loaves, and the two paths. And the answer to the second doubt: that the will to perform the act at the coming of the intention is the will to perform the act at the coming of tomorrow, and the discussion regarding it is the same as what we mentioned in the issue that knowledge that something will exist is identical to the knowledge of its existence when it exists. Tomorrow is the same? And the answer to the third doubt: that since the bringing into existence of this specific time is not dependent on another time, why would it not be permissible that the will to originate it not be in need of another time? And the answer to the fourth doubt is what we have already presented in the issue of proving power.

Commentary
This passage contains Razi's responses to four objections (shubah) that could be raised against his denial of an attribute of will in God. The first objection claims that a willer must have a reason to prefer one option over another (knowledge, opinion, or belief). زمان (time)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The will to create at a specific time must itself exist at some time.
Correction: Razi shows that just as the creation of a time does not depend on another time, the will to create it also needs no other time.

Checkpoint

B) Because the creation of that time itself does not depend on another time.
C) Because will is timeless.
D) Because the objection is invalid.

Key Takeaway
If creation of a time is independent of another time, then the will to create it is also independent.

?

Answer to the Fourth Objection on Divine Will
Razi answers the fourth objection by referencing a previous proof of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi answers the fourth objection by referring to an earlier proof of God's power.

Core Idea
The fourth objection is refuted by the earlier argument that establishes God's power as an attribute.

The fourth objection is not specified here, but Razi's answer is a direct reference to a previous discussion in the section on proving God's power (qudra). This underscores the interconnected nature of Razi's arguments: the same proofs that establish one attribute also answer objections about another. This lesson highlights that method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

2

3

Razi's method is to build on previous conclusions. This lesson teaches that a well-constructed theological system can handle multiple objections with a few foundational proofs.

Refuting the Negative Definition of Will
Razi refutes al-Najjar's negative definition of will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Najjar's definition of will as 'not being overpowered' is rationally insufficient.

Core Idea
A negative definition cannot capture the positive attribute of will.

Al-Najjar attempted to define God's will in negative terms, equating it with being unvanquished. This seems plausible at first, but Razi shows that it fails because it also applies to non-willing entities. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of Razi's refutation of al-Najjar's negative definition.
2
Identify the flaw
Razi points out that inanimate objects and sleeping people are also not overpowered, yet they are not willing. See how this undermines the definition.
3
Reflect on the method
Notice that Razi uses counterexamples to show the definition is too broad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قول النجار أن معنى كونه مريدًا أنه غير مقهور ولا مغلوب فهذا باطل لأن الجماد والنائم غير مقهور مع أنه ليس بمريد. وأما قول من قال: كونه مريدا هو نفس ذاته فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأنه لما دل الدليل على استناد هذا العالم إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، فقد علمنا ذاته بعد ما علمنا كونه مريدا ، والمعلوم غير ما هو معلوم. وأما قول من قال: إن إرادته محدثة فهو باطل لأنه لما ثبت أن إحداث المحدثات موقوف على الإرادة فلو كانت الإرادة محدثة لافتقر إحداثها إلى إرادة أخرى، ولزم التسلسل.
As for the statement of al-Najjar that the meaning of His being willing is that He is not overpowered nor vanquished, this is false because an inanimate object and a sleeping person are not overpowered while they are not willing. And as for the statement of those who said that His being willing is identical to His essence, it is also false because when the proof indicates that this world is dependent on an existent necessary of existence by itself, we have known His essence after we have known His being willing, and the known is other than what is known. And as for the statement of those who said that His will is originated, it is false because when it is established that the origination of originated things is dependent on will, then if the will were originated, its origination would need another will, and an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
In this passage, Razi refutes three specific incorrect views. First, al-Najjar's negative interpretation: God being 'willing' means He is not overpowered. Razi objects that inanimate objects and sleeping people also are not overpowered, yet they are not willing. This shows the definition is too broad. Second, al-Najjar's second view that God's will is identical to His essence: Razi argues that we first know that God is willing (through revelation or inference) and then we know His essence (through proof of necessary existence). Since the known (the will) is not identical to what else is known (the essence), the two cannot be identical. Third, the view that will is originated: the same argument used earlier—if will were originated, it would need another will to originate it, leading to infinite regress. These refutations systematically eliminate all but the Ash'ari position (eternal meaning).

Key Terms
جماد (inanimate object)
نائم (sleeping person)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining 'willing' as 'not overpowered' is safe because it seems to emphasize God's power.
Correction: Razi shows that the definition is too broad: it includes non-willing entities like inanimate objects and sleeping people, so it fails to specify will.

Checkpoint
What counterexample does Razi use to refute al-Najjar's negative definition of will?
A) Angels are not overpowered but are willing.
B) Inanimate objects and sleeping people are not overpowered but are not willing.
C) God is not overpowered but cannot be compared.
D) Will is an essential attribute, not a negative one.

Key Takeaway
A negative definition of will fails because it does not distinguish will from mere non-coercion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often define your obedience to God in negative terms—'I don't sin'—rather than positive terms—'I actively seek His pleasure'? How does Razi's argument challenge this approach? What would it look like for you to define your will for God positively today?
?
When you think of God's will, do you tend to focus on what He is not (e.g., not weak, not coerced) rather than what He is? How might this affect your trust and worship? Take a moment to affirm a positive attribute of God's will and act on it.

Refuting the Identity of Will and Essence
Razi refutes the view that will is identical to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why will cannot be identical to God's essence based on the order of knowledge.

Core Idea
We know God's will before His essence, so they cannot be identical.

Some theologians argued that God's will is not a separate attribute but identical to His essence. Razi counters this by noting that our knowledge of God's will precedes our knowledge of His essence, which proves they are distinct. Let's see the logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of Razi's refutation of the identity view.
2
Understand the argument
Razi says: we know God is willing (e.g., through revelation) before we know His essence through philosophical proof. If A is known before B, they are not identical.
3
Evaluate the logic
Consider whether the order of knowledge is a reliable indicator of real distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قول النجار أن معنى كونه مريدًا أنه غير مقهور ولا مغلوب فهذا باطل لأن الجماد والنائم غير مقهور مع أنه ليس بمريد. وأما قول من قال: كونه مريدا هو نفس ذاته فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأنه لما دل الدليل على استناد هذا العالم إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، فقد علمنا ذاته بعد ما علمنا كونه مريدا ، والمعلوم غير ما هو معلوم. وأما قول من قال: إن إرادته محدثة فهو باطل لأنه لما ثبت أن إحداث المحدثات موقوف على الإرادة فلو كانت الإرادة محدثة لافتقر إحداثها إلى إرادة أخرى، ولزم التسلسل.
As for the statement of al-Najjar that the meaning of His being willing is that He is not overpowered nor vanquished, this is false because an inanimate object and a sleeping person are not overpowered while they are not willing. And as for the statement of those who said that His being willing is identical to His essence, it is also false because when the proof indicates that this world is dependent on an existent necessary of existence by itself, we have known His essence after we have known His being willing, and the known is other than what is known. And as for the statement of those who said that His will is originated, it is false because when it is established that the origination of originated things is dependent on will, then if the will were originated, its origination would need another will, and an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
In this passage, Razi refutes three specific incorrect views. First, al-Najjar's negative interpretation: God being 'willing' means He is not overpowered. Razi objects that inanimate objects and sleeping people also are not overpowered, yet they are not willing. This shows the definition is too broad. Second, al-Najjar's second view that God's will is identical to His essence: Razi argues that we first know that God is willing (through revelation or inference) and then we know His essence (through proof of necessary existence). Since the known (the will) is not identical to what else is known (the essence), the two cannot be identical. Third, the view that will is originated: the same argument used earlier—if will were originated, it would need another will to originate it, leading to infinite regress. These refutations systematically eliminate all but the Ash'ari position (eternal meaning).

Key Terms
واجب الوجود (necessary existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Will is contingent, so it cannot be essential.
B) We know God's will before we know His essence, so they are distinct.
C) The Quran separates will and essence.
D) Infinite regress would follow.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between will and essence is supported by the order in which we come to know them.

Refuting the Originated Will
Razi refutes the view that will is originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God's will cannot be originated on pain of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Originating the will requires another will, leading to an endless chain.

Some claimed that God's will is created (originated) in time. Razi refutes this by noting that the origination of anything depends on a prior will. If the will itself is originated, it would need another will, and so on, resulting in an infinite regress. This is a classic argument for an eternal attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of Razi's refutation of originated will.
2
Follow the regress argument
Razi uses the principle: every originated thing requires a will. If will is originated, it requires another will, leading to infinite regress.
3
Understand the conclusion
Since infinite regress is impossible, the will must be eternal, not originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما قول النجار أن معنى كونه مريدًا أنه غير مقهور ولا مغلوب فهذا باطل لأن الجماد والنائم غير مقهور مع أنه ليس بمريد. وأما قول من قال: كونه مريدا هو نفس ذاته فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأنه لما دل الدليل على استناد هذا العالم إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، فقد علمنا ذاته بعد ما علمنا كونه مريدا ، والمعلوم غير ما هو معلوم. وأما قول من قال: إن إرادته محدثة فهو باطل لأنه لما ثبت أن إحداث المحدثات موقوف على الإرادة فلو كانت الإرادة محدثة لافتقر إحداثها إلى إرادة أخرى، ولزم التسلسل.
As for the statement of al-Najjar that the meaning of His being willing is that He is not overpowered nor vanquished, this is false because an inanimate object and a sleeping person are not overpowered while they are not willing. And as for the statement of those who said that His being willing is identical to His essence, it is also false because when the proof indicates that this world is dependent on an existent necessary of existence by itself, we have known His essence after we have known His being willing, and the known is other than what is known. And as for the statement of those who said that His will is originated, it is false because when it is established that the origination of originated things is dependent on will, then if the will were originated, its origination would need another will, and an infinite regress would follow.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
In this passage, Razi refutes three specific incorrect views. First, al-Najjar's negative interpretation: God being 'willing' means He is not overpowered. Razi objects that inanimate objects and sleeping people also are not overpowered, yet they are not willing. This shows the definition is too broad. Second, al-Najjar's second view that God's will is identical to His essence: Razi argues that we first know that God is willing (through revelation or inference) and then we know His essence (through proof of necessary existence). Third, the view that will is originated: the same argument used earlier—if will were originated, it would need another will to originate it, leading to infinite regress. These refutations systematically eliminate all but the Ash'ari position (eternal meaning).

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's will could be a created attribute that He brings into existence.
Correction: Razi shows that if will were created, it would need a prior will, leading to an infinite chain, which is impossible.

Checkpoint

B) It would require an infinite regress of wills.
C) It would contradict God's power.

Key Takeaway
God's will must be eternal because an originated will would lead to an infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Or does it mean your choice to sin happened within His will? How does the eternity of God's will affect your sense of responsibility and repentance? Write one concrete change you will make today to align your will with His.

Refutation of Karramiyya and Mu'tazila on Divine Will
Razi refutes Karramiyya and Mu'tazila views on divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two refutations: why God's essence cannot be a locus for will, and why will cannot exist without a substrate.

Core Idea
God's will cannot reside in His essence nor exist without a locus—both are impossible.

Razi has argued that God's will is not originated like human will. Now he refutes two alternative proposals: the Karramiyya's claim that will occurs within God's essence, and the Mu'tazila's claim that will exists without a substrate. Both are shown to be rationally untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of Razi's argument against both sects.
Notice that Razi gives two separate proofs for the Mu'tazila refutation.
2
Understand the refutation of Karramiyya
Identify the premise that God's essence cannot be a locus for originated things, and how this invalidates the Karramiyya view.
This is a foundational Ash'ari principle.
3
Analyze the first proof against Mu'tazila
The existence of an accident without a substrate is inconceivable; if allowed, it would permit absurdities like blackness without a substrate.
This is an a priori argument.
4
Analyze the second proof against Mu'tazila
Without particularization, any will without a locus would be equally related to all essences, forcing all living beings to will what God wills.
This leads to a false consequence.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why both refutations preserve divine transcendence and the necessity of a substrate for accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول الكرامية : إنه تحدث الإرادة في ذاته فهو أيضًا باطل لما ثبت أن ذاته يمتنع أن تكون محلا للحوادث. وأما قول المعتزلة إنه تحدث الإرادة لا في محل فهذا أيضًا باطل، ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: أن وجود عرض لا في محل بعيد عن العقول، ولو جاز ذلك فلم يجوز وجود سواد لا في محل وبياض لا في محل ؟ وكذا القول في سائر الأعراض. الثاني: أن ذوات الحيوانات تصح عليها صفة المريدية، فلو جوزت إرادة لا في محل لكانت نسبة تلك الإرادة إلى ذات الله تعالى كنسبتها إلى سائر الذوات، فوجب أن توجد صفة المريدية لكل من يصح أن يكون مريداً، لعدم الاختصاص، فيلزم أن كل ما يريده الله تعالى يريده كل الأحياء، وذلك باطل.
And as for the statement of the Karramiyya that will occurs in His essence, it is also false because it is established that His essence is impossible to be a locus for originated things. And as for the statement of the Mu'tazila that will occurs not in a locus, this is also false, and two aspects indicate it: First: that the existence of an accident not in a locus is remote from intelligences, and if that were permissible, why would it not be permissible for blackness to exist not in a locus and whiteness not in a locus? And likewise the statement regarding all other accidents. Second: that the essences of animals are suitable for the attribute of being willing. So if a will not in a locus were allowed, the relation of that will to the essence of God Most High would be like its relation to other essences, so the attribute of being willing would have to exist for everyone who can be a willer, due to the lack of particularization; then it would follow that everything that God Most High wills is willed by all living beings, and that is false.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
Razi here refutes the two remaining positive views after eliminating the general originated view. First, the Karramiyya hold that the will is originated and subsists in God's essence. Razi rejects this because God's essence cannot be a locus for originated things (a fundamental Ash'ari principle to avoid divine change and temporality). Second, the Mu'tazila hold that the will is originated but exists not in any locus. Razi gives two proofs against this. The first proof is an a priori argument: an accident (like will) existing without a substrate is inconceivable; if allowed, one could similarly posit blackness or whiteness without a substrate, which is absurd. The second proof uses parity of reasoning: if a will can exist without a locus, its relation to God and to other beings (like animals) would be the same. Since there is no special relation, the will would have to be present in all beings capable of will. This would mean everything God wills would also be willed by all living beings, which is false. Therefore, the Mu'tazila view is impossible.

Key Terms
محل (locus/substrate)
عرض (accident)
سواد (blackness)
بياض (whiteness)
اختصاص (particularization)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think accidents (like will) can exist without being attached to a substance or substrate.
Correction: Razi argues that such a notion is inconceivable to reason; if accepted, it would allow arbitrary absurdities like blackness existing without a colored object.

Checkpoint
What is the second proof Razi gives against the Mu'tazila view that will exists not in a locus?
A) It leads to regress.
B) It would make God changeable.
C) If so, the will would have to be present in all beings capable of will, leading to absurdity.
D) It contradicts the eternity of God.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that both the Karramiyya and Mu'tazila views on divine will are rationally impossible, leaving the Ash'ari position (eternal will in God's essence) as the only coherent one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God's will, do you ever secretly think of it as changing or reactive, like the Karramiyya assumed? How does this passage challenge you to purify your concept of God's will from any temporality?
?
The Mu'tazila thought will could float without a substrate—do you ever treat God's will as if it were a force detached from His essence, something He just 'has' rather than what He is? What would it mean for your worship if God's will were not uniquely His?
?
Razi argues that if will without a locus were possible, all living beings would will the same thing as God. Does your will actually align with God's revealed will (in the Qur'an and Sunnah) in your daily choices, or do you comfort yourself that your will is independent? What one specific thing today can you consciously will to match God's will?

Classification and Refutation of Views on Divine Will
Razi classifies sects' views on divine will and refutes four of them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different early Islamic theological views on the nature of Allah's will and Razi's refutations of four of them.

Core Idea
Razi refutes four erroneous views on divine will, affirming that will is a distinct eternal attribute.

In this passage, Razi presents a concise but comprehensive survey of early debates about Allah's will (إرادة). He classifies the major sectarian positions and then systematically refutes those he considers invalid. This lesson will guide you through his classification and his arguments against al-Najjar's view, the essence-identity view, the originated will view, and the Karramiyya view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the classification tree
The passage begins by classifying views on divine will. Find the two main branches: 'willing by essence' vs 'willing by a meaning'. Then see how the second branch splits into pre-eternal vs originated, and originated into three positions.
Pay attention to the different sects mentioned: al-Najjar, Ash'aris, Karramiyya, Mu'tazilites.
3
Examine each refutation
Razi refutes four views in order: al-Najjar's, essence-identity, originated will, and Karramiyya. For each, identify the specific argument he uses and why he considers it false.
The refutations involve logic, inference from creation, and the principle against infinite regress.
4
Reflect on Razi's method
Ask yourself: why does Razi only refute these four? How do his refutations support the Ash'ari position that will is a pre-eternal attribute?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة عشرة
أما الأول : فهو القول الثاني للنجار، وذلك أنه قال: إنه تعالى مريد لذاته وأما الذين قالوا : المريدية معللة بمعنى فذلك المعنى إما أن يكون قديما قائما بذات الله تعالى، وهذا هو قول أصحابنا، وإما أن يكون محدثًا، وعلى هذا التقدير فهذه الإرادة المحدثة إما أن تكون قائمة بذات الله تعالى - وهو قول الكرامية - أو موجودة لا في محل - وهو قول أبي على وأبي هاشم والقاضي عبد الجبار بن أحمد - وإما أن تكون قائمة بذات غير الله تعالى وما رأيت أحدًا اختار هذا القسم، فهذا تفصيل مذاهب الناس في هذه المسألة، ولما كثر خوض المتقدمين في هذه المباحث لا جرم اكتفينا فيها بالقليل من القول. فأما قول النجار أن معنى كونه مريدًا أنه غير مقهور ولا مغلوب فهذا باطل لأن الجماد والنائم غير مقهور مع أنه ليس بمريد. وأما قول من قال: كونه مريدا هو نفس ذاته فهو أيضًا باطل ؛ لأنه لما دل الدليل على استناد هذا العالم إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، فقد علمنا ذاته بعد ما علمنا كونه مريدا ، والمعلوم غير ما هو معلوم. وأما قول من قال: إن إرادته محدثة فهو باطل لأنه لما ثبت أن إحداث المحدثات موقوف على الإرادة فلو كانت الإرادة محدثة لافتقر إحداثها إلى إرادة أخرى، ولزم التسلسل. وأما قول الكرامية : إنه تحدث الإرادة في ذاته فهو أيضًا باطل لما ثبت أن ذاته يمتنع أن تكون محلا للحوادث.
The thirteenth problem. As for the first: it is the second saying of al-Najjar, and that is that he said: He, exalted, is willing by His essence. And as for those who said: willing is caused by a meaning, that meaning is either pre-eternal, subsisting in the essence of Allah – and this is the saying of our companions – or it is originated. On this assumption, this originated will either subsist in the essence of Allah – which is the saying of the Karramiyya – or exist not in a locus – which is the saying of Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, and al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad – or it subsists in the essence of other than Allah, and I have not seen anyone choose this division. So this is a detailed account of the schools of the people on this problem. And since the predecessors delved extensively into these investigations, necessarily we suffice with little speech. As for the saying of al-Najjar that the meaning of His being willing is that He is not overpowered nor subdued, this is false because an inanimate object and a sleeping person are not overpowered while they are not willing. And as for the saying of the one who said: His being willing is identical to His essence, that is also false because when the evidence indicated that this world is dependent on an existent necessary of existence in itself, we have known His essence after we knew His being willing, and the known is other than that by which it is known. And as for the saying of the one who said: His will is originated, it is false because when it is established that the origination of originated things is dependent on will, if will were originated, its origination would need another will, and infinite regress would follow. And as for the saying of the Karramiyya that will occurs in His essence, that is also false because it is established that His essence is impossible to be a locus for accidents.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of early views on the nature of Allah's will (إرادة). After introducing the problem as the thirteenth, Razi begins by classifying the various schools. He first mentions al-Najjar's view that Allah is willing by His essence (i.e., will is identical to essence without a distinct attribute). Then he explains the view that willing is caused by a meaning (معنى): that meaning is either pre-eternal (the Ash'ari position) or originated. If originated, it can subsist in Allah's essence (Karramiyya), exist not in a locus (the position of the Mu'tazilites Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, and 'Abd al-Jabbar), or subsist in another essence (no one chose this). He then states that since the predecessors discussed this extensively, he will be brief. Next, he refutes al-Najjar's view: if will is simply not being overpowered, then inanimate objects and sleeping persons would be willers, which is false. He refutes the essence-identity view: since we know Allah's essence after we know He is willing (through inference from creation), the known essence is other than the attribute of will, so they are distinct. He refutes the view that will is originated: if will were originated, it would need another will to originate it, leading to infinite regress (تسلسل). Finally, he refutes the Karramiyya: the essence of Allah cannot be a locus for accidents (حوادث), so will cannot occur in it as an originated accident.

Key Terms
إرادة
مريد
محدث
تسلسل
كرامية
محل للحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that al-Najjar's view is plausible because it avoids anthropomorphism, but Razi shows it fails to distinguish will from mere lack of coercion.
Correction: Razi argues that if 'willing' means simply not being overpowered, then inanimate objects and sleeping persons would be considered willing, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is the view that Allah's will is identical to His essence (كونه مريدا هو نفس ذاته) false?
A) Because it leads to infinite regress.
B) Because we know Allah's essence after we know His will, so they are distinct.
C) Because it implies will is an accident in the essence.
D) Because it denies that Allah is willing.

Key Takeaway
Razi classifies and refutes four early views on divine will, affirming the Ash'ari position that will is a distinct, pre-eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that if will were identical to essence, we could not know will before essence. In your own understanding of Allah, do you ever treat His attributes as mere labels rather than real distinctions? How can you deepen your awe of each attribute separately?
?
The refutation of originated will shows that infinite regress is impossible. When you think about the chain of causes in your life, do you truly rely on Allah's eternal will as the ultimate cause? What would change if you lived with that conviction today?
?
Razi rejects the Karramiyya view because Allah's essence cannot be a locus for accidents. Does your heart ever imagine Allah as changing or subject to time? What verse or proof will you hold onto when that thought arises?

8 min
Razi's Refutation of the Mu'tazilite View on Divine Will
Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite view that will is an accident without location, with two proofs and an objection-answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two rational proofs against the Mu'tazilite claim that will exists without a locus, and his response to the objection that Allah's essence also lacks a locus.

Core Idea
An accident cannot exist without a locus; without specification, will would be shared by all.

Razi engages with a key theological dispute about the nature of God's will. The Mu'tazila argued that will is an originated accident existing without a location, avoiding anthropomorphism. Razi counters with two rational proofs and an objection-answer, showing this view leads to logical absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
The Mu'tazila claim that Allah's will is an accident (عرض) that occurs without a locus (لا في محل). Grasp this claim before engaging with the refutation.
This is a crucial point of difference between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili schools.
2
First proof: impossibility of accident without locus
Read Razi's first proof: the existence of an accident not in a locus is rationally absurd. If allowed, why not blackness or whiteness without a body? This shows the view is arbitrary.
This proof appeals to the universal rational principle that accidents inhere in substances.
3
Second proof: lack of specification leads to universal will
Examine the second proof: if will could exist without a locus, it would have no special relation to Allah, so every being capable of willing would have the same will. This is false as Allah's will is unique.
This proof uses the principle that without specification (اختصاص), a property applies universally.
4
Objection and Razi's response
The Mu'tazila object: Allah's essence is also without locus, so the will attaches more properly to Him. Razi replies that 'not in a locus' is a negative attribute, and a negation cannot be a cause for a positive specification. Thus the objection fails.
This introduces the important kalam principle: negation (سلب) cannot cause affirmation (ثبوت).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول المعتزلة إنه تحدث الإرادة لا في محل فهذا أيضًا باطل، ويدل عليه وجهان
الأول: أن وجود عرض لا في محل بعيد عن العقول، ولو جاز ذلك فلم لا جوز وجود سواد لا في محل وبياض لا في محل ؟ وكذا القول في سائر الأعراض.
الثاني: أن ذوات الحيوانات تصح عليها صفة المريدية، فلو جوزت إرادة لا في محل لكانت نسبة تلك الإرادة إلى ذات الله تعالى كنسبتها إلى سائر الذوات، فوجب أن توجد صفة المريدية لكل من يصح أن يكون مريداً، لعدم الاختصاص، فيلزم أن كل ما يريده الله تعالى يريده كل الأحياء، وذلك باطل.
فإن قيل : ذات الله تعالى لا في محل، وهذه الإرادة لا في محل فكان اختصاص هذه الإرادة بذات الله تعالى أولى من اختصاصها بسائر الأحياء. قلنا : كونه لا في محل مفهوم سلبي، فلا يجوز أن يكون ذلك علة لاختصاص صفة المريدية بذات الله تعالى؛ لأن السلب لا يكون علة للثبوت، وإذا ظهر أن هذا القيد لا يصلح أن يكون علة لهذا الاختصاص، عاد المحذور المذكور، وبالله التوفيق.
And as for the saying of the Mu'tazila that will occurs not in a locus, that is also false. Two proofs indicate it: The first: that the existence of an accident not in a locus is far from intellects. If that were permissible, why would you not permit the existence of blackness not in a locus and whiteness not in a locus? And likewise the saying for the rest of accidents. The second: that the essences of animals are valid for the attribute of willing. So if you permitted a will not in a locus, the relation of that will to the essence of Allah would be like its relation to other essences. Thus the attribute of willing would necessarily exist for everyone who can be a willer, due to lack of specification. Hence it follows that everything Allah wills, every living being wills it, and that is false. If it is said: The essence of Allah is not in a locus, and this will is not in a locus, so the specification of this will to the essence of Allah is more appropriate than its specification to other living beings. We say: His being not in a locus is a negative concept, so it cannot be a cause for the specification of the attribute of willing to the essence of Allah, because negation cannot be a cause for affirmation. And when it becomes clear that this condition is not fit to be a cause for this specification, the mentioned objection returns. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that Allah's will is an originated accident existing without a locus (لا في محل). Razi presents two proofs. First, it is rationally absurd for an accident (عرض) to exist without a locus; if such a thing were allowed, then by the same token blackness and whiteness could exist without a body, which is impossible. Second, if a will without location were possible, then since both Allah and animals can be described as willing, a will without location would relate equally to all essences, and thus everything Allah wills would also be willed by every living being, which is false. Then an objection is raised: Allah's essence itself is not in a locus, so the will not in a locus is more specifically related to Allah than to animals. Razi answers: 'Not in a locus' is a negative attribute, and a negation cannot be the cause (علة) of a positive specification. Therefore, the lack of specification remains, and the second proof stands. The passage ends with 'wa billahi al-tawfiq' (and with Allah is success).

Key Terms
إرادة
عرض
محل
مريدية
سلب
علة
اختصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazilite view elegantly avoids placing an accident in Allah's essence by saying will exists without a locus.
Correction: Razi shows this leads to absurd consequences: either accidents can exist without a body (which is rationally unacceptable), or the will would be shared by all beings capable of willing, which is false.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second proof against the Mu'tazilite view, if will exists without a locus, what would necessarily follow?
A) Allah would not be able to will anything.
B) All living beings would will the same things Allah wills.
C) Will would be an eternal attribute.
D) Only Allah would have will.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite position is rationally untenable because accidents require a locus, and a will without specification would be shared by all beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that a negative attribute cannot be a cause for a positive specification. When you think about Allah, do you sometimes assign positive significance to what is merely the absence of limitation in your mind? How might that distort your understanding of His attributes?
?
The second proof implies that if Allah's will were not specifically His, it would be shared by all. Do you treat Allah's will as something distant and impersonal, or do you truly believe that His will concerning your life is unique and specific to you? What would change if you lived with that conviction today?
?
Razi ends with 'wa billahi al-tawfiq'—and with Allah is success. After studying this argument, are you relying on your own intellectual grasp to secure truth, or do you humbly seek Allah's guidance to confirm what is correct? What step can you take today to depend more on Him in your search for certainty?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
5 min
Refuting the Allocation of Will to God's Essence via Negation
Imam Razi refutes the claim that will is specific to God's essence due to being not in a locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a negative attribute (like 'not being in a locus') cannot serve as a cause for a positive divine attribute, and how Imam Razi uses this principle to refute a specific objection.

Core Idea
A negative concept cannot cause a positive attribute.

Imam Razi engages with an opponent who claims that God's will is specifically attached to His essence because God's essence is not in a locus. Razi's response is concise and powerful: a negative concept cannot be a cause for a positive attribute. This lesson walks through that reasoning and its implications for thinking about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note that this is a short objection-and-answer unit.
Observe the brevity and directness of Razi's style.
2
Identify opponent's claim and Razi's reply
The opponent says will belongs to God's essence because He is not in a locus. Razi says that 'not in a locus' is a negative concept.
3
Understand why negation cannot cause affirmation
Razi gives the logical principle: negation cannot be a cause for existence. Reflect on why this makes sense.
A cause must be something positive; a mere absence cannot bring about an attribute.
4
Consider the consequence
If the condition fails, the original difficulty (unallocated will to other beings) remains. Recognize the structure of the argument.
The argument returns to its starting point, exposing the opponent's failure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختصاص هذه الإرادة بذات الله تعالى أولى من اختصاصها بسائر الأحياء. قلنا : كونه لا في محل مفهوم سلبي، فلا يجوز أن يكون ذلك علة لاختصاص صفة المريدية بذات الله تعالى؛ لأن السلب لا يكون علة للثبوت، وإذا ظهر أن هذا القيد لا يصلح أن يكون علة لهذا الاختصاص، عاد المحذور المذكور، وبالله التوفيق.
The allocation of this will to God's essence is more appropriate than its allocation to other living beings. We said: His being not in a locus is a negative concept, so it is not permissible for that to be the cause for the allocation of the attribute of willing (al-muridiyya) to God's essence; because negation cannot be a cause for affirmation. And when it becomes apparent that this condition is not fit to be a cause for this allocation, the mentioned difficulty returns. And with God is the success.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The opponent claims that the attribute of will is specifically attached to God's essence rather than to other living beings because God's essence is not in a locus (la fi mahall). The reply (qulna) points out that 'being not in a locus' is a negative concept (mafhum sulbi). A negative concept cannot serve as a cause (illa) for a positive attribute like being a willer (al-muridiyya). Since negation cannot cause existence (al-sulb la yakunu illatan li al-thubut), the proposed justification fails, and the original difficulty (al-mahdhar al-madhkur) returns. The author ends with 'bi Allah al-tawfiq'.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
مريدية (willing attribute)
سلب (negation)
علة (cause)
ثبوت (affirmation/existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attribute of will is allocated to His essence simply because He is not in a locus.
Correction: This passage shows that a negative attribute cannot be a cause for a positive attribute, so that reasoning is invalid.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi reject the argument that will is allocated to God's essence because He is not in a locus?
A) Because God is not in a locus is a positive attribute
B) Because negation cannot be a cause for affirmation
C) Because all living beings are not in a locus
D) Because will is not an attribute of God

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi establishes a key logical principle: negative concepts cannot serve as causes for positive attributes, thereby refuting a flawed argument about divine will.
